

Lesson l 
The Legal Structure 
ATPA OPS 07 
The Legal Structure 

Introduction 
This section describes the regulations under which air transport aircraft are operated. We shall examine the legal structure of the Regulatory Authorities, the rules under which an air transport company operates and the requirements placed on the crew. Later on the subject broadens to include normal and abnormal operating procedures and hazards such as turbulence, icing and wind shear. 



The Legal Structure 
The International Civil Aviation Organisation (ICAO) sets out standards, practices and recommendations for air transport operation in the 1944 Chicago Convention and its Annexes. The regulations dealing with scheduled and non-scheduled international commercial air transport are set out in ICAO Annex 6. 
ICAO Annex 6 Part 1 is applicable to operators authorised to conduct international commercial air transport operations 
Individual States or groups of States align their Within Europe, over the last 20 years or so, the rules for operating commercial aeroplanes have been through change: 
In the 1980s, the Joint Aviation Authority (JAA), some 43 European States, established the Joint Aviation Requirement for the operation of commercial air transport (aeroplanes), called JAR-OPS 1, along with supporting documentation. These contained the rules for operating commercial aeroplanes. Under JAA, these rules were simply an agreement between member states. 
The Legal Structure 
The Legal Structure continued 
By 2009 the European Aviation Safety Agency (EASA) had been established (32 European Union Countries -or the EU plus Norway, Iceland, Switzerland and Liechtenstein as signatories). EASA took over the responsibilities for regulation of commercial aviation from the JAA. In terms of documentation, for the operation of commercial aeroplanes, EASA produced EU OPS 1, known officially as Council Regulation (EEC) No 3922/91, on the harmonisation of technical requirements and administrative procedures in the field of civil aviation. 
The regulations in EU OPS 1 were derived from and very similar to JAR-OPS 1, only now the rules were legally binding under EU law. This was only a temporary arrangement as EASA consolidated the rules for civil aviation under: COMMISSION REGULATION (EU) No 965/2012 of 5 October 2012 which lays down technical requirements and administrative procedures related to air operations pursuant to Regulation (EC) No 216/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council. 


The legal authority lies with the EU or the individual National Authority as applicable 
All Operators are required to comply with the Commission Regulation and it's Annexes (see below). 
Note syllabus questions may refer to Annex number or Part (ORO) section and number i.e ORO.GEN.200. 
The full Regulation and annexes can be accessed on the EASA website under Easy Access Rules for Air Operations.

Supporting documentation to each of these Annexes is given in the Acceptable Means of Compliance (AMC) and Guidance Material (GM). 
Note that Parts NCC, NCO and SPO are outside the Operational Procedures syllabus. 


The referencing 'trail' within EC Regulations can get quite involved. For instance, so far we have considered Commission Regulation (EU) No 965/2012 of 5 October 2012, and as already stated, this lays down technical requirements and administrative procedures related to air operations pursuant to Regulation (EC) No 216/2008. Looking at EC No 216/2008, this ensures 'common rules in the field of civil aviation'. The principal objective of this Regulation is to establish and maintain a high uniform level of civil aviation safety in Europe. 
Additional objectives are 
¦ Air Ops Regulations continued 
to ensure a high uniform level of environmental protection 
¦ 
to facilitate the free movement of goods, persons and services 

¦ 
to promote cost-efficiency in the regulatory and certification 

¦ 
to assist Member States in fulfilling their obligations under the Chicago Convention 

¦ 
to promote civil aviation safety standards and rules throughout the world by establishing appropriate cooperation with third countries and international organisations 

¦ 
to provide a level playing field for all actors in the internal aviation market. 


The Legal Structure 
Air Ops Regulations continued 
From this, we can see that the principles for safe commercial air transport operations within Europe are laid down in Regulation (EC) No 216/2008, the detail of exactly how we operate is detailed in Commission Regulation (EU) No 965/2012 of 5 October 2012. 
In the discussion that follows, we will simply reference this as the 'Regulations'. 
So, as a Commercial Air Transport (CAT) organisation, an operator has to produces a set of operating procedures and standards. These are based on EC Regulations, which in turn are based on the Chicago Convention. Variations and additions from the standard may be permitted in certain areas by negotiation with and the approval of EASA and/or the appropriate National Authority. 
In the end, then, it becomes a matter of conformity in the main and negotiation of the detail. The operator sets out how it intends to operate aircraft basing its procedures on the regulations. Where the regulations allow for variations the operator will submit procedures that it considers commercially viable and safe. Where the Authority agrees approval will be given, where it does not it will impose more stringent standards. 


Subject Matter and Scope of EU Regulations No 965/2012 
¦ 
This Regulation lays down detailed rules for commercial air transport (CAT) operations with aeroplanes and helicopters. 

¦ 
This Regulation also lays down detailed rules on the conditions for issuing, maintaining, amending, limiting, suspending or revoking the certificates of operators of aircraft engaged in commercial air transport operations, the privileges and responsibilities of the holders of certificates as well as conditions under which operations shall be 

prohibited, limited or subject to certain conditions in the interest of safety. 

¦ 
This Regulation shall not apply to air operations while carrying out military, customs, police, search and rescue, firefighting, coastguard or similar activities or services. 


The Legal Structure 
Air Operator's Certificates (AOC} 
An AOC is a certificate issued by the State in which the operator is registered which authorises it to carry out specific CAT operations. 
The Competent Authority, having been designated by the EASA member state, shall issue the air operator certificate (AOC) when satisfied that the operator has demonstrated compliance with the elements required in the regulations. The operator must have their principal place of business in that member state. 
A competent authority is an authority which complies with regulations. Annex II Part ARO details the authority requirements for air operations. Here the responsibilities of the (competent) authority are detailed under management requirements, oversight and enforcement, certification of CAT operators and ramp inspection and reporting. 
An AOC specifies the type of operation that the operator is licensed to perform and sets out the conditions under which the authorised operations will be performed. It will include 
¦ 
the operator's identification and location 

¦ 
the date of issue of the AOC and period of validity 

¦ 
the description of the types of operation authorised 

¦ 
the types of aircraft authorised and registration markings 




¦ 
the authorised area of operation or route structure 

¦ 
special limitations/authorisations/approvals relating to 

¦ 
low visibility operations including minima 

¦ 
NAT HLA 

¦ 
ETOPS 

¦ 
navigation specifications for Performance Based Navigation (PBN) 

¦ 
RVSM 

¦ 
transportation of dangerous goods and 

¦ 
authorisation to provide cabin crew initial safety training. 




The Legal Structure 

The Overall Responsibility of the Operator 
ICAO Annex 6 states that the operator is responsible to the State for the control of the operation and that the responsibility may be delegated to a designated representative. EU regulations parallel ICAO, stating clearly that it is the operator who is responsible for the control of operations under the terms of their AOC. 
It places a general responsibility on the operator to ensure that its pilots and flight crew are familiar with the laws, regulations and procedures that relate to the performance of their duties with regard to the areas in which they operate, the aerodromes and the air navigation facilities to be used. The following bullet points are copied from the regulations and it illustrates how the regulations always put responsibility for CAT on the operator. 
In the lessons that follow, we will look at all the aspects of a Commercial Air Transport operation. We will look at the AOC and the operator responsibilities, then we will look at Crew requirements, aeroplane equipment and then we will move on to the process of actually flying a CAT sector, the rules on aerodromes and routing. 


After that we look at a number specialist areas such as Extended Twin Operations, Long Range sectors and some of the navigation and planning issues, and we will have a short revision on sector fuel requirements. 
Lastly we look at some emergency scenarios and aviation hazard situations such as icing and windshear. 
The very last thing we will do in this introduction is simply give you a list of definitions. This list is extracted from EU Regulation 965/2012. Some of these you will be familiar with, some will be new. As we go through the lessons, many of these will be discussed, but having a straight forward list available for reference may be useful!

Lesson 2 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
ATPA OP8 02 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Introduction 
The flight operations department is made up of both the flight and cabin crew on the aeroplane and the flight operations personnel on the ground. This chapter examines the various named roles and the training and recency requirements, where relevant to the syllabus. 

Ground Personnel 
Those that work on the ground are usually grouped together under the collective name of 'operations'. The principle units are 
¦ 
flight operations management -pilot management and ground staff management 

¦ 
flight operations or flight dispatch officers 

¦ operations controllers 

¦ 
crew rostering and scheduling controllers 

¦ 
aeroplane performance engineers and performance officers 

¦ 
route planning and navigation services staff and 


¦ operations manual preparation and editorial staff. 

All personnel assigned to, or directly involved in, ground and flight operations must be properly instructed, have demonstrated their abilities in their particular duties and are aware of their responsibilities and the relationship of their duties to the operation as a whole. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

The Flight Dispatch Officer 
The 'flight dispatch' officer is responsible for providing supporting paperwork and information to the flight crew and for monitoring 
the progress of the flight from the ground. The Regulations do not imply a requirement for licensed flight dispatchers. The exact duties will, of course, vary from operator to operator these, and the training requirements, would be detailed in the Operations Manual. Typically, the flight dispatch officer duties will include: 
¦ 
assisting the pilot-in-command in flight preparation and providing the relevant planning information including MET, specific route information and NOTAMs, company notices, aircraft information including serviceability items and traffic load 

¦ 
assisting the pilot-in-command in preparing the operational and ATS flight plans. Sign, when applicable, and file the ATS flight plan with the appropriate ATS unit 

¦ 
furnishing the pilot-in-command while in flight, by appropriate means, with information, which may be necessary for the safe conduct of the flight and 



in the event of an emergency, initiate such procedures as may be outlined in the Operations Manual. 
The flight dispatch officer must also be careful not to take any action that would conflict with the procedures established by Air Traffic Control, the meteorological service or the communications service. 
The actions of the flight dispatcher should not conflict with ATC, met or comms services 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 



Crew Members 
The Composition of Flight Crew 
Flight crew are pilots and, where carried, navigators and systems operators. 
The composition of the flight crew and the number of flight crew members at designated crew stations shall be not less than the minimum specified in the aircraft flight manual. 
The flight crew shall include additional flight crew members when required by the type of operation and shall not be less than the minimum specified in the operations manual. 
All flight crew members shall hold a licence and ratings issued or accepted in accordance with the regulations and appropriate to the duties assigned to them. 
The flight crew member may be relieved in flight of his/her duties at the controls by another suitably qualified flight crew member. 
When engaging flight crew members on a part-time basis, the operator shall verify that all applicable qualification and recent experience requirements are complied with. The operator must determine the total number of aircraft types or variants operated and the applicable flight and duty time limitations and rest requirements. 
The operator should ensure that pilots with an Operational Multi­Pilot Limitation (OML) on their medical certificate only operate when the other pilot is qualified on type, is not subject to an OML and is less than 60 years old. 
The Operator has to take all reasonable measures to ensure no person recklessly, intentionally or negligently acts or omits to act so as to endanger an aircraft or person in the aircraft nor cause or permit an aircraft to endanger any person or property. Flight Crew must have undergone a psychological assessment before commencing line flying to identify their suitability and reduce the likelihood of negative interference with safe operation of the aircraft. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Pilot-in-Command 
In multi-crew operations the commander must complete the prescribed command course and hold an ATPL. 
The Command Course should include: FSTD line orientated flight training (LOFT) OPC check as commander 
Command responsibilities training 
Line training of at least 1 O flight sectors, as commander under supervision. 
CRM training 
Line check as commander One pilot amongst the flight crew, qualified as pilot-in-command shall be designated by the operator as pilot-in­command/commander. The operator shall only designate a flight crew member to act as commander if he/she has: 

¦ 
the minimum level of experience specified in the operations manual 

¦ 
adequate knowledge of the route or area to be flown and of the aerodromes, including alternate aerodromes, facilities and procedures to be used 

¦ 
in the case of multi-crew operations, completed an operator's command course if upgrading from co-pilot to commander. 



Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Pilot-in-Command continued 
The commander or the pilot, to whom the conduct of the flight may be delegated, shall have had initial familiarisation training of the route or area to be flown and of the aerodromes, facilities and procedures to be used. This route/area and aerodrome knowledge shall be maintained by operating at least once on the route or area or to the aerodrome within a 12-month period. (Route/area training not applicable for performance class B aeroplanes involved in CAT operations under VFR by day). 
There shall not be more than one inexperienced flight crew member in any flight crew. 
¦ 
A flight crew member is inexperienced, following completion of training until he/she has achieved on the type either 

¦ 
100 flight hours and flown 10 sectors within a consolidation period of 120 consecutive days or 

¦ 
150 flight hours and flown 20 sectors (no time limit). 



Variations to these requirements are possible dependent on previous experience and aeroplane type. 
The holder of a CPL(A) shall only act as commander in commercial air transport on a single-pilot aeroplane if: 
¦ 
when carrying passengers under VFR outside a radius of 50 NM (90 km) from an aerodrome of departure, he/she has a minimum of 500 hours of flight time on aeroplanes or holds a valid instrument rating(not applicable to performance class B under VFR by day) or 

¦ 
when operating on a multi-engine type under IFR, he/she has a minimum of 700 hours of flight time on aeroplanes, including 400 hours as pilot-in-command. These hours shall include 100 hours under IFR and 40 hours in multi-engine operations. The 400 hours as pilot-in-command may be substituted by hours operating as co-pilot within an established multi-pilot crew system prescribed in the operations manual, on the basis of two hours of flight time as co-pilot for one hour of flight time as pilot-in command. 



The holder of a CPL(A) when operating a single-engined aeroplane under I FR, must have a minimum of 700 hours of flight time on aeroplanes, including 400 hours as pilot-in -command, which should include 100 hours IFR. The 400 hours as pilot-in­command may be substituted by hours operating as co-pilot within an established multi-pilot crew system prescribed in the 
operations manual, on the basis of two hours of flight time as co­pilot for one hour of flight time as pilot-in command. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


In-Flight Relief of Flight Crew Members 
The Captain may allow the assigned first officer to operate under his supervision. If there are more than two pilots required, the rules on in-flight relief are as follows: 
The commander may delegate the conduct of the flight to 
¦ 
another qualified commander or 

¦ 
for operations only above flight level (FL) 200, a pilot who complies with the following minimum qualifications 

¦ 
ATPL 

¦ 
conversion training and checking, including type rating 

¦ 
all recurrent training and checking 

¦ 
route/area and aerodrome competence. 





The co-pilot may be relieved by: 
¦ 
another suitably qualified pilot 

¦ 
for operations only above FL 200, a cruise relief co-pilot that complies with the following minimum qualifications: 

¦ 
valid commercial pilot licence (CPL) with an instrument rating 

¦ 
conversion training and checking, including type rating training, except the requirement for take-off and landing training 

¦ 
recurrent training and checking, except the requirement for take-off and landing training. 




In-flight relief co-pilots only need a CPL(A)/IR and do not have to be qualified to land or take-off 

Any type rated pilots can taxy the aircraft, as indeed can any other personnel provided they have been fully trained and briefed on aerodrome layout, signs, markings and ATC instructions. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Flight Engineers 
When a dedicated system panel operator is required by the AFM, the flight crew must include one crew member who holds a flight engineer's (FE) licence or is otherwise a suitably qualified flight crew member and acceptable to the Authority. 
A flight engineer may be relieved in flight by a crew member suitably qualified in accordance with applicable national rules. 

Flight Navigator 
ICAO Annex 6 states "the flight crew must include at least one member who holds a flight navigator licence in all operations where, as determined by the State of the Operator, navigation necessary for the safe conduct of the flight cannot be adequately accomplished by the pilots from the pilot station."There is no mention of this in EASA regulations. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Minimum Flight Crew for IFR or Night Operations 


Multi-Pilot Operation, IFR or Night 
For operations under IFR or at night the minimum flight crew shall be two pilots for all turbo-propeller aeroplanes with a Maximum Operational Passenger Seating Configuration (MOPSC) of more than nine and all turbojet aeroplanes. 

Single Pilot Operation 
Piston engine aircraft and small turbo-props (in other words, aeroplanes other than those described in the paragraph above) can be operated by a single pilot provided that the following requirements are satisfied: 
The Operations Manual must include a pilot's conversion and recurrent training programme which includes any additional requirements for a single pilot operation. In particular the cockpit procedures must include 
¦ 
engine management and emergency handling 

¦ 
the use of normal, abnormal and emergency checklists 

¦ 
ATC communications procedures 

¦ 
departure and approach procedures 

¦ 
autopilot management procedures and 




Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Single Pilot Operation continued 
Recurrent checks shall be performed in the single-pilot role on the relevant type or class of aircraft in an environment representative of the operation. 
There are also recency requirements that must be complied with: 
For aeroplane operations under IFR the pilot shall have: 
¦ 
a minimum of 50 hours flight time under IFR on the relevant type or class of aeroplane, of which 10 hours are as commander and 

¦ 
completed during the preceding 90 days on the relevant type or class of aeroplane: 

¦ 
five IFR flights, including three instrument approaches, in a single-pilot role or 

¦ 
an IFR instrument approach check. 





For aeroplane operations at night the pilot shall have: 
¦ 
a minimum of 15 hours flight time at night which may be included in the 50 hours flight time under IFR and 

¦ 
completed during the preceding 90 days on the relevant type or class of aeroplane: 

¦ 
three take-offs and landings at night in the single pilot role or 

¦ 
a night take-off and landing check. 





Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Conversion Training and Checking 

Whenever a flight crew member first joins an operator, changes aircraft type or role in the cockpit, they complete an operator's conversion course. 
If converting to a new aircraft type or class, a type rating course is completed to EASA standards as part of the conversion, if a flight crew member is recruited with a type rating this can be omitted from the training. 
The minimum standards of qualification and experience required of flight crew members before undertaking conversion training are specified in the Operations Manual. The amount of training required by the operator's conversion course takes account of any previous training as recorded in his training records. All training must be conducted by suitably qualified personnel acceptable to the Authority. 


Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Operators Conversion Course 
An operator's conversion course must include, in order: 
¦ 
Ground training and checking including aeroplane systems, normal, abnormal and emergency procedures 

¦ 
Emergency and safety equipment training and checking which must be completed before aeroplane training commences 

¦ 
Crew resource management training 

¦ 
Aeroplane or flight simulator training and checking 

¦ 
Line Flying Under Supervision (LIFUS) and line check. 



If it is the first conversion course the flight crew member is taking for the operator, they also need general first aid training and if applicable, ditching procedures training using the equipment in water. 
Each flight crew member undergoes an operator proficiency check (OPC), and emergency and safety equipment training and checking before starting LIFUS (line training).

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Operators Conversion Course continued 
The minimum flight sectors/hours should be specified in the operations manual and should be determined by the following: 
¦

¦

The details of an operator conversion course will be in the operations manual. The specifics above just offer an initial look at the guidance offered in the regulations. 
For pilots that have been issued a type rating based on a zero flight-time training (ZFTT) course (this would be the case for most modern airliners), there are additional rules on take-off and landing training. 
After completing line flying under supervision a line check is flown. 
Once an operator's conversion course has been started a flight crew member must not undertake flying duties on another type or class until the course is either completed or terminated. 
Operation on More Than One Type 
When operating more than one type or variant of an aeroplane an operator shall ensure flight crew complete the appropriate differences and/or familiarisation training. The required training must be specified in the Operations Manual approved by the Authority. 
When a flight crew member operates more than one aeroplane class, type or variant but not within a single licence endorsement, the operator should ensure the flight crew member does not operate more than: 
¦ 
three reciprocating engine aeroplane types or variants 

¦ 
three turbo-propeller aeroplane types or variants 


¦ one turbo-propeller type or variant and one reciprocating engine aeroplane type or variant or 
¦ one turbo-propeller type or variant and any aeroplane within a particular class. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Operation on More Than One Type continued 
When a flight crew member operates more than one aeroplane type or variant within one or more licence endorsements, the operators should ensure that: 
the minimum crew specified in the Operations Manual is the same for each type or variant 
the flight crew member does not operate more than two aeroplane types or variants for which separate endorsements are required 
only aeroplanes within one endorsement are flown in any one flight duty period, unless the operator has established procedures to ensure adequate preparation time. 
Pilot Operations from Either Seat 
A pilot who operates in either pilot's seat must also complete the necessary training and checking. Once again, this must be detailed in the Operations Manual, approved by the Authority. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Recurrent Training and Checking 

Each flight crew member must undergo recurrent training and checking relevant to the type or variant of aeroplane on which the flight crew member operates. Details of recurrent training and checking requirements are outlined in the Regulations. From these requirements an operator would create a far more detailed programme of training and checking, approved by the Authority, and detailed in the training section of the Operations Manual. 
Recurrent training in the aeroplane or flight simulator must be conducted by a type rating instructor (TRI) or, in the case of the flight simulator content, a synthetic flight instructor (SFI). 
Recurrent checking would be by appropriate Type (or Class) Rating Examiner (TRE, CRE), or if in a FSTD, by an appropriately qualified Synthetic Flight Examiner (SFE). 
Training and checking on the ground would be by suitably qualified personnel. 
The Regulations identify the following checking and training requirements. 
A line check is valid for 12 calendar months. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

The OPC is valid for 6 calendar months 
¦ 
Each flight crew member shall complete OPCs as part of the normal crew complement to demonstrate competence in carrying out normal, abnormal and emergency procedures. 

¦ 
When the flight crew member will be required to operate under IFR, the OPC shall be conducted without external visual reference. 

¦ 
The validity period of the operator proficiency check shall be six calendar months (this can vary for operations under VFR by day in performance class B aeroplanes). 



Out of interest, when an OPC takes place in a FSTD for a multi­engine aeroplane, the following manoeuvres are required as pilot flying: 
¦ 
rejected take-off 

¦ 
take-off with engine failure between V1 and V2 

¦ 
precision instrument approach to minima with one-engine­inoperative 

¦ 
non-precision approach to minima 

¦ 
missed approach on instruments from minima with one­engine-inoperative 

¦ 
landing with one-engine-inoperative. 



A line check is conducted annually in the aircraft to demonstrate a crew member's competence in normal line operations. The check will include a CRM assessment. 
Line checks shall be conducted by a suitably qualified commander nominated by the operator and will probably comprise 2 sectors, one as pilot flying, one as pilot monitoring. 
A line check is valid for 12 calendar months. 
Line checks are valid for 12 calendar months 


Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Emergency and Safety Equipment Training and Checking 
Emergency and safety equipment training and checking can be combined and should be conducted in an aircraft or a suitable training device. 
Every year the training should include the use and handling of all safety equipment on board the aeroplane. 
Every 3 years, a more practical training is required, including actual operation of all types of exit, operation of slides, actual use of fire extinguishers, the effects of smoke. Again, the precise detail will be in the Operations Manual. 
The training should take place in conjunction with cabin/technical crew undergoing similar training. 
The validity of checks is 12 calendar months. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Crew Resource Management (CRM) 
Flight crew shall have completed an intial CRM course before commencing unsupervised flying which should be conducted by a suitably qualified CRM trainer who may be assisted by experts in specific areas. If the flight crew member has not completed a theoretical training in human factors toaATPL level, he/she must complete a theoretical course provided by the operator. 
Elements of CRM should be integrated into all appropriate phases of recurrent training. In addition, specific CRM training should be established such that all major topics of CRM training are covered over a period not exceeding 3 years. The major topics listed are: 
¦ 
human error, prevention and detection 

¦ 
company safety culture, sops 

¦ 
stress, fatigue and vigilance 

¦ 
information processing, workload management 

¦ 
decision making 

¦ 
communication 

¦ 
leadership and team behaviour 


¦ 
automation (philosophy of use) if relevant 

¦ 
specific type-related differences and 


¦ other areas (identified by the flight safety programme). 

The training should include specific case studies and operators must update the progamme to reflect the results of CRM assessments and reports. 
Ground Training 
The ground training programme should include: 
¦ 
aircraft systems 

¦ operational procedures and requirements, including ground de-icing/anti-icing and pilot incapacitation and 

¦ 
accidenUincident and occurrence review. 



Knowledge of the ground training should be verified suitable testing methods. 
Validity 12 calendar months. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Aircraft/FSTD Training 
Aeroplane or flight simulator training takes place at least every 12 months. There will be a required number of simulator training sessions each year (dependant on operator/aircraft type), these sessions are likely to be combined with or same detail as the instrument rating (annual event) and OPC (every 6 months). These training periods will be structured and planned as detailed in the Operations Manual. Typically, each year fleet management will chose certain aircraft systems that the simulator sessions will concentrate on, so that over a given time frame (2 or 3 years), all major aircraft systems and emergencies are covered during simulator training. Also, each year, various accidents or incidents will be chosen for review during these training sessions.

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Recency 
A pilot shall not operate an aircraft in commercial air transport or carrying passengers: 
1. as PIC or co-pilot unless he/she has carried out, in the preceding 90 days, at least 3 take-offs, approaches and landings in an aircraft of the same type or class or a Full Flight Simulator (FFS) representing that type or class. 
The 3 take-offs and landings shall be performed in either multi-pilot or single-pilot operations, depending on pilot qualification and 

2. as PIC at night unless he/she: 
¦ 
has carried out in the preceding 90 days at least 1 take­off, approach and landing at night as a pilot flying in an aircraft of the same type or class or an FFS representing that type or class or 

¦ 
holds an IR 



3. as cruise relief co-pilot unless he/she: 
¦ 
has complied with the PIC or co-pilot requirements above or 

¦ 
has carried out in the preceding 90 days at least 3 sectors as a cruise relief pilot on the same type or class of aircraft or 

¦ 
has carried out recency and refresher flying skill training in an FFS at intervals not exceeding 90 days. This refresher training may be combined with the operator's recurrent training. 



In the case of commercial air transport, for (1) or (2) above, the 90-day period may be extended up to a maximum of 120 days, as long as the pilot undertakes line flying under the supervision of a type rating instructor or examiner. 
When the pilot does not comply with the (1 ), they shall complete a training flight in the aircraft or an FFS of the aircraft type to be used, which shall include at least the specified recency requirements. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Route and Aerodrome Competence Qualification 
Before acting as operating pilot, a pilot must acquire adequate knowledge of the route to be flown and of the aerodromes, including alternates, facilities and procedures to be used. The rules in OPS simply talk about route and aerodrome competence qualification, and once again, the precise detail of definition and of the training required is left up to the operator. 
Route Competence 
Typically, route competence training should include knowledge of: 
¦ 
terrain and minimum safe altitudes 

¦ 
seasonal meteorological conditions 

¦ 
meteorological, communication and air traffic facilities, services and procedures 

¦ 
Search And rescue (SAR) procedures and 

¦ 
navigational facilities associated with the route along which 


the flight is to take place. 

For the less complex routes, familiarisation by self-briefing with route documentation, or by means of programmed instruction is an adequate way of satisfying these requirements. 
For the more complex routes (North Atlantic NAT HLA operations?) the pilot should receive in-flight familiarisation as a commander, co-pilot or observer under supervision. A simulator may be used. 
Aerodrome Competence 
The Operations Manual should specify a method of categorisation of aerodromes and specify the requirements necessary for each of these categories. The least demanding aerodromes are classed as category A. Category Baand C would be applied to progressively more demanding aerodromes. 
The Operations Manual should specify the parameters that qualify an aerodrome to be considered category A and then provide a list of those aerodromes categorised as B or C. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Category A Aerodromes 
Category A aerodromes should have the following: 
¦ 
an approved instrument approach procedure 

¦ 
at least one runway with no performance limited procedure for take-off and/or landing 

¦ 
published circling minima not higher than 1000 ft above aerodrome level and 

¦ 
night operations. 



Category B Aerodromes 
A Category B aerodrome is an aerodrome that does not meet the category A requirements or which requires extra considerations such as: 
¦ 
non-standard approach aids and/or approach patterns 

¦ 
unusual local weather conditions 

¦ 
unusual characteristics or performance limitations or 

¦ 
any other relevant considerations, including obstructions, 


physical layout, lighting, etc. 

Before operating to a category B aerodrome the commander should be briefed or self-brief by means of programmed instruction on the aerodrome concerned. He must certify that he has carried out the briefing. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Category C Aerodromes 
An aerodrome which is considered to be more difficult to operate from than a category B aerodrome would be classified as category C. 
Before operating to a category C aerodrome the commander should be briefed and visit the aerodrome as an observer and/or undertake instruction in a flight simulator approved by the Authority for that purpose. The operator should certify this instruction. 
In Europe, depending on the operator and aeroplane, London City Aerodrome is Category C because of the 5.5a° glide slope, noise abatement procedures and runway length. 
Salzburg and Innsbruck are Category C for terrain reasons. 


Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Validity Period 
The period of validity of the route and aerodrome competence qualification is 12 calendar months in addition to the remainder of the month of qualification or the month of the latest operation on the route or to the aerodrome. 
Route and aerodrome competence qualification is revalidated by operating on the route or to the aerodrome within the period of validity. 

If revalidated within the final 3 calendar months of validity of previous route and aerodrome competence qualification, the period of validity extends from the date of revalidation until 12 calendar months from the expiry date of that previous route and aerodrome competence qualification. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Cabin Crew 
'Cabin Crew member' means an appropriately qualified crew member, other than a flight crew or technical crew member, who is assigned by an operator to perform duties related to the safety of passengers and flight during operation. 
Number and Composition 
The number and composition of cabin crew shall be determined in taking into account operational factors or circumstances of the particular flight to be operated. At least one cabin crew member shall be assigned for the operation of aircraft with an MOPSC of more than 19 when carrying one or more passenger(s). 
The minimum number of cabin crew shall be the greater of either: 
¦ one crew member for every 50, or fraction of 50, passenger seats installed on the same deck of the aeroplane or 
¦ the number of cabin crew required by relevant certification 
process. 

Cabin crew must be at least 18 years of age, they must pass an initial medical examination, and must remain medically fit to discharge the duties specified in the Operations Manual. 
Cabin crew must have successfully completed initial training in accordance with the Regulations and have completed any applicable conversion and/or differences training and undergo recurrent training as detailed in the Operations Manual. 
Senior cabin crew must have at least one year's experience and have successfully completed a senior cabin crew training course and the associated check. They have responsibility to the commander for the conduct and coordination of procedures detailed in the Operations Manual. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 
Training 
The operator must also set down procedures for the training of cabin crew which must include training on: 
¦ 
the arming and disarming of slides 

¦ 
the operation of cabin lights, including emergency lighting 

¦ 
the prevention and detection of cabin, oven and toilet fires 

¦ 
action to be taken when turbulence is encountered and 

¦ 
actions to be taken in the event of an emergency and/or an evacuation. 




Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Personnel or Crew Members other than Cabin Crew 
Crew members are often positioned in company aircraft in uniform and on duty but not as part of the operating crew. They are listed on the manifest as additional crew members. 
The operator shall ensure that such personnel: 
¦ 
are not confused by the passengers with operating cabin crew members 

¦ 
do not occupy required cabin crew assigned stations 

¦ 
do not impede operating cabin crew members in their duties. 





Training Records 
An operator must maintain records of all training, checking and qualification undertaken by a flight crew member. The operator should certify this instruction. 
It must also make the records of all conversion courses and recurrent training and checking available, on request, to the flight crew member concerned. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Flight and Duty Time Limitations 

An operator must establish a flight and duty time limitations and rest scheme (FTL) for crew members. Flights must be planned to be completed within the allowable flight duty period taking into account the time necessary for pre-flight duties, the flight and turn­around times. Duty rosters must be prepared and published sufficiently in advance to provide the opportunity for crew members to plan adequate rest. 
The rules and definitions can become quite involved and detailed, so only the basic definitions and limitations from the Regulations are outlined on the following pages. In practice, the rules you work to will be clearly detailed by the Operator (approved by the Authority) in Part A of the Operations Manual. 

Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Definitions 
¦ "Acclimatised" means a state in which a crew member's 
circadian biological clock is synchronised to the time zone 
where the crew member is. A crew member is considered to 
be acclimatised to a 2-hour wide time zone surrounding the 
local time at the point of departure. 
¦ "Augmented flight crew" means a flight crew which comprises more than the minimum number required to operate the aircraft, allowing each flight crew member to leave the assigned post, for the purpose of in-flight rest, and to be replaced by another appropriately qualified flight crew member. 
¦ "Break" means a period of time within a flight duty period, 
shorter than a rest period, counting as duty and during which 
a crew member is free of all tasks. 
¦ "Disruptive schedule" means a crew member's roster which 
disrupts the sleep opportunity during the optimal sleep time 
window. 
¦ "Night duty" means a duty period encroaching any portion of the period between 02:00 and 04:59 in the time zone to which the crew is acclimatised. 
¦ 
"Duty" means any task that a crew member performs for the operator, including flight duty, administrative work, giving or receiving training and checking, positioning, and some elements of standby. 

¦ 
"Duty period" means a period which starts when a crew member is required by an operator to report for or to commence a duty and ends when that person is free of all duties, including post-flight duty. 

¦ 
"Flight duty period ('FOP')" means a period that commences when a crew member is required to report for duty, and finishes when the aircraft finally comes to rest and the engines are shut down, at the end of the last sector on which the crew member acts as an operating crew member. 

¦ 
"Flight time" means the time between an aircraft first moving from its parking place for the purpose of taking off until it comes to rest on the designated parking position and all engines or propellers are shut down (block time). 

¦ 
"Home base" means the location, assigned by the operator to the crew member, from where the crew member normally starts and ends a duty period or a series of duty periods and where, under normal circumstances, the operator is not responsible for the accommodation of the crew member concerned. 



Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Definitions continued 
¦ 
"Local day" means a 24-hour period commencing at 00:00 local time. 

¦ 
"Local night" means a period of 8 hours falling between 22:00 and 08:00 local time. 

¦ 
"Operating crew member" means a crew member carrying out duties in an aircraft during a sector. 

¦ 
"Positioning" means the transferring of a non-operating crew member from one place to another, at the behest of the operator. 

¦ 
"Reserve" means a period of time during which a crew member is required by the operator to be available to receive an assignment for an FOP, positioning or other duty notified at least 10 hours in advance. 

¦ 
"Rest period" means a continuous, uninterrupted and defined period of time, following duty or prior to duty, during which a crew member is free of all duties, standby and reserve. 

¦ 
"Single day free of duty" means a time free of all duties and standby consisting of one day and two local nights, which is notified in advance. 


¦ 
"Standby" means a pre-notified and defined period of time during which a crew member is required by the operator to be available to receive an assignment for a flight, positioning or other duty without an intervening rest period. 

¦ 
"Airport standby" means a standby performed at the airport. 

¦ 
"Other standby" means a standby either at home or in a suitable accommodation. 

¦ 
"Window of circadian low ('WOCL')" means the period between 02:00 and 05:59 hours in the time zone to which a crew member is acclimatised. 



Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Limitations 
Duty Hours 
The total duty periods to which a crew member may be assigned shall not exceed: 
¦ 
60 duty hours in any 7 consecutive days 

¦ 
110 duty hours in any 14 consecutive days 

¦ 
190 duty hours in any 28 consecutive days. 



Flight Duty Period (FDP) 
The maximum basic daily FOP is 13 hours. This may be reduced for each sector from the third sector onwards with a maximum total reduction of two hours. The maximum FOP also depends on the time of day that the duty commences, acclimatisation to local time and duty encroach into the WOCL. 
Flight Time (Block Hours) 
The total flight time of the sectors on which an individual crew member is assigned as an operating crew member shall not exceed: 
¦ 
100 hours in any 28 consecutive days 

¦ 
900 hours in any calendar year and 

¦ 
1000 hours in any 12 consecutive calendar months. 



Post-flight duty shall count as duty period. The operator shall specify in its operations manual the minimum time period for post­flight duties. 
Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Flight Duty Hours -Extensions 
Planned Extension 
Without In-Flight Rest 
¦ 
The maximum daily FDP may be extended by up to 1 hour not more than twice in any 7 consecutive days. Of course there are conditions imposed regarding the minimum pre­flight and post-flight rest periods, and conditions on the number of sectors and WOCL encroachment. 

¦ 
Extension of the maximum basic daily FDP without in-flight rest shall not be combined with extensions due to in-flight rest or split duty in the same duty period. 



With In-Flight Rest 
¦ Flight time specification schemes shall specify the conditions for extensions of the maximum basic daily FDP with in-flight rest. The rules would be specific to the type of operation, taking into account the number of sectors flown, the in-flight rest allocated to each crew member and the type of in-flight rest facilities. 

Commander's Discretion 
To cope with unforeseen circumstances in flight operations the captain is allowed what is called "commander's discretion" to increase FDPs in-line with these regulations: 
¦ 
The maximum daily FDP may not be increased by more than 2 hours unless the flight crew has been augmented, in which case the maximum flight duty period may be increased by not more than 3 hours. If on the final sector within an FDP the allowed increase is exceeded because of unforeseen circumstances after take-off, the flight may continue to the planned destination or alternate aerodrome. The rest period following the FDP may be reduced but can never be less 

than 10 hours. 

¦ 
The commander must consider circumstances which could lead to severe fatigue, and if necessary reduce the actual flight duty period and/or increase the rest period in order to eliminate any detrimental effect on flight safety. 

¦ 
The commander shall consult all crew members, and shall submit a report to the operator. 

¦ 
Where the increase of an FDP or reduction of a rest period exceeds 1 hour, a copy of the report, to which the operator shall add its comments, shall be sent by the operator to the competent authority not later than 28 days after the event. 



Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Standby duty 
If an operator assigns crew members to standby at the airport, the following shall apply as applicable to the type of operation: 
¦ 
standby at the airport shall be in the roster and, the start and end time of standby shall be defined and notified in advance to the crew members concerned 

¦ 
a crew member is considered on airport standby from reporting at the reporting point until the end of the notified airport standby period 

¦ 
airport standby shall count in full as duty period 

¦ 
any FDP shall count in full from the airport duty reporting time 

¦ 
the operator shall provide accommodation 

¦ 
flight time specification schemes shall specify the following elements: 

¦ 
the maximum duration of any standby 

¦ 
the impact of the time spent on standby on the maximum FDP that may be assigned, taking into account facilities provided to the crew member to rest, and other relevant factors. 





Reserve 
If an operator assigns crew members to reserve, the following requirements shall apply as applicable to the type of operation: 
¦ 
reserve shall be in the roster 

¦ 
the operator must establish the maximum duration of any single reserve period and the number of consecutive reserve days that may be assigned. 



Flight Operations and Personnel Training 

Minimum Rest 
The minimum rest which must be provided before undertaking a 

flight duty period starting at home base shall be at least as long as the preceding duty period or 12 hours whichever is the greater. 
The minimum rest which must be provided before undertaking a flight duty period starting away from home base shall be at least as long as the preceding duty period or 10 hours whichever is the greater when on minimum rest away from home base, the operator must allow for an eight hour sleep opportunity taking due account of travelling and other physiological needs. 


Flight Operations and Personnel Training 


Flight Duty, Duty and Rest Period Records 
An operator shall ensure that crew member's records include: 
¦ 
block times 

¦ 
start, duration and end of each duty or flight duty periods and 

¦ 
rest periods and days free of all duties. 



These records must be maintained to ensure compliance with the requirements of the regulations copies of these records will be made available to the crew member upon request. 
If the records held by the operator do not cover all of his/her flight duty, duty and rest periods, the crew member concerned shall maintain an individual record. 
ORO.FTL.205 is the regulation covering Flight Duty Periods (FOP) and includes tables such as Table 2 (below) which is used to calculate the Maximum Daily FOP for acclimatised crew members dependent upon the start time of the FOP and the number of sectors. It is not necessary to memorise the table but EASA exam candidates may be asked to use it and the other associated tables in answering FOP questions. 
Maximum daily FOP -Acclimatised crew members

Lesso n 3 
AOCs 
ATPA OPB 03 

AOCs 
Introduction 

This chapter looks in more detail at Air Operators Certificates (AOC) and at the general obligations concerning the operation of public transport aircraft placed on the operator by the regulations. 

The AOC 
The AOC is the approval certificate 

The AOC is the certificate that permits an operator to run a commercial air transport operation. We have already considered it in outline, now we need to look at it in detail. 
A remindera-the rules regarding Commercial Air Transport (CAT) have been established by EASA. In each member state, the competent authority then oversees those rules and regulations for individual CAT operators whose principal place of business is in that state. 
The competent authority works to a set of rules and requirements established in regulations (Part ARO of EU Regulation 965/2012 ­Authority Requirements for Air Operations). These include the requirement for the authority to set up and organise a management system capable of ensuring that operators work within these rules. 


To qualify for an AOC, the operator has to tell the authority exactly what kind of CAT operations will be conducted, The Operator also has to produce documentation in support of that AOC. So far as operations are concerned, the primary document that we would be concerned with would be the operations manual. Of course the operator is not just producing an operations manual, there will be documentation supporting all aspects of the CAT organisation. An obvious example would be the maintenance organisation and all the rules and regulations for aeroplane servicing and 
maintenance. 
AOCs 
Issue of the Air Operator Certificate 

The competent authority shall issue the air operator certificate (AOC) when satisfied that the operator has demonstrated compliance with the Regulations. Responsibility for the operation is always placed on the operator, and the Regulations state this throughout EASAadocumentation. Indeed, the list below, copied directly from EU Regulation 965/2012 Part ORO (Organisation Requirements for Operators) illustrates this perfectly. 
Operator responsibilities 
¦ 
The operator is responsible for the operation of the aircraft in accordance with the relevant regulations. 

¦ 
Every flight shall be conducted in accordance with the provisions of the operations manual. 

¦ 
The operator shall establish and maintain a system for exercising operational control over any flight operated under the terms of its certificate. 

¦ 
The operator shall ensure that its aircraft are equipped and its crews are qualified as required for the area and type of operation. 




¦ 
The operator shall ensure that all personnel assigned to, or directly involved in, ground and flight operations are properly instructed, have demonstrated their abilities in their particular duties and are aware of their responsibilities and the relationship of such duties to the operation as a whole. 

¦ 
The operator shall establish procedures and instructions for the safe operation of each aircraft type, containing ground staff and crew member duties and responsibilities for all types of operation on the ground and in flight. These procedures shall not require crew members to perform any activities during critical phases of flight other than those required for the safe operation of the aircraft. 

¦ 
The operator shall ensure that all personnel are made aware that they shall comply with the laws, regulations and procedures of those States in which operations are conducted and that are pertinent to the performance of their duties. 

¦ 
The operator shall establish a checklist system for each aircraft type to be used by crew members in all phases of flight under normal, abnormal and emergency conditions to ensure that the operating procedures in the operations manual are followed. 


AOCs 
Issue of the Air Operator Certificate continued 
¦ 
The operator shall specify flight planning procedures to provide for the safe conduct of the flight based on considerations of aircraft performance, other operating limitations and relevant expected conditions on the route to be followed and at the aerodromes concerned. These procedures shall be included in the operations manual. 

¦ 
The operator shall establish and maintain dangerous goods training programmes for personnel which shall be subject to review and approval by the competent authority. 

¦ 
The operator must ensure crew members can communicate in a common language, and that operations personnel can understand the language in those parts of the Operations Manual which are relevant to their duties and responsibilities. 



So, the operator is given an AOC which lays out exactly what he can do in terms of CAT. The 'certificate' is awarded by the 'competent authority' in the state where he is registered. 
In applying for his AOC, the operator has told the authority what he wants to do and how he will do it. All of that has been detailed in the supporting paperwork (eg the operations manual). 


The procedures the operator outlines will essentially conform with EASA regulations: EU Regulation 216-2008 (which contains the principles for CAT operations) and EU Regulation 965-2012 which contain the details of how to operate. EU regulation 965-2012 is supported by further documentation called Acceptable Means of Compliance (AMC) and Guidance Material (GM). This document gives the operator even more detail of how to operate. 
If the operator wants to operate using a variation or alternative procedure, the authority would need to be informed with a supporting case it maybe a particular sector or operation that requires this variation. 
The competent authority would then evaluate the request proposed by an operator, and within the powers available to him, either approve, adjust or refuse the request. If the request is approved, the authority will inform the operator, EASA (the agency) and other member states. 
AOCs 
Issue Variation & Continued Validity of an AOC 

An operator will not be granted an AOC, or a variation to an AOC, and that AOC will not remain valid unless: 
(a) 
Aeroplanes operated have a standard Certificate of Airworthiness issued in accordance with the applicable regulations 

(b) 
The maintenance system has been approved by the 

Authority in accordance with OPS maintenance requirements and 

(c) 
The Authority is satisfied that the operator has the ability to: 

(i) 
establish and maintain an adequate organisation appropriate to the scale of the operation 

(ii) 
establish and maintain a quality system in accordance with the Regulations 




(iii) comply with required training programmes 
(iv) 
comply with maintenance requirements, consistent with the nature and extent of the operations specified, including the relevant items prescribed in the requirements to hold and AOC and 

(v) 
comply with the conditions of the AOC. 





Changes to the AOC 
The operator needs prior approval from the authority for any changes which affect the scope of the certificate or the CAT operations conducted or which change any of the elements of the operator's management system. 
For any changes requiring prior approval, the operator shall apply for and obtain an approval issued by the competent authority. The application shall be submitted before any such change takes place. The operator must provide the competent authority with any relevant documentation. 
The change shall only be implemented following formal approval by the competent authority. 
All changes not requiring prior approval shall be managed and notified to the competent authority as appropriate. 
AOCs 
Management Organisation 

The operator shall establish, implement and maintain a management system that includes: 
¦ 
clearly defined lines of responsibility and accountability throughout the operator, including a direct safety accountability of the accountable manager 

¦ 
a description of the overall philosophies and principles of the operator with regard to safety, referred to as the safety policy 

¦ 
the identification of aviation safety hazards entailed by the activities of the operator, their evaluation and the management of associated risks, including taking actions to mitigate the risk and verify their effectiveness 

¦ 
maintaining personnel trained and competent to perform their tasks 

¦ 
documentation of all management system key processes, including a process for making personnel aware of their responsibilities 

¦ 
a function to monitor compliance of the operator with the relevant requirements. This must include a feedback system of findings to ensure effective implementation of corrective actions. This 'function' was previously held under a 'Quality System'. The regulations specify a Maintenance Quality System. 




¦ any additional requirements that are prescribed in the 
relevant documentation. 

The management system shall correspond to the size of the operator and the nature and complexity of its activities. 
An Organization is classed as Complex if it has 20 or more staff a Simple Organization has less than 20 staff. 
AOCs 
Personnel Requirements 
¦ 
The operator shall appoint an accountable manager, who 

has the authority for ensuring that all activities are carried out in accordance with the applicable requirements. The accountable manager shall be responsible for establishing and maintaining an effective management system. 

¦ 
A person or group of persons shall be nominated by the operator, with the responsibility of ensuring that the operator remains in compliance with the applicable requirements. These would include persons responsible for the management and supervision of the following areas 

¦ 
flight operations 

¦ 
crew training 

¦ 
ground operations and 

¦ 
continuing airworthiness (Maintenance) 






¦ 
The operator shall have sufficient qualified personnel for the planned tasks and activities to be performed. 

¦ 
The operator shall maintain appropriate experience, qualification and training. 

¦ 
The operator shall ensure that all personnel are aware of the rules and procedures relevant to the exercise of their duties. 

¦ 
The operator must notify the authority of any proposed change of supervisory personnel. 


AOCs 
Safety Manager and Safety Review Board 
The management system of an operator should encompass safety by including a safety manager and a safety review board in the organisational structure. 
The safety manager should act as the focal point and be responsible for the development, administration and maintenance of an effective safety management system. The functions of the safety manager should be to: 
¦ 
facilitate hazard identification, risk analysis and management 

¦ 
monitor the implementation of actions taken to mitigate risks, 

as listed in the safety action plan 

¦ 
provide periodic reports on safety performance 

¦ 
ensure maintenance of safety management documentation 

¦ 
ensure that there is safety management training available and that it meets acceptable standards 

¦ 
provide advice on safety matters and 

¦ 
ensure initiation and follow-up of internal occurrence/accident investigations. 




The safety review board should be a high level committee that considers matters of strategic safety in support of the accountable manager's safety accountability. The board should be chaired by the accountable manager and be composed of heads of functional areas. The safety review board should monitor: 
¦ 
safety performance against the safety policy and objectives 

¦ 
that any safety action is taken in a timely manner and 

¦ 
the effectiveness of the operator's safety management 


processes. 

The safety review board should ensure that appropriate resources are allocated to achieve the established safety performance. The safety manager or any other relevant person may attend, as appropriate, safety review board meetings. He/she may communicate to the accountable manager all information, as necessary, to allow decision making based on safety data. 
AOCs 

Safety Policy 
The safety policy should: 
¦ 
be endorsed by the accountable manager 

¦ 
reflect organisational commitments regarding safety and its proactive and systematic 

¦ 
management 

¦ 
be communicated, with visible endorsement, throughout the operator and 

¦ 
include safety reporting principles. 




The safety policy should include a commitment: 
¦ 
to improve towards the highest safety standards 

¦ 
to comply with all applicable legislation, meet all applicable 

standards and consider best 


¦ 
practices 

¦ 
to provide appropriate resources 

¦ 
to enforce safety as one primary responsibility of all managers and 

¦ 
not to blame someone for reporting something which would not have been otherwise detected. 


AOCs 
Safety Management Manual 
The safety management manual (SMM) should be the key instrument for communicating the approach to safety for the whole of the operator. The SMM should document all aspects of safety management, including the safety policy, objectives, procedures and individual safety responsibilities. 
The contents of the safety management manual should include all of the following: 
¦ 
scope of the safety management system 

¦ 
safety policy and objectives 

¦ 
safety accountability of the accountable manager 

¦ 
safety responsibilities of key safety personnel 

¦ 
documentation control procedures 

¦ 
hazard identification and risk management schemes 

¦ 
safety action planning 

¦ 
safety performance monitoring 

¦ 
incident investigation and reporting 

¦ 
emergency response planning 




¦ 
management of change (including organisational changes with regard to safety responsibilities) and 

¦ 
safety promotion. 


The SMM may be contained in (one of) the manual(s) of the operator. 
AOCs 
Adequacy and Competency of Personnel 
The operator shall employ sufficient personnel for the planned ground and flight operations, and the operator must ensure all personnel assigned to, or directly involved in, ground and flight operations shall: 
¦ 
be properly trained 

¦ 
demonstrate their capabilities in the performance of their assigned duties and 

¦ 
be aware of their responsibilities and the relationship of their duties to the operation as a whole. 




Supervision of Personnel 
¦ 
The operator shall appoint a sufficient number of personnel supervisors, taking into account the structure of the operator's organisation. 

¦ 
The duties and responsibilities of these supervisors shall be defineda. 

¦ 
The supervision of crew members and personnel involved in the operation shall be exercised by individuals with adequate experience and. 


AOCs 


Operational Control and Supervision 
An operator must establish and maintain a method of exercising operational control approved by the Authority. The operator exercises operational control over any flight under the terms of his 
AOC. 
The operator exercises operational control over any flight 
under the terms of his AOC 



Power to Inspect 
An operator must ensure that any person authorised by the Authority is permitted at any time to board and fly in any aeroplane operated in accordance with an AOC issued by that Authority. Such a person (or persons) must be permitted to enter and remain on the flight deck provided that the commander may refuse access to the flight deck if, in his opinion, the safety of the aeroplane would thereby be endangered. 
Persons authorised by the Authority are permitted access to any documents and records relating to flight operations. The Operator is required to produce all such documents and records within a reasonable time when requested to do so by the Authority. Likewise, the Commander is required to produce any of the documents required to be carried on board an aeroplane when requested to do so by an authorised person. 
AOCs 
Maintenance 
Regulations 

Commission Regulation (EU) No 1321/2014 details the continuing airworthiness of aircraft and aeronautical products, and the approval of organisations and personnel involved in these tasks. 
Continuing airworthiness requirements: 
¦ 
The continuing airworthiness of aircraft shall be ensured in accordance with Annex I (Part-M). 

¦ 
Organisations and personnel involved in the continuing airworthiness of aircraft and components, including maintenance, shall comply with the provisions of Annex I (Part-M) 



Maintenance organisation approvals: 
¦ 
Organisations involved in the maintenance of large aircraft or of aircraft used for commercial air transport, and components intended for fitment thereto, shall be approved in accordance with the provisions of Annex II (Part-145). 

¦ 
Certifying staff shall be qualified in accordance with the provisions of Annex Ill (Part-66) 

¦ 
Organisations involved in the training of (maintenance) personnel shall be approved in accordance with Annex IV (Part-147). 




AOCs 
Aircraft Technical Log 
Each aircraft will have its own technical log, this will contain the following information for each aircraft: 
¦ 
information about each flight, necessary to ensure continued flight safety, and 

¦ 
the current aircraft certificate of release to service, and 

¦ 
the current maintenance statement giving the aircraft maintenance status of what scheduled and out of phase maintenance is next due except that the competent authority may agree to the maintenance statement being kept elsewhere, and 

¦ 
all outstanding deferred defects rectifications that affect the operation of the aircraft, and 

¦ 
any necessary guidance instructions on maintenance 


support arrangements. 

The aircraft technical log system and any subsequent amendment shall be approved by the competent authority. 
An operator shall ensure that the aircraft technical log is retained for 36 months after the date of the last entry. 


Release to Service 
No aircraft can be released to service unless a certificate of release to service is issued at the completion of any maintenance, when satisfied that all maintenance required has been properly carried out, by appropriate certifying staff on behalf of the maintenance organisation approved in accordance Part M or Part­66 as applicable. 
The release to service is saying the aircraft is fit for the intended flight. 
Pre-Flight Inspection 
Pre-flight inspection means the inspection carried out before flight to ensure that the aircraft is fit for the intended flight. The pilot-in­command or, in the case of commercial air transport, the operator shall be responsible for the satisfactory accomplishment of the pre-flight inspection. This inspection must be carried out by the pilot or another qualified person but need not be carried out by an approved maintenance organisation or by Part-66 certifying staff. A pre-flight inspection does not include defect rectification. 
AOCs 
Maintenance Rules 

Operators must either be approved to maintain their own aircraft in accordance with part 145, or contract an approved organisation to do their maintenance for them. The operator's AOC depends on following EASA Regulation maintenance requirements. 
An operator shall not operate an aeroplane unless it is maintained and released to service by an organisation appropriately approved in accordance with part 145, except that the pre-flight inspections need not necessarily be carried out by the part 145 organisation. 
There must be a nominated manager for maintenance. If the operator is using a contractor for maintenance the postholder should not, unless approved by the Authority, be an employee of the contractor. 
An operator must provide an operator's maintenance management exposition which must be approved by the Authority. It contains details of the organisation structure including the nominated managers responsible for the maintenance system 
and the procedures that must be followed. 


An operator must ensure that the aeroplane is maintained in accordance with the operator's aeroplane maintenance programme. The programme must contain details, including frequency of all maintenance required to be carried out. The programme may be required to include a reliability programme of the maintenance system. 
If an operator wishes to vary procedures from the approved standards, it must establish a need and present a case proving an equivalent level of safety. This must be approved by the Authority and supported by other member Authorities. 
AOCs 
The quality system is there to monitor compliance with, and the adequacy of, procedures required to ensure airworthy aircraft. Compliance monitoring shall include a feedback system to the accountable manager to ensure corrective action as necessary. A quality manager would designated. 
¦ The quality system shall monitor activities carried out under Regulations (Part M). It shall at least include the following functions: 
¦ 
monitoring that all activities are being performed in accordance with the approved procedures, and 

¦ 
monitoring that all contracted maintenance is carried out in accordance with the contract, and 

¦ 
monitoring the continued compliance with the requirements of this Part. 



The records of these activities shall be stored for at least two years. 
¦ 
Where the approved continuing airworthiness management organisation is approved in accordance with another Part, 

the quality system may be combined with that required by the other Part. 

¦ 
In case of commercial air transport the quality system provided shall be an integrated part of the operator's quality system. 




ICAO Annex 6 and EASA Regulations specify several manuals that the operator must create in support of its AOC application. In most cases these manuals will have been prepared by the operator and will contain information compiled from manuals provided by the State of the Operator, EASA and the aircraft and engine manufacturer. 
The Flight Manual 
The Flight Manual is usually based on manuals issued by the aircraft manufacturer and contains the information needed for normal and abnormal operation of the aircraft. 

The Maintenance Control Manual 
The Maintenance Control Manual will include the maintenance exposition and the maintenance programme. It contains the information required to properly maintain and service the aeroplane in accordance with ICAO, EASA, the State, and the aircraft and engine manufacturers' requirements. 
AOCs 
The Operations Manual 
So far as aircraft operations are concerned, by far the most important manual the operator produces is the Operations Manual. The Operations Manual must be amended or revised as is necessary to ensure that it is kept up to date. All such amendments or revisions must be issued to all personnel that are required to use this manual. 
The Authority (State of the Operator) must establish a requirement for the operator to provide a copy of the Operations Manual together with all amendments and/or revisions, for review and acceptance and, where required, approval. The operator must incorporate in the Operations Manual such mandatory material as the State of the Operator may require. 
Unless otherwise approved by the Authority, or prescribed by national law, an operator must prepare the Operations Manual in the English language. 
The Regulations are quite specific about how the Operations Manual should be structured:

The Sections listed above are just an overview of the detail contained in the Operations Manual. This manual contains all the detail you need for operating the aircraft. The rules, numbers and words will seem familiar when you first look through it they are based on EU Regulations, which in turn use ICAO Annex 6 as a starting block. There will be a few variations of course, depending on the operator, aircraft and route/role, but the basics will all sound very familiar to the Operational Procedure syllabus and detail that you are learning. 


Just as an example of the detail, Part A lists Operating Procedures under item 8. That sounds fairly straight forward, but if we looked at the Regulations we would find some 6 pages detailing exactly what needs to be there in terms of operating instructions. 
Part B of the Operations Manual can be supplemented with or substituted by parts of the Aircraft Flight Manual. In Part C, operator produced material can be substituted with route guide material produced by specialised companies (e.g. Jeppesen). 


AOCs 
Journey Logs 
The aeroplane journey log, or equivalent, is simply a single page form completed by the commander at the end of each flight. The information, or parts thereof, may be recorded in a form other than on printed paper, although accessibility should be assured. The 'or equivalent' means that the required information may be recorded in other documentation such as the operational flight plan or the aircraft technical log. 
The journey log is a single piece of paper completed by the commander at the end of each flight 

The Journey Log should contain the following information: 
¦ 
The aeroplane nationality and registration. 

¦ 
The date 

¦ 
The names of crew members. 

¦ 
The duty assignment of crew members. 

¦ 
Place of departure. 

¦ 
Place of arrival. 

¦ 
Time of departure 

¦ 
Time of arrival. 

¦ 
Hours of flight. 

¦ 
Nature of flight (private, aerial work, scheduled or non­scheduled). 

¦ 
Incidents, observations, if any. 

¦ 
Signature of person in charge. 


AOCs 
Technical Logs 
As we have already touched on, each aeroplane has its own technical log which is used to track maintenance and servicing carried out on the aircraft. 
Pilots enter all technical problems encountered during the sector in the technical log after flight. The maintenance personnel then either rectify the problem, or list it as an acceptable deferred defect (ADD) and records an entry in the tech log cancelling the previous entry by the pilot. Against each entry in the ADD section, the maintenance engineer would list a Minimum Equipment List (MEL) reference number which would show legality of flight with that particular piece of equipment inoperative. The MEL is discussed in more detail in Chapter 4 Minimum Equipment. 
The tech log entry by the engineer after rectification is called a maintenance release or release to service. It must include: 
¦ 
Basic details of the maintenance carried out 

¦ 
Date such maintenance was complete 

¦ 
When applicable, the identity of the approved maintenance organisation and 

¦ 
The identity of the person or persons signing the release. 




In order to accept an aeroplane for service the Captain checks the maintenance release is complete and has been signed by the engineer and the signs the technical log book in turn. 
The Captain signs the tech log to accept the aircraft
