Flight Planning Rules 

Choice of Level continued 
Operating above this optimum altitude will reduce the buffet boundaries, while operating below it will result in a fuel penalty 
and therefore a range penalty. The penalties are listed in the table below. 
Off-Optimum Condition 2000 ft above Optimum  Fuel Mileage LRC or Mach 0.74 -1 0  Penalty % Mach 0.78 -1 0  
2000 ft below  -1  -2  
4000 ft below  -4  -4  
8000 ft below  -10  -11  
12 000 ft below  -15  -20  


Example: 
For a mass of 55 000 kg, determine the optimum altitudes for long range cruise (LRC) and the cruise at Mach No 0.78. 
Use the table to determine, for each cruise, the fuel penalty if the aeroplane operates at an altitude of 29 000 ft at this mass. 
Flight Planning Rules 
Choice of Level continued 
Solution: 
When using this or any optimum altitude graph be sure to use the correct weight scale. 
Entering at the base of the graph at the CRUISE WEIGHT 55 000 kg travel vertically to the lines for 0.78 Mach and LRC /0.74 Mach in turn in each case then go horizontally left to 
read:  
For LRC, 10.74  34 200 ft  
For 0.78 Mach:  33 400 ft  
For LRC/Mach 0.74:  34 200 ft  

At the table below the graph the penalty in fuel and mileage is determined by interpolation: 
For 0.78 Mach, 29 000 ft is 4400 ft below the optimum, the correction is -4.7% 
For LRC, 29 000 ft is 5200 ft below the optimum, the correction is -5.8%. 

The optimum altitude is bound to be constrained by ATC requirements. One would normally file for the next available FL above the optimum, as the fuel burns off this level would become first the optimum then lower than the optimum. 
Once this happens the aircraft can climb another 2000 ft to 4000 ft if ATC and the cruise buffet boundary permit. 
Flight Planning Rules 
Choice of Route 
From a commercial point of view the operator would, at least in still air, wish to fly the direct, great circle, track to destination operating at all times at the optimum level and speed. Three things get in the way of this. 
¦ 
The first is the effect of wind, the shortest distance may not be the quickest route. 

¦ 
The second is ATC routings and requirements. 

¦ 
The third is the Regulatory Authority which impose restrictions on the routing to make sure that the flight is adequately safe so that loss of a system such as pressurisation or loss of an engine would not lead to loss of the aircraft. 


Therefore, a Minimum Time Route is the route that gives the shortest flight time from departure to destination adhering to all ATC and airspace restrictions. 


The Effect of Wind 
The minimum time route is calculated nowadays by computer. It is still possible to roughly work out a min time routing by construction, by dividing the route into one hour sectors and using the triangle of velocities to find the shortest time. 
The following example is only a demonstration of a coarse technique that can be used to find a minimum time route. No scale is given and you will not be asked to do this in the exam. You may, however, be asked to recognise the technique. Exam questions use the odd term 'isochronic lines' meaning lines of equal time. 
Example: 
An aircraft is to fly from A to Bat a TAS of 400 KT with the 
winds as shown. Determine the minimum time route by 
construction. 

Flight Planning Rules 
Solution: 
The first wind speed is 70 KT. Draw a wind vector 70 NM 
long from A. From the end of this line draw an arc corresponding to the TAS, 400 NM long and mark the tangent to the arc at the closest point to B. 
A .----------B 
Now draw the second wind vector 30 NM long from the new 
point and repeat the process. 

A --======------------B 
t 
Now the same for the third leg and join up the route at the tangents for the min time routing. 


The modern reality is that min time, min fuel and min cost routes and levels are selected by the computer flight planning system. A number of companies exist (Jeppesen is one) that produce operational flight plans for individual operators. The computer system produces the flight plan to an exact company specification, and variables such as aircraft weight, forecast winds and temperatures can all be taken into account in the selection of 
route and levels. Standard company routes would already be stored on the FMS database, and any slight variations could 
easily be inserted by the crew during preflight preparations. 
Flight Planning Rules 
ATC Requirements 
Certain routes such as the trans-Atlantic track from the US to Europe attract large amounts of traffic at particular times of day. 
Over the North Atlantic most of the traffic flies west to east (to Europe) overnight and back to North America in the daytime. As all the aircraft take-off over a short time period and all want the optimum levels and tracks there are ATC routing requirements to keep them apart. This is the North Atlantic organised track system (NAT OTS) which we will discuss later. 
The NAT OTS is the most extreme example of organised routing over the oceans but examples also exist in other areas such as the Pacific. 


Flight Planning Rules 
Alternate Aerodromes 
When flying under instrument flight rules (IFR) an alternate aerodrome must be included in the operational flight plan. An operator has to establish procedures for the selection of destination and alternate aerodromes that are adequate for the types of aeroplanes to be used and the operations to be undertaken. 
You must have an alternate for an IFR flight 
The learning objectives require ICAO Annex 6 definitions for alternate aerodromes these are fairly obvious, but for completeness, these definitions are given below: 


ICAO Definitions 
Alternate aerodrome. An aerodrome to which an aircraft may proceed when it becomes either impossible or inadvisable to proceed to or to land at the aerodrome of intended landing. Alternate aerodromes include the following: 
Take-off alternate. An alternate aerodrome at which an aircraft can land should this become necessary shortly after take-off and it is not possible to use the aerodrome of departure. 
En-route alternate. An aerodrome at which an aircraft would be able to land after experiencing an abnormal or emergency condition while en route. 
ETOPS en-route alternate. A suitable and appropriate alternate aerodrome at which an aeroplane would be able to land after experiencing an engine shutdown or other abnormal or emergency condition while en route in an ETOPS operation. 
Destination alternate. An alternate aerodrome to which an aircraft may proceed should it become either impossible or inadvisable to land at the aerodrome of intended landing. 
Note.-The aerodrome from which a flight departs may also be an en-route or a destination alternate aerodrome for that flight. 
Flight Planning Rules 
Aerodrome Selection (UK CAA) 
EASA Regulations 
The EASA regulations changed in October 2022, although the FCL Learning Objectives (LOs) have yet to be updated. The new EASA regulations, as covered by the present LOs are covered in a separate section at the end of this lesson. 
An adequate aerodrome is an aerodrome which the operator considers to be satisfactory, taking account of the applicable performance requirements and runway characteristics. In addition, it should be anticipated that, at the expected time of use, the aerodrome will be available and equipped with necessary ancillary services, such as ATS, sufficient lighting, communications, weather reporting, navaids and emergency services. 
Take-Off Alternate Aerodrome 
A take-off alternate aerodrome must be selected and specified in the operational flight plan if the weather conditions at the aerodrome of departure are at or below the applicable aerodrome operating landing minima or it would not be possible to return to the aerodrome of departure for other reasons. This take-off alternate aerodrome must be located within the following distance from the aerodrome of departure: Aeroplanes with two engines -within the distance equivalent to a flight time of one hour or, if authorised for ETOPS, the approved ETOPS diversion time (subject to any MEL restriction}, up to a maximum of 2 hours, at One Engine Inoperative (OEI) cruise speed in still air, standard conditions, based on the actual take-off 


mass. 
For a twin without ETOPS the take-off alternate must be within 60 minutes 

Aeroplanes with three or more engines -within a distance equivalent to a flight time of two hours at the One Engine Inoperative (OEI) cruise speed in still air, standard conditions, based on the actual take-off mass. If the Flight Manual does not contain an OEI cruising speed, the speed to be used for calculation must be that which is achieved with the remaining engine(s) set at maximum continuous power. 
The operator shall only select an aerodrome as a take-off alternate aerodrome when the appropriate weather reports and/or forecasts indicate that, during a period ETAa± 1 the weather conditions will be at or above the applicable landing minima. The ceiling shall be taken into account when the only approach operations available are non-precision approaches (NPA) and/or circling operations. Any limitation related to OEI operations shall 
Flight Planning Rules 
En-Route Alternate Aerodromes 
For the majority of IFR flights, the requirement for alternate aerodromes is limited to one for departure, if required, and at least one for the destination. However, for certain planning procedures an en-route alternate must be nominated at the planning stage. 
ETOPS En-Route Alternates 
In the case of extended range operations with two engine aeroplanes (ETOPS) sufficient en-route alternate aerodromes must be selected in order for the aircraft to be within the authorised ETOPS diversion time from a suitable diversion. The aerodrome must be adequate in terms of facilities (at least one instrument approach procedure) and the weather reports or forecasts must be better than the applicable planning minima for the period between the anticipated time of landing until one hour after the latest possible time of landing. 


En-Route Alternates 3% Contingency ERA and Reduced Contingency Fuel (RCF) Procedures 
Certain fuel planning procedures (RCF or 3% contingency procedures) make use of en-route alternates which must be nominated at the planning stage. The weather must be at or above planning minima from one hour before ETA to one hour after ETA. 
Flight Planning Rules 
Destination Alternates 
An operator must select at least one destination alternate for each IFR flight unless: 
1. 
the flight time (take-off to landing) is less than 6 hours, two separate runways are available at the destination and the meteorological conditions prevailing are such that, between one hour before and one hour after the ETA the ceiling will be at least 2000 ft or circling heighte+ 500 ft, whichever is greater, and the visibility will be at least 5 km or 

2. 
the destination is isolated and no adequate destination alternate exists. In this case the weather at the destination must be at least that required for a destination alternate aerodrome. The rules on fuel required state that at take-off, minimum fuel must be the sum of: 


TRIPe+ CONTINGENCYe+ 2 HOURS AT CRUISE (which includes Final Reserve). 


Separate runways in this context are separate landing surfaces, which may cross. If one of the runways is blocked, it must not prevent the planned type of operations on the other runway. Each of the landing surfaces must have a separate approach procedure based on a separate aid. 
An operator must select two destination alternates when the weather reports or forecasts for the destination indicate that between one hour before and one hour after the ETA the weather conditions will be below the applicable planning minima or when no meteorological information is available. 
Flight Planning Rules 

Aerodrome Operating Minima (UK CAA) 
The operator shall establish aerodrome operating minima for each departure, destination or alternate aerodrome planned to be used. These minima shall not be lower than those established for such aerodromes by the State in which the aerodrome is located, 
except when specifically approved by that State. Any increment specified by the competent (operator's) authority shall be added to the minima. 
In establishing the airport operating minima which apply to any particular operation full account must be taken of: 
¦ 
the type, performance and handling characteristics of the aeroplane 

¦ 
the composition of the flight crew, their competence and experience 

¦ 
the dimensions and characteristics of the runways, which may be selected for use 

¦ 
the adequacy and performance of the available visual and non-visual ground aids 

¦ 
the equipment available on the aeroplane for the purpose of navigation and/or control of the flight path during the approach to landing and the missed approach 



¦ 
the obstacles in the approach and missed approach areas and the obstacle clearance attitude/height for the instrument approach procedures 

¦ 
the means used to determine and report meteorological conditions and 

¦ 
the obstacles in the climb-out areas and necessary clearance margins. 


Flight Planning Rules 
Approach Types {UK CAA) 
Precision Approaches 
'Category I (CAT I) approach operation' means a precision instrument approach and landing using an instrument landing system (ILS), microwave landing system (MLS), GLS (ground­based augmented global navigation satellite system (GNSS/GBAS) landing system), precision approach radar (PAR) or GNSS using a satellite-based augmentation system (SBAS) with a decision height (DH) not lower than 200 ft and with a runway visual range (RVR) not less than 550 m for aeroplanes: 
'Category II (CAT II) operation' means a precision instrument approach and landing operation using ILS or MLS with: 
(a) 
DH below 200 ft but not lower than 100 ft and 

(b) 
RVR of not less than 300 m 


'Category IIIA (CAT IIIA) operation' means a precision instrument approach and landing operation using ILS or MLS with: 
(a) 
DH lower than 100 ft and 

(b) 
RVR not less than 200 m 




'Category 11IB (CAT 11IB) operation' means a precision instrument approach and landing operation using ILS or MLS with: 
(a) 
DH lower than 100 ft, or no DH and 

(b) 
RVR lower than 200 m but not less than 75 m. We will revisit these minima in chapter 7. 


Non-Precision Approaches 
'Non-precision approach (NPA) operation' means an instrument approach with a minimum descent height (MOH), or DH when flying a continuous descent final approach (CDFA) technique, not lower than 250 ft and an RVR/CMV of not less than 750 m for aeroplanes 
Flight Planning Rules 
Approach Techniques 
Stabilised approach (SAp) 
A precision approach is an example of a stabilised approach (SAp) it is an approach which is flown in a controlled and appropriate manner in terms of configuration, energy and control of the flight path from a predetermined point or altitude/height down to a point 50 ft above the threshold. 
The decision whether to land or not is made at DH. If the required visual reference is not achieved at DH, missed approach is initiated. During execution of the missed approach procedure the aeroplane will momentarily descend below DH. 
Continuous descent final approaches (CDFA) 
Historically non precision approaches (NPAs) were flown using a slightly higher rate of descent than used on a stabilised approach, with the aim of levelling out and achieving MOH in level flight at the missed approach point (MAP). The decision to land or execute missed approach was made at the MAP. This created a rather unsatisfactory situation because, if the decision was to land, the glideslope had to be regained from above and a stable flight path achieved rather quickly in order to carry out a safe flare and touchdown. 


Under EASA Regulations, this technique may not be used unless an exemption has been granted by the Authority. Instead, all 
NPAs are flown using a CDFA from at or above the final approach fix aiming to be approximately 15 m (50 ft) above the landing runway threshold. This creates a problem, because unlike the DH on a precision approach, the MOH or MDA on a NPA is a 'don't-go­below height' unless you have the required visual references. 
This means the MOH on a NPA cannot be used as a decision height. Because of this, on a CDFA, an artificial DH is created by adding a margin to the MOH, typically 50 ft. Thus a 'company decision height' or 'derived decision height' is created 50 ft above M DH. If the decision is made to execute a missed approach at the 'GOH', that extra 50 ft means that the aeroplane will not descend below MOH. Regulations leave the exact margin to be added to the MOH up to the operator, but this, along with NPA approach procedures must be detailed in the Operations Manual. The terminology will also be detailed, and as the CDFA has become 
the norm, we now just have a DH for a NPA. 

Flight Planning Rules 

Continuous descent final approaches (CDFA) continued 
In each case 'the required visual reference' means that section of the visual aids or of the approach area which should have been in view for sufficient time for the pilot to have made an assessment of the aircraft position and the rate of change of the position in 
J
relation to the desired flight path. In the case of a circling approach, the required visual reference is the runway environment. For a CAT II ILS, in order to continue past DH the visual reference could be as little as 'a segment of at least 3 consecutive lights' on the centre line of the approach lights for a CAT 111B (fail operational) approach, you cannot continue below DH unless a visual reference of 'at least one centre line light is attained and can be maintained'. 
MDA and DA (both altitudes) are referenced to mean sea level (MSL) or altimeter setting QNH. DH is referenced to the runway threshold (THR) elevation or altimeter setting QFE. MOH is referenced to the aerodrome(AD) elevation, or the runway THR elevation if that is more than 2 metres, or 7 feet below the aerodrome elevation. 
An MOH for a circling approach is referenced to the aerodrome elevation. 
OCH and OCA for each approach use the same reference as the approach. 
Flight Planning Rules

(1)

(4)

(5)

The minimum descent height (MOH) for an NPA operation flown without the CDFA technique should not be lower than the highest of: 
(1)

(2)

(3) 
the minimum MOH specified in theAFM, if stated. 


There is a further requirement that the ceiling and visibility minima can not be lower than those established by the State in which the aerodrome is located except when specifically approved by the State. 


The planning minima for a destination aerodrome are effectively the applicable approach operating minima. The RVR or visibility should be the same as or greater than the minima for the actual approach and, for a non-precision approach or a circling approach, the ceiling must be at or above DH or MOH as applicable for the period ETAa± 1 hour. Operating minima will be dependent not only on the actual approach being flown, but also by crew qualification and competence. 
Alternate Aerodromes 
The planning minima for alternate airfields are not the same as the aerodrome operating minima. Generally higher cloud bases and visibilities are required than the minima. Thus an alternate aerodrome with a CAT II approach allowing an RVR of 300 m would not be suitable for use as an alternate unless the weather conditions were CAT I, 550 m RVR, or better. This allows a margin for fate.

Flight Planning Rules 
EASA All Weather Operations and Aerodrome Selection 
The EASAAII weather Operations and Aerodrome Selection regulations now relate to the type of fuel policy used by the Operator: Basic Fuela/ Energy Policy or Basic Fuela/ Energy Policy with Variations, including in-flight fuel/energy management. The 
Individual Fuela/ Energy scheme is not considered for the exams. 
The Basic Fuel Policy uses 5% contingency fuel. 
The Basic Fuel Policy with Variations includes 3% Contingency, Reduced Contingency Fuel Procedure, and the Isolated aerodrome Procedure. 
Where possible the regulations have been paraphrased for brevity and ease of retention, but some regulations are presented in full because some question writers use the complete text when 
posing questions. 


Aerodromes are defined as follows: 
¦ 
An Adequate aerodrome, if at the expected time of use, the aerodrome is available and equipped with necessary ancillary services such as air traffic services (ATS), sufficient lighting, communications, meteorological reports, navigation aids and emergency services, including Rescue Fire Fighting services (RFFS). Weather conditions are not required to be considered. 

¦ 
A Weather-Permissible is an adequate aerodrome with additional weather condition requirements. 


Flight Planning Rules 
The operator shall ensure that sufficient means are available to navigate and land at the destination aerodrome or at any destination alternate aerodrome in the case of loss of capability for the intended approach and landing operation. le. If a GNSS approach was planned, the aircraft must have the capability of making an alternative approach if the GNSS fails: ILS or VOR/DME etc. 
Reaching Destination Aerodrome 
In the context of the basic fuel scheme and basic fuel scheme with variations, 'reaching the destination' means the point at which the aircraft has reached the applicable DA/H or MDA/H at the destination aerodrome. 


The take-off alternate aerodrome should not be farther from the departure aerodrome than: 
(a) 
For two-engined aeroplanes: 

1. 
1-hour flight time at the one-engine-inoperative (OEI) cruising speed according to theaAFM in ISAaand still-air conditions using the actual take-off mass, or: 

2. 
The extended-range twin operations (ETOPS) approved diversion time, subject to any minimum equipment list (MEL) restriction, up to a maximum of 2-hour flight time at OEI cruising speed according to the AFM in ISA and still-air conditions using the actual take-off mass. 



(b) 
For three or four-engined aeroplanes, 2-hour flight time at an all-engines-operating cruising speed according to the AFM in ISA and still-air conditions using the actual take-off mass. 

(c) 
For operations approved in accordance with Annex V (Part­SPA), Subpart L, Single-engined Turbine Aeroplane Operations at Night or in IMC (SET-IMC), 30 minutes flying time at normal cruising speed in still-air conditions, based on the actual take-off mass. 

(d) 
In the case of multi-engined aeroplanes, if the AFM does not contain an OEI cruising speed, the speed to be used for calculation shall be that which is achieved with the remaining enqine(s) set at maximum continuous power.

(a)

(b)

(c)

ii.) Flight Planning Rules 
Aerodrome Operating Criteria 

Basic Fuel Scheme-Destination Aerodromes -PBN Operations 
¦ 
When the operator intends to use PBN, the operator should select an aerodrome as destination alternate aerodrome only if an instrument approach procedure that does not rely on a GNSS is available either at that aerodrome or at the destination aerodrome. 

¦ 
A take-off or an ERA aerodrome with instrument approach procedures that rely on a GNSS may be planned without restrictions. A destination aerodrome with all instrument approach procedures that rely solely on a GNSS may be used without a destination alternate aerodrome if the conditions for a flight without a destination alternate aerodrome are met. 


Flight Planning Rules 
Operational Credits 
When the operator intends to use 'operational credits' (e.g. EFVS, SA CAT I, etc.), the operator should select an aerodrome as destination alternate aerodrome only if an instrument approach procedure that does not rely on the same 'operational credit' is available either at that aerodrome or at the destination aerodrome. 
(a) The operator shall establish procedures for in-flight 
fuel/energy management that ensure: 
1. 
Continual validation of the assumptions made during the planning stage (pre-flight or in-flight re-planning, or both). 

2. 
Re-analysis and adjustment, if necessary. 

3. 
That the amount of usable fuel/energy remaining on board is protected and not less than the fuel/energy that is required to proceed to an aerodrome where a safe landing can be made and: 

4. 
Relevant fuel/energy data for the purpose of points (1 ), (2), and (3) shall be recorded. 




(b) The operator shall have procedures in place to require the commander to obtain delay information from a reliable source when unforeseen circumstances may result in landing at the destination aerodrome with less than the final reserve fuel/energy plus any: 
1. 
Fuel/energy to proceed to an alternate aerodrome, if required or 

2. 
Fuel/energy required to proceed to an isolated aerodrome. 



(c) 
The commander shall advise air traffic control (ATC) of a 'minimum fuel/energy' state by declaring 'MINIMUM FUEL' when the commander has 

1. 
committed to land at a specific aerodrome and 

2. 
calculated that any change to the existing clearance to that aerodrome may result in landing with less than the planned final reserve fuel/energy. 



(d) 
The commander shall declare a situation of 'fuel/energy emergency' by broadcasting 'MAYDAY MAYDAY MAYDAY FUEL' when the usable fuel/energy that is calculated to be available upon landing at the nearest aerodrome where a safe landing can be made is less than the planned final reserve fuel/energy. 


Flight Planning Rules 
In-flight fuel management 
Basic Fuel Scheme -Relevant Data to be recorded 
(a) The operator may decide at which regular intervals the 
relevant fuel data should be recorded. 
An example of such intervals could be every 30 minutes for short­range flights and every 60 minutes for longer flights. 
(b) The operator should record at least the following relevant fuel-related data: 
1. 
Off-block fuel. 

2. 
Take-off fuel if this data can be recorded automatically. 

3. 
'MINIMUM FUEL' declarations. 

4. 
'MAYDAY MAYDAY, MAYDAY FUEL' declarations. 

5. 
Fuel after touchdown if this data can be recorded automatically and 

6. 
On-block fuel. 




When an aircraft communications addressing and reporting system (AGARS) is available, the operator does not need to record this data. 
Reliable Source to Obtain Delay Information 
(c) A reliable source to obtain delay information may be derived from data provided by an air navigation services provider (ANSP) and should have the following characteristics ranked in order of priority: 
1. 
Integrity provide timely warnings to users when the delay information should not be used. 

2. 
Availability the time during which the delay information is accessible to the crew. 

3. 
Accuracy the degree of conformity between the estimated delay and the true delay the delay information should be communicated with its corresponding gap error, e.g. delay of 15 ± 2 minutes the gap error should be added to the base value. 

4. 
Continuity the capability of the service to provide the delay information without unscheduled interruptions during the intended operation. 


Flight Planning Rules 
(d) 
The 'MINIMUM FUEL' declaration informs the ATC that all planned aerodrome options have been reduced to a specific aerodrome of intended landing. It also informs the ATC that any change to the existing clearance may result in landing with less than the planned FRF. This is not an emergency situation but an indication that an emergency situation is possible, should any additional delay occur. 

(e) 
When committed to land at a specific aerodrome, the commander should take into account any operational factor that may cause a delay to landing, and thus determine whether the aircraft will land with less than the planned FRF, even after receiving clearance from ATC. A change that may cause a delay to landing could be other than the ATC, e.g. a change of weather conditions, etc. If any such factor is likely to result in landing with less than the planned FRF, the commander should declare 'MINIMUM FUEL' to ATC. 

(f) 
The pilot should not expect any form of priority handling as a result of a 'MINIMUM FUEL' declaration. However, the ATC should advise the flight crew of any additional expected delays, as well as coordinate with other ATC units when transferring the control of the aeroplane, to ensure that the other ATC units are aware of the flight's fuel state. 




Example 1: The aircraft is on the final approach to the destination aerodrome with a single runway, with just the destination alternate fuel plus FRF available. The aircraft ahead has a tyre burst upon landing and has stopped on the runway. The ATC orders the aircraft on final approach to execute a go-around as the destination aerodrome is closed due to a blocked runway. After completing the go-around, the flight crew decides to divert to the destination alternate aerodrome. After the ATC gives clearance for the destination alternate aerodrome and if the calculated fuel upon landing is close to the FRF, the flight crew should declare 'MINIMUM FUEL'. The flight crew has now committed to land at the destination alternate aerodrome, and any change to the clearance may result in landing there with less than the planned FRF. 
Example 2: The aircraft is approaching the clearance limit point, which has a holding pattern operating at the time. The ATC gives the aircraft an expected arrival time that would result in a delay of 25 minutes, and the aircraft enters the holding zone. On receiving this information and prior to entering the holding pattern, the remaining fuel is ?-minute contingency fuel plus 25-minute destination alternate fuel plus 30-minute FRF. The weather conditions and aircraft serviceability are such that the flight crew can convert the destination alternate fuel into holding time over the destination aerodrome. When the remaining fuel no longer allows a diversion from the holding pattern, then the flight crew 
Flight Planning Rules 
'Minimum Fuel' Declaration continued 
Example 3: The aircraft reaches FL350, which is the cruising flight level on its 5-hour flight. The weather forecast information that was obtained before departure was favourable and, therefore, the commander did not order any discretionary fuel. The destination alternate fuel is sufficient for 25-minute flight time and the destination alternate aerodrome is located beyond the destination aerodrome. For some reason (unexpected severe turbulence, cockpit window crack, etc.), the aircraft has to descend and continue the flight at FL230, where fuel consumption is higher. In­flight fuel checks and fuel management now show that the destination aerodrome can still be reached but only if in-flight re­planning is done without the destination alternate aerodrome (the destination aerodrome has two runways and good weather, and it is less than 6-hour flight time away, thus meeting the conditions for not requiring an alternate aerodrome). By doing so, the aircraft 
will arrive at destination for a straight-in approach with exactly the FRF plus 15-minute flight time. During the next 3.5 hours, an ERA aerodrome is available, and the situation is under control. 


When approaching the destination, the aircraft has to commit to land at the destination aerodrome as there is no other destination alternate aerodrome within 15 minutes of reaching the destination aerodrome. 
The ATC now informs the pilots that there is a change of landing runway resulting in a 12-minute trip fuel increase. It is time to declare 'MINIMUM FUEL'. 
Flight Planning Rules 
Basic Fuel Scheme-Procedures for in-flight Fuel Management 
In-flight fuel checks 
1. 
The operator should establish a procedure to ensure that in­flight fuel checks are carried out at regular intervals or at specified points indicated in the operational flight plan (one check at least every 60 minutes). 

2. 
The remaining usable fuel should be evaluated to: 

i) compare the actual consumption with the planned consumption. 
ii) check that the remaining usable fuel is sufficient to complete the flight and 
(iii) determine the usable fuel that is expected to remain upon landing at the destination aerodrome. 

3. 
In relation to the recording of relevant data, the operator should: 


i) agree with the competent authority on what constitutes relevant data for the purpose of recording. 
ii) use the relevant data as safety performance indicators (SP ls) of the current fuel scheme and 


In-Flight Fuel Management 
1. The flight should be conducted to ensure that the usable fuel expected to remain upon landing at the destination aerodrome is not less than: 
i) The required alternate fuel plus the FRF or 
ii) The FRF if no alternate aerodrome is required. 

2. If an in-flight fuel check shows that the usable fuel expected to remain upon landing at the destination aerodrome is less than: 
i) the required alternate fuel plus the FRF, the commander should request delay information from the ATC, and take into account the prevailing traffic and operational conditions at the destination aerodrome, at the destination alternate aerodrome, and at any other adequate aerodrome, to decide whether to proceed to the destination aerodrome or to divert in order to 
perform a safe landing with not less than the FRF or 
ii) the FRF, if no destination alternate aerodrome is required, the commander should take appropriate action and proceed to an aerodrome where a safe landing can be made with not less than the FRF.

ii) check that the remaining usable fuel is sufficient to complete the flight and

2.

5.

1.

2. 
Contingency fuel from the PNR to the destination isolated aerodrome and 

3. 
The relevant additional fuel required for isolated aerodromes. 



Commander's Responsibility 
The commander shall only commence a flight or continue in the event of in-flight re-planning, when satisfied that the aircraft carries at least the planned amount of usable fuel/energy and oil to safely complete the flight, taking into account the expected operating conditions. 


Lesson 8 
Fuel and Oil Requirements 
ATPA OP8 08 
Fuel and Oil Requirements 
A flight must not be commenced unless, taking into account both the meteorological conditions and any delays that are expected in flight, the aeroplane carries sufficient fuel and oil to ensure that it can safely complete the flight. In addition, a reserve must be carried to provide for contingencies. 
The fuel load is split into distinct elements, and under OPS these are: 
Taxy Fuel 
Fuel required for start up and taxy to the end of the runway. An operator will probably use a standard figure for particular aircraft type, but this can be varied to account for local conditions, 
Trip Fuel 
Unless otherwise specified the fuel required from take-off to touchdown. 
Reserve Fuel 
Reserve fuel is required consisting of: 
Contingency. An amount of fuel to allow for unforecast winds and ATC re-routes. Contingency fuel can be calculated by a number of methods it should be the greater of the selected calculation from list A (the operations manual will tell you which method is to be used), compared with calculation B. 


ListeA 
¦ 
5% of planned trip fuel 

¦ 
3% of planned trip fuel (if an en-route diversion is available 

¦ 
20 minutes flying time (providing an individual airframe fuel monitoring programme is established) and 

¦ 
an amount of fuel based on a statistical method (typically 


used on long haul sectors). 

Calculation B 
5 minutes holding 1500 ft above the destination aerodrome 
Diversion fuel. Total fuel required from the point of diversion to touchdown at the alternate 
Final reserve fuel. Defined by the operator in the Operations Manual, but not less than: 
¦ 
turbine engine aeroplanes, fuel for 30 minutes holding at 1500 ft above the aerodrome, or 

¦ 
reciprocating engine aeroplanes, fuel for 45 minutes holding at 1500 ft above the aerodrome 


Fuel and Oil Requirements 
Reserve Fuel continued 
Additional fuel: 
¦ 
This is an amount of fuel that would cater for failure of a power unit or loss of pressurisation, based on the assumption that such a failure occurs at the most critical point. Fuel should include fuel to descend as necessary, proceed to an adequate aerodrome and hold for 15 minutes a 1500 ft, and make an approach and landing. 

¦ 
Holding for 15 minutes at 1500 ft (450 m) above destination aerodrome elevation in standard conditions, when a flight is operated without a destination alternate aerodrome. 




Extra Fuel 
This is at the discretion of the commander and could take into account a number of factors -weather, likelihood of holding and routing problems. Local knowledge and experience play a key part in this decision. 
The sum of the above 'elements' of fuel make up the total fuel loaded into the aeroplane on the ramp, simply called RAMP (or BLOCK) FUEL 
RAMP FUELa= TAXY +aTRIPa+ RESERVEa+ EXTRA 
That is the 'standard' fuel calculation that will be used on most sectors, but, as always, there are some additional rules that might apply to some special planning scenarios. We will consider 2 of those scenarios, the RCF procedure and the Pre-Determined Point (PDP) procedure. 
Fuel and Oil Requirements 
The Reduced Contingency Fuel (RCF) Procedure 
The RCF procedure (previously the 'decision point procedure') offers additional flexibility in fuel planning for an operator. The procedure makes use of an en-route alternate (ERA) and a decision point (DP). The ERA provides an optional refuel destination. The RCF procedure reduces the minimum fuel required for the sector by reducing contingency fuel. This may only reduce the fuel by a few tonnes, but this may be the difference required in order to fly the sector without offloading payload. 
The amount of fuel required should be the greater of the following two calculations: 
Calculation 1 : 
Taxy fuel 
+ 
Sector fuel (to destination via DP) 

+ 
Contingency (5% of trip DP to destination) 

+ 
Alternate 

+ 
Final reserve 

+ 
Additional fuel 

+ 
Extra fuel = Total fuel 




Calculation 2: 
Taxy fuel 
+ 
Sector fuel (to ERA via DP) 

+ 
Contingency (departure to ERA via DP) 

+ 
Alternate 

+ 
Final reserve 

+ 
Additional fuel 

+ 
Extra fuel 


= Total fuel 
Use of the RCF procedure requires the approval of the Authority. When and exactly how an operator can use the procedure will be contained in the Aircraft Operations Manual. 
Fuel and Oil Requirements 
Example of the RCF Procedure 
Consider the following scenario. An aeroplane is chartered to operate a route from Prestwick to Toronto with Ottawa as the alternate. The route over-flys Goose Bay. 
Because of payload and performance restrictions, the fuel load on take-off from Prestwick is limited to only 73 000 kg. 
We start the calculations as normal for the route and come out with the following fuel plan: 
Trip fuel to Toronto  62 000 kg  
Contingency 5%  3100 kg  
Alternate (Ottawa)  3600 kg  
Final reserve  6000 kg  
Total fuel  74 700 kg  

Which is awkward as we only have room for 73 000 kg without unloading passengers. We now revert to plan B, and look at applying the reduced contingency fuel (RCF) procedure using Goose Bay as our ERA. The fuel calculations work as follows on the next page: 



Fuel and Oil Requirements 
Example of the RCF Procedure continued 
Calculation 1  
Trip fuel to Toronto via DP  62 000 kg  
Cont 5% DP to Toronto only  1000 kg  
Alternate (Ottawa)  3600 kg  
Final reserve  6000 kg  
Total fuel  72 600 kg  
Calculation 2  
Trip Fuel to Goose Bay via DP  54 700 kg  
Contingency (5% of trip)  2735 kg  
Alternate (St Johns)  2300 kg  
Final reserve  6000 kg  
Total fuel  65 735 kg  

From the two totals, we must take the higher value, 72 600 kg, which is within our 73 000 kg limit. 

Total fuel load consists of ...

Other Fuel Planning Procedures 
The Pre-Determined Point (PDP) Procedure 
If an operator's fuel policy includes planning to a destination alternate aerodrome where the distance between the destination aerodrome and the destination alternate aerodrome is such that a flight can only be routed via a predetermined point (PDP) to one of these aerodromes, the amount of usable fuel, on board for departure, should be the greater of (1) or (2): 
(1) The sum of: 
¦ 
taxi fuel 

¦ 
trip fuel from the departure aerodrome to the destination aerodrome, via the PDP 

¦ 
contingency fuel 

¦ 
additional fuel if required, but not less than: 


(a) 
for aeroplanes with reciprocating engines, fuel to fly for 45 minutes plus 15% of the flight time planned to be spent at cruising level or 2 hours, whichever is less or 

(b) 
for aeroplanes with turbine engines, fuel to fly for 2 hours at normal cruise consumption above the destination 


aerodrome, this should not be less than final reserve fuel and 


¦ extra fuel if required by the commander. 
(2) The sum of: 
¦ 
taxi fuel 

¦ 
trip fuel from the departure aerodrome to the destination alternate aerodrome, via the predetermined point 

¦ 
contingency fuel 

¦ 
additional fuel if required, but not less than: 


(a) 
for aeroplanes with reciprocating engines: fuel to fly for 45 minutes or 

(b) 
for aeroplanes with turbine engines: fuel to fly for 30 minutes at holding speed at 1500 ft (450 m) above the destination alternate aerodrome elevation in standard conditions, this should not be less than final reserve fuel and 


¦ extra fuel if required by the commander. 
Fuel and Oil Requirements 

Isolated Aerodrome Procedure 
If an operator's fuel policy includes planning to an isolated aerodrome, the last possible point of diversion to any available en­route alternate aerodrome shall be used as the predetermined point, then continue as above. 
The Regulations also actually define what we mean by an isolated aerodrome: 
An isolated aerodrome is one for which the alternate and final fuel reserve required to the nearest adequate destination alternate aerodrome is more than: 
¦ 
for aeroplanes with reciprocating engines, fuel to fly for 45 minutes plus 15% of the flying time planned to be spent at cruising level or two hours, whichever is less or 

¦ 
for aeroplanes with turbine engines, fuel to fly for two hours at normal cruise consumption above the destination aerodrome, including final reserve fuel.

Performance and Mass Considerations 

The regulations separate aircraft into three performance categories labelled Class A, B and C with performance Class A being the most capable. ICAO do not use this classification. Annex 6 lists some general requirements for all aircraft and then some more specific requirements relating to multi-engine aircraft with MCTOM in excess of 5700 kg. These roughly correspond to EASA Class A and C. We will consider the EASA performance restrictions later.

General Requirements 
The competent authority is responsible for ensuring aeroplanes are operated in accordance with a comprehensive and detailed code of performance. In practice, because aeroplanes are produced for international sales, the requirements in different states only differ in the detail. 
An aeroplane must be operated according to any terms and restrictions on its certificate of airworthiness and within the approved operating limitations of the Aeroplane Flight Manual (AFM). This means that maximum take-off mass, max landing mass and other limitations must be observed. 
A flight must not be commenced unless the performance information provided in the flight manual indicates that the performance requirements regarding the aeroplane, ambient weather conditions, aerodrome characteristics (including elevation) and the runway conditions, length and gradient can be complied with the flight to be undertaken. 


In fulfilling these requirements, account must be taken of all factors that significantly affect the performance of the aeroplane. 
These factors will include: 
¦ 
mass 

¦ operating procedures 
¦ 
the pressure-altitude appropriate to the elevation of the 

aerodrome 


¦ 
temperature 

¦ 
wind 

¦ 
runway gradient and condition of runway, i.e. presence of slush, water and/or ice, for land planes, water surface condition for seaplanes and 

¦ 
the presence of slush, significant water, ice or snow on a 


runway is known as 'runway contamination'. 

Single-engine aeroplanes must only be operated in conditions of weather and light, and over such routes and diversions there from that permit a safe forced landing to be executed in the event of engine failure.

Take-Off 
If a critical power unit fails on take-off the aircraft must either be able to reject the take-off and stop in the accelerate stop distance available or continue the take-off and clear obstacles by an adequate margin. Account must be taken of any distance lost as the aircraft lines up. 
The aircraft must also be able to satisfy a limitation of minimum climb gradient governed by its weight, the aerodrome pressure altitude and the air temperature. 
En-Route 
One Power-Unit Inoperative 
If a single power unit is lost en-route or on diversion the aeroplane must not descend below the minimum safe altitude and must be able to reach and safely land at an aerodrome. 
Two Power-Units Inoperative 
Aircraft with three or more power units are allowed to operate at longer ranges from airfields than twin engine aircraft without ETOPS. Beyond a certain distance from a suitable aerodrome such an aircraft must be able to sustain a double engine failure, continue to an en-route alternate and land. 


Landing 
At a destination or alternate landing aerodrome the aircraft must be able to clear obstacles in the approach path by a safe margin, be able to land and stop within the landing distance available. Allowance must be made for variations in the approach and landing techniques if such allowance has not been built into the performance data. 
The aircraft must also be able to satisfy a separate landing climb limit. 
We will look at performance requirements under EASA regulations in a later Lesson. 


Lesson 9 
Final Preparations 
ATPA OP8 09 
Fina IPre pa rations 
Introduction 
There are a series of final considerations for the aircraft commander before take-off. The latest weather forecasts and actuals for departure, destination and diversion must be considered, the paperwork must be complete and on board and the commander must make sure the aircraft is ready for flight. 


Aircraft Contents, Equipment and Paperwork 
The commander must not commence a flight unless he or she is satisfied that: 
¦ 
the aeroplane is airworthy 

¦ 
the aeroplane is not operated contrary to the provisions of the configuration deviation list (CDL) 

¦ 
the aeroplane is fitted with the instruments and equipment required for the flight to be conducted 

¦ 
the instruments and equipment are in serviceable condition except as provided in the MEL 

¦ 
the required documents are on board 

¦ 
ground facilities and services required for the planned flight are available and adequate 

¦ oxygen requirements, minimum safe altitudes, aerodrome operating minima and availability of alternate aerodromes, where required, can be complied with for the planned flight 

¦ 
the load is properly distributed and safely secured 

¦ 
the performance criteria can be satisfied 

¦ 
a flight plan has been filed and 

¦ 
any additional operational limitation can be complied with. 


Fina IPre pa rations 
Weather 
Take-Off Weather 
Take-Off Operations -Aeroplanes 
Take-off minima for each aerodrome should be expressed as visibility or runway visual range (RVR) limits. Where there is a specific need (e.g. to avoid obstacles on departure) additional conditions such as ceiling may be specified. 
The commander should not commence take-off unless the weather conditions at the aerodrome of departure are equal to or better than applicable minima for landing at that aerodrome unless a take-off alternate aerodrome is available. 
When the reported meteorological visibility (VIS) is below that required for take-off and RVR is not reported, or when no reported meteorological visibility or RVR is available, a take-off should only be commenced if the commander can determine that the visibility along the take-off runway is equal to or better than the required minimum. 


Visual references -general 
The take-off minima should be selected to ensure sufficient guidance to control the aircraft in the event of both a rejected take­off in adverse circumstances and a continued take-off after failure of the critical engine. 
For night operations, ground lights should be available to illuminate the runway and any obstacles. 
Take-off minima when not approved for LVO

Fina IPre pa rations 
Low Visibility Take-Offs 
Take-offs in RVRs of less than 400m fall into the category of Low Visibility Operations (LVO), making them Low Visibility Take-Offs (LVTOs). 
The operator may only conduct Low Visibility Operations when approved by the competent authority. The aerodrome must also be approved for LVO by the state of the aerodrome, and Low Visibility Procedures (LVP) need to be established. If the operator selects an aerodrome where the term LVP is not used, the operator must ensure that there are equivalent procedures in place. 
The crew need to be trained and qualified, and that is both commander and co-pilot. A minimum experience level will be required before they become eligible for training this and the training programme would be detailed in Part D of the operations manual. 
The requirements for a Low Visibility Take-Off are shown in the following table. 
In addition to the provisions in the table, for LVTOs with an RVR of between 150 m and 125 m (ie fourth row of the table) there must be a 90 m visual segment seen from the flight deck at the start of the take-off run.

Fina IPre pa rations 
Destination and Diversion Weather 
To commence take-off on an IFR flight or to revise the flight plan in flight the expected weather conditions at the destination and/or required alternate aerodromes must be at or above the planning minima we have already encountered. 
Once the aircraft is airborne these rules change the flight cannot continue to destination unless either the destination or the alternate are above the planning applicable aerodrome operating minima at the estimated time of arrival. So once airborne, even if the destination weather goes below minimums, you can continue provided the weather at the destination alternate is within limits. 
On a VFR flight a commander must not take-off unless forecasts indicate that compliance with VFR will be possible throughout. 


Fina IPre pa rations 
After Take-Off 
The normal and abnormal flight procedures are detailed in the Aircraft Flight Manual/ Operations Manual. EASA regulations and ICAO have the following further requirements which should be found in the Operations Manual. Detailed information about noise abatement procedures is to be found in Part Aerodromes (AD), sections 2 and 3 of the AIP. 
Noise Procedures 
Noise abatement procedures are common for take-off and landing at international airports, the local Authority is responsible for ensuring noise abatement objectives are specified by aerodrome operators. 
Preferred runway directions for noise abatement are published to avoid noise sensitive areas. For a runway to be 'preferred' it must have a suitable glide path guidance system like ILS or, in visual conditions, VASls or PAP ls. Noise abatement departure routings may be published for any runway. 


Compliance with noise abatement procedures which require a reduced power take-off is not required: 
¦ 
if the runway is not clear and dry 

¦ 
if the visibility is less than 1900 m (1 NM) 

¦ 
if the crosswind is more than 15 KT, including gusts 

¦ 
if the tailwind is greater than 5 KT, including gusts 

¦ 
when wind shear is reported or forecast or 

¦ 
when thunderstorms are expected to affect the approach or 


departure. 

The same list with the addition of 'if the cloudbase is below 500ft' applies to compliance with all noise abatement procedures on landing. The pilot in command also has the authority to decide either to not start or to abandon a noise abatement procedure on safety grounds. 
An operator has to establish departure and arrival/approach procedures for each aircraft type (approved by the Authority) in accordance with the following: 
¦ 
Safety has priority over noise abatement. 

¦ 
The procedures shall be designed to be simple and safe to operate with no significant increase in crew workload during critical phases of flight. 


Fina IPre pa rations 
Noise Procedures continued 
For each aeroplane type two departure procedures shall be defined, in accordance with ICAO Doc 8168: 
¦ 
Noise abatement departure procedure one (NADP 1 ), which reduces noise near the airfield and 

¦ 
Noise abatement departure procedure two (NADP 2), which 


reduces noise away from the airfield. 
Each NADP climb profile can only have one sequence of actions. Both procedures require an initial climb under full power climbing at V2 + 10 KT to V2 + 20 KT. NADP 1 reduces thrust early and delays flap retraction to 3000 ft, NADP 2 starts flap retraction under full power at a minimum of 800 ft. Diagrams of the two procedures are shown here. 
If a noise abatement procedure other than one of these is approved some minimum criteria must be observed: 
1. The minimum speed must not be less than V2 + 10 KT unless 
this exceeds the maximum body angle. 
2. 
If thrust reductions are used they must be after 800 ft. 

3. 
No turns may be required as thrust is being reduced. 

4. 
Obstacle clearance must be maintained allowing for the time to reset full power if an engine fails.

Fina IPre pa rations 
Simulated Abnormal Situations in Flight 
Simulated emergencies and simulated IMC (screens) are not allowed on public transport flights. 
Simulated emergencies in flight are not allowed 


In-Flight Fuel Management 
An operator must establish a procedure to ensure that in-flight fuel checks and fuel management is carried out. A commander must ensure that the amount of usable fuel remaining in flight is not less than the fuel required to proceed to an aerodrome where a safe landing can be made, with final reserve fuel remaining. 
Note that this means that, once airborne, it is legal to continue to destination even if you don't have sufficient fuel to divert ­provided you are comfortable that a safe landing can be made. The regulations state: 
If, as a result of an in-flight fuel check, the expected fuel remaining on arrival at the destination is less than the required alternate fuel plus final reserve fuel, the commander must take into account the traffic and the operational conditions prevailing at the destination aerodrome, at the destination alternate aerodrome and at any other adequate aerodrome, in deciding whether to proceed to the destination aerodrome or to divert so as to perform a safe landing with not less than final reserve fuel, 
The commander shall declare an emergency when calculated usable fuel on landing, at the nearest adequate aerodrome where a safe landing can be performed, is less than final reserve fuel. 
Fina IPre pa rations 
Ground Proximity Detection 
When undue proximity to the ground is detected by any flight crew member or by a ground proximity warning system, the 
commander or the pilot to whom conduct of the flight has been delegated must ensure that corrective action is initiated immediately to establish safe flight conditions. 


Fina IPre pa rations 
Occurrence Reporting 
Flight Incidents 
The operator or commander of an aeroplane must submit a report to the Authority within 72 hours of any incident that has endangered or may have endangered safe operation of a flight. 
Technical Defects 
A commander must ensure that all technical defects and occasions of exceeding technical limitations occurring while he was responsible for the flight are recorded in the aeroplane's technical log. 
Air Traffic Incidents 
A commander must submit an air traffic incident report in accordance with ICAO PANS ATM (sometimes referred to by its old name, PANS-RAC) whenever an aeroplane in flight has been endangered by a near collision with any other flying device, faulty air traffic procedures or lack of compliance with applicable procedures by air traffic services or by the flight crew or a failure of ATS facilities. 


Bird Hazards and Strikes 
Birds are a major hazard for aircraft, particularly when taking off and landing. Birds congregate naturally in areas where food is available, like rubbish sites, open sewage works and recently ploughed fields as well as their natural habitats. One of the measures adopted to reduce the number of birds on airfields has been to adopt a 'long grass' policy to discourage larger birds, others include using recorded bird distress calls or even hawks to drive them off. 
Birds congregate around rubbish sites 
Bird strikes can damage airframes but at landing and take-off speeds the greatest risk is to the engines. Ingested birds can cause structural damage and surges. A commander must immediately inform the appropriate ground station whenever a potential bird hazard is observed. 
ICAO publish the Airport Services Manual (ICAO DOC 9137 Part 3 Bird Control and Reduction) where information and recommendations on aerodrome bird control policy and statistics on bird strikes are published. 
Fina IPre pa rations 
Bird Hazards and Strikes continued 
Statistics suggest that 90% of bird strikes occur below 1500 ft (500 m), although there have been strikes recorded above 10 000 ft luckily these are very rare. Another study suggests the levels at greatest risk from birds is ground level to 150 m. 
Under EASA regulations, if there is a bird strike that results in significant damage, the Captain must file a written bird strike report after landing. Reporting of bird strikes will be done at a national level, and individual states are responsible for establishing a structure to deal with bird hazards and develop bird control policies. At an international level, the ICAO Bird strike Information System (IBIS) has been established to provide analyses of bird strike reports received from individual States. Some figures from this analysis are interesting: 
¦ 
70% of bird strikes occur during daylight 

¦ 
Some 55% of bird strikes occur during either the approach or 

take-off phases of flight 

¦ 
5% of bird strikes result in 'substantial' damage 

¦ 
51 % of bird strikes occur below 100 ft 

¦ 
In 92% of strikes, pilots were not warned of bird activity 




The last point leads on to the question -where do flight crew find information regarding bird hazards? The answer is, of course, several places: 
¦ 
NOTAMs (specific airport hazards, breeding areas, migration 

routes) 


¦ 
ATIS (updated and current airport information) 

¦ 
ATC/crew reports 

¦ 
BIRDTAMs (these exist -but use of them varies widely and depends on individual States. In general, not in wide useage 

¦ 
Airport plates and charts (again, the detail varies widely with the State, the hazard and the chart concerned) 

¦ 
AIP ENR 5.6 contains information regarding bird migrations 

¦ 
Significant data regarding bird strikes is contained in ICAO DOC 9137 Airport Services Manual.

If an in-flight emergency occurs and the situation permits, a commander must inform the appropriate air traffic services unit of any dangerous goods on board. 
Unlawful Interference 
Acts of unlawful interference with a flight include attempted or successful hijacks, unlawful interceptions and attempts to take control of or crash the aircraft. 
Following an act of unlawful interference on board an aeroplane a commander must submit a report as soon as practicable to the local Authority and the Authority in the State of the Operator.

Fina IPre pa rations 
Accident Reporting 
An operator must establish procedures to ensure that the nearest appropriate authority and the authority of the State of the Operator are notified. 
The commander, or in his absence the operator, must then submit a written report to the Authority of the State of the Operator within 72 hours. 


Emergency Violation of Local Regulations or Procedures 
If an emergency situation, which endangers the safety of the aeroplane or persons, necessitates the taking of an action that involves a violation of local regulations or procedures, the pilot-in­command must notify the appropriate local authority without delay. 
If required by the state in which the incident occurs, the pilot-in­command must submit a report on any such violation to the appropriate authority in that state or country. In that event the pilot-in-command must also submit a copy of the report to the State of Origin of the operator as soon as possible and normally within ten days. 
Notify without delay and notify in writing within 10 days

Lesson 70 
Approach and Landing 
ATPA OP8 70 
Approach and Landing 
Introduction 
The landing is a critical stage of flight where decisions need to be made quickly and correctly. This section examines the legality of making an approach in the first place, deciding to continue if the weather is bad and finally deciding to land or go-around. 
Legality 
The governing documentation here is the Operations Manual conforming to OPS all weather operations (OPS AWO) with, once again, ICAO regulations in the background. 


Definitions 
Circling. The visual phase of an instrument approach to bring an aircraft into position for landing on a runway which is not suitably located for a straight-in approach. 
Low Visibility Procedures (LVP). Procedures applied at an aerodrome for the purpose of ensuring safe operations during category II and Ill approaches and low visibility take-offs. 
Low Visibility Take-Off (LVTO). A take-off where the runway visual range (RVR) is lower than 400 m but not less than 75 m. 
Flight Control System. A system which includes an automatic landing system and/or a hybrid landing system. 
Fail-Passive Flight Control System. A flight control system is fail-passive if, in the event of a failure, there is no significant out­of-trim condition or deviation of flight path or attitude but the landing is not completed automatically. A fail-passive automatic flight control system disengages the autopilots after a failure. 
Fail-Operational Flight Control System. Aflight control system is fail-operational if, in the event of a failure below alert height, the approach, flare and landing, can be completed automatically. 
Approach and Landing 
Fail-Operational Hybrid Landing System. A system which consists of a primary fail-passive automatic landing system and a secondary independent guidance system enabling the pilot to complete a landing manually after failure of the primary system. 
A typical secondary independent guidance system would be a monitored head-up display with command or situation information. 
Visual Approach. An approach when either part or all of an instrument approach procedure is not completed and the approach is executed with visual reference to the terrain. 
Precision approach. An instrument approach and landing using precision lateral and vertical guidance. 
Non-precision approach. An instrument approach and landing which utilizes lateral guidance but does not utilize vertical guidance. 
CDFA. The Constant Descent Final Approach technique for non­precision approaches where a nominal rate of descent is set to maintain a continuous approach. 
DA/DH. Decision Altitude or Height, the altitude or height on a precision approach (or a non-precision approach using the CDFA technique )at which a missed approach must be initiated if the required visual reference to continue the approach has not been established. Note that initiating a missed approach at DH inevitablv means the aircraft will descend below DH. 


MDA/MDH. A specified altitude or height in a non-precision approach or circling approach below which descent must not be made without the required visual reference. Note that a missed approach from level flight at MOH will not result in the aircraft descending below MOH. 
OCA/OCH. The lowest altitude or height above the elevation of the relevant runway threshold or theaerodrome elevation as applicable, used in establishing compliance with appropriate obstacle clearance criteria. 
DME:  distance measuring equipment  
GNSS:  global navigation satellite system  
ILS:  instrument landing system  
LNAV:  lateral navigation  
LOC:  localiser  
LPV:  localiser performance with vertical guidance  
SBAS:  satellite-based augmentation system  
SRA:  surveillance radar approach  
VDF:  VHF direction finder  
VNAV:  vertical navigation  
VOR:  VHF omnidirectional radio range.  

Approach and Landing 
Aeroplane Categories 
Aircraft are divided into five categories depending on their target threshold speed (VAT or VREF)-VAT is found by taking either the stalling speed or minimum steady flight speed in the landing configuration (Vso) and multiplying it by 1.3 or by taking the slightly higher Vs1G, the stalling speed where the aircraft can generate lift equal to its weight, and multiplying it by 1.23. 
Aircraft approach categories are determined by threshold speed 
Both calculations are run in the landing configuration at the maximum certificated landing mass. Generally, the resulting values of VAT are very similar but, if both Vso and V81 Gare available, the higher resulting VAT is be used. 


The landing configuration that is to be taken into consideration must be specified in the operations manual. 
The operator may apply a lower landing mass for determining the VAT if approved by the competent authority. Such a lower landing mass shall be a permanent value, independent of the changing conditions of day-to-day operations. 
A  Less than 91 KT  
B  From 91 KT to 120 KT  
C  From 121 KT to 140 KT  
D  From 141 KT to 165 KT  
E  From 166 KT to 210 KT  

Approach and Landing 
The actual minimum landing RVR for a particular CAT I, APVaor NPA approach depends on a number of variables, and EASA regulations lists several pages worth of minima, conditions and equipment criteria that are considered. One of the first criteria that needs to be considered is the airport approach light system (ALS) and this is described as full (FALS), intermediate (IALS), basic (BALS) or none (NALS). In each case, where lights are specified, they must be on for the RVR minima to apply. 
A full approach light system (FALS) consists of 720 m or more of high intensity approach light system (HIALS), runway edge lights, threshold lights and runway end lights. This corresponds to an ICAO precision approach CAT 1 lighting system. 
An intermediate approach light system (IALS) comprises up to 420 -719 m of HIALS, runway edge lights, threshold lights and runway end lights. This corresponds to an ICAO simple approach lighting system 
A basic approach light system (BALS) consists of any other approach light system (HIALS, medium intensity approach light system (MIALS) or other ALS of up to 210 m -419 m) approach lights, runway edge lights, threshold lights and runway end lights. 
No approach light system (NALS) consists of any other approach lighting system (HIALS, MIALS or ALS extending to less than 210 
m) or no approach lights. 


For night operations at least runway edge, threshold and runway end lights must be on. 
Other variables considered in establishing minimum RVR for an approach include: 
¦ 
glide path approach angle 

¦ 
final approach track offset from runway centerline 

¦ 
location of final approach fix and means of locating 

¦ 
availability of runway touchdown zone lights (RTZL) and runway centre line lights (RCLL), spacing of RCLL 

¦ 
use of head-up-display (HUD), HUD landing system (HUDLS) and 

¦ 
use of an enhanced vision system (EVS). (An electronic means of displaying a real-time image of the external scene through the use of imaging sensors.) 


The detail in the regulations is quite involved, there are some basic rules and then any number of special cases and exceptions. In reality, of course, the Operations Manual will make the rules 
and minimums to be used quite clear, so once you are operating 'on line', things will become relatively straight forward. 
Approach and Landing 
Starting and Continuing an Approach 
EASA allows the Captain or operating pilot to begin an instrument approach regardless of the reported RVR or prevailing visibility. The approach cannot be continued, however, below 1000 ft above the aerodrome if the reported RVR or visibility is less than the applicable approach minima. 
In the (very unusual) case where the DA/H or MDA/H is more than 1000 ft above the aerodrome the approach cannot be continued into the final approach segment if the RVR is below minima. 
If the RVR is below minimum the approach may not be continued beyond 1000 ft above the aerodrome 
However, if after passing 1000 ft above the aerodrome the reported RVR/visibility falls below the minimum the approach may be continued to DA/Haor MDA/H. 
Once you get to DA/H or MDA/H the landing may be completed provided that the required visual reference is established at the DA/Haor MDA/H and is maintained. If the required visual reference is not available or maintained a go-around is required. 


Converted Met Visibility and RVR 
Where RVR is not available for landing equivalent values can be found by converting the prevailing met visibility to converted meteorological visibility (CMV) using the table. 
Runway lighting  CMV = Met visibility multiplied by:  
Day Night  
HI Approach and Runway Lighting  1.5 2.0  
Any other lighting  1.0 1.5  
No lighting  1.0  Not applicable  

This table may not be used to assess take-off RVR, only landing RVR, and it cannot be used for calculating required RVR minimum less than 800 m, or when the RVR is actually reported. 
RVR is the same as met visibility or greater, it can never be less 
Approach and Landing 
Landing Minima 
The approach may be visual, may be a non-precision approach, it may be a circling approach flown to break off and land on another runway or it may be a precision approach. 
Precision approaches with decision heights below 200ft fall into a special category of Low Visibility Operations (LVOs) which will be considered separately. 
Landing minima for instrument approaches are expressed as an MDA/H or DA/H as appropriate with an associated RVR/CMV. 


Visual Approaches 
This is an approach when either part or all of an instrument approach procedure is not completed and the approach is executed with visual reference to the terrain The minimum RVR is 800 m for a visual approach. 
Approach and Landing 
Non-Precision Approaches 
A non-precision approach (NPA) operation is an instrument approach using any of the facilities described here, with a MOH or DH not lower than 250 ft and an RVR/CMV of not less than 750 m, unless accepted by the Authority. 
NPAs are flown using the CDFA technique. For this technique a DH is calculated by adding a margin (commonly 50 ft) to the approach MOH. Not using the CDFA profile requires approval If the approach is flown with a level flight segment at or above MDA/H (i.e. not using a CDFA technique), 200 m should be added to the minimum RVR/CMVNIS for category A and B aeroplanes and 400 m for category C and D aeroplanes.

Approach with Vertical Guidance 
An Approach with Vertical Guidance (APV) operation is an instrument approach which utilises lateral and vertical guidance, but does not meet the requirements established for precision approach and landing operations, with a DH not lower than 250 ft and a runway visual range of not less than 600 m unless approved by the Authority. 
APVs may be currently divided into two types: 
¦ 
those that use vertical guidance provided by a path derived by the bare-altimeter and the flight management systems and 

¦ 
those where the vertical guidance is provided by a GNSS (global navigation satellite system) augmentation system such as SBAS (satellite based augmentation system) or GBAS (ground based augmentation system). 


Category I Precision Approaches 
A Category I, or CAT I, approach operation is a precision instrument approach and landing using ILS, MLS, GLS (global positioning landing system, such as GNSS/GBAS) or PAR with a decision height not lower than 200 ft and with an RVR not less than 550 m, unless accepted by the Authority. 
Approach and Landing 
Low Visibility Operations 
Low visibility operations (LVO) and the associated low visibility procedures (LVP) were first developed commercially on the Trident and Belfast aircraft to increase the probability of landing, particularly in the bad fogs and smog of the 1950s and 1960s in the Thames Valley near London. 
The reduction in air pollution since then has improved visibility but it was quickly realised that an aircraft that could operate in all visibility conditions was very unlikely to have to divert, a major consideration for a tightly run scheduled operation. Low visibility operations are now widely used at international and some national airfields where the weather factor can be marginal. 
LVO rules cover low visibility take-off (LVTO) operations and precision approaches below CAT I minima, specifically: 
¦ 
lower than standard category I (LTS CAT I) operations 

¦ 
standard category II (CAT II) operations 

¦ other than standard category II (OTS CAT II) operations 

¦ 
standard category Ill (CAT Ill) operations 

¦ 
approach operations utilising enhanced vision systems (EVS). 




You will note the 'other than standard' terminology. Additional equipment on the aircraft may allow these clearances. An aeroplane with a head-up-display (HUD), head-up-display landing system (HUDLS), or an enhanced vision system (EVS) may be allowed lower than standard minima for a particular approach. 
Approach and Landing 
LVO General Rules 
The operator shall not use an aerodrome for LVOs below a visibility of 800 m unless: 
¦ 
the aerodrome has been approved for such operations by the State of the aerodrome and 

¦ 
low visibility procedures (LVP) have been established. 


If the operator selects an aerodrome where the term LVP is not used, the operator shall ensure that there are equivalent procedures that adhere to the requirements of LVP at the aerodrome. This situation shall be clearly noted in the operations manual or procedures manual including guidance to the flight crew on how to determine that the equivalent LVP are in effect. 
An operator must establish procedures and instructions to be used for Low Visibility Operations. These procedures must be included in the Operations Manual, be consequently approved by the Authority, and contain the duties of flight crew members during taxying, take-off, approach, flare, landing, roll-out and missed approach as appropriate. 


An autopilot system with a rollout or roll-out capability is one capable of guidance on the runway centre line after landing. 
Particular emphasis must be placed on flight crew responsibilities during transition from non-visual conditions to visual conditions, and on the procedures to be used in deteriorating visibility or when failures occur. The distribution of flight deck duties also needs to be very clear with DHs below 200 ft, who is looking where, who is saying what, and exactly who is responsible for what is critical. 
The operator shall only conduct CAT II or CAT Ill operations if: 
¦ 
each aircraft concerned is certified for operations with a decision height (DH) below 200 ft, or no DH, and equipped in accordance with the applicable airworthiness requirements 

¦ 
a system for recording approach and/or automatic landing success and failure is established and maintained to monitor the overall safety of the operation 

¦ 
the flight crew consists of at least two pilots. 


Approach and Landing 
LVO continued 
The instructions in the Operations Manual must be compatible with procedures in the Aeroplane Flight Manual and cover the following items in particular: 
¦ 
checks for the satisfactory functioning of the aeroplane equipment, both before departure and in flight 

¦ 
effect on minima caused by changes in the status of the ground installations and airborne equipment 

¦ 
procedures for the take-off, approach, flare, landing, roll-out and missed approach 

¦ 
procedures to be followed in the event of failures, warnings 

and other non-normal situations 

¦ 
the minimum visual reference required 

¦ 
the importance of correct seating and eye position 

¦ 
action which may be necessary arising from a deterioration of the visual references 

¦ 
allocation of crew duties to allow the commander to devote himself mainly to supervision and decision making 




¦ 
the requirement for all height calls below 200 ft to be based on the radio altimeter and for one pilot to continue to monitor the aeroplane instruments until the landing is completed 

¦ 
the requirement for the localiser sensitive area to be protected 

¦ 
the use of information relating to wind velocity, wind shear, turbulence, runway contamination and use of multiple RVR assessments 

¦ 
procedures to be used for practice approaches and landing on runways at which the full Category 11 or Category 111 aerodrome procedures are not in force 

¦ operating limitations resulting from airworthiness certification and 

¦ 
information on the maximum deviation allowed from the ILS glide path and/or localiser. 


Approach and Landing 
LVO continued 
The operator is also responsible for training crews in low visibility operations. They must be simulator checked and qualified for each operation. This training is type specific and all flight crew require qualification. 
The Operations Manual must list the minimum equipment that has to be serviceable at the start of a low visibility take-off or a category II or Ill approach. 
The aerodrome must be approved for CAT II or Ill operations by its own authority and the operator must establish that low vis procedures are in place. 
It is up to the commander to satisfy himself that the aeroplane, the crew and the airport all conform to the requirements before conducting low visibility operations. If any one does not, then low visibility operations are not permitted. 


Approach and Landing 
Category II Approaches 
A category II operation is a precision instrument approach and landing using ILS or MLS with a decision height below 200 ft but not lower than 100 ft and an RVR of not less than 300 m. 
To continue an approach below the category II decision height the pilot must have the required visual references. The required visual reference is defined as three centre line, approach or runway edge lights and one crossbar or threshold lighting. 
The image opposite demonstrates CAT I or II minimum visual reference. 


Approach and Landing 
Category II Approaches continued 
The minimum RVR for CAT II approaches is shown below. These minima assume an autopilot approach to at least DH. It is worth noting that, for some aircraft, although the decision to land is made at not less than 100 ft, the autopilots may stay engaged below that height, typically to 50 ft or 60 ft.
