CAT Ill operations have decision heights less than 100 ft and on down to Oaft. The CAT Ill minima depend on the autopilot capabilities. Fail operational, as discussed earlier, means the capability to lose an autopilot channel and safely continue the autoland, fail passive means manual reversion and go-around. 
Category I I IC has a minimum decision height of zero and a minium RVR of zero. Currently this category is not used -mostly because it is not required -and it is not mentioned in EASA regulations. 
Approach and Landing 
EASA Low Visibility Operations 
EASA updated the All-weather Operations (AWO), in October 2022 but have yet to create new FCL Learning objectives (LO). This brief covers the areas of the new regulations covered by the present LO including new elements yet to be examined. 
Low Visibility Operations 
Low visibility operations (LVO) are put into place when the runway visual range (RVR) is less than 550 m and associated Low visibility Procedures (LVP) are aerodrome procedures that are put in place when ATC are not always able to maintain full visual control of the Movement Area. LVP include restricting or banning vehicles from the movement area or positioning vehicles at entrances to the live runway to prevent incursions. LVPs are required: 
(a) 
For low-visibility flight approach operations. 

(b) 
For LVTOs with RVR less than 400 m. 


If an operator selects an aerodrome with equivalent procedures, where the term 'LVPs' is not used (e.g. regional procedures), the operator should verify that suitable procedures are established to ensure an equivalent level of safety to that achieved at approved aerodromes. 


This situation should be clearly noted in the operations manual or procedures manual, including guidance to the flight crew on how to determine that the suitable procedures are in effect at the time of an actual operation. 
Note: the AFM may state that some elements of LVPs are not required and therefore the equivalent level of safety may be established on that basis. 
LVO rules cover Low Visibility Take-off (LVTO) operations. Take­off operations are classified as 'normal take-off operations' with an RVR at or above 550 m and 'LVTO operations' with an RVR below 550 m. Only LVTO operations in an RVR of less than 400 m require a specific approval. 
The operator shall conduct the following operations only if they are approved by the competent authority: 
(a) 
Take-off operations with visibility conditions of less than 400 mRVR 

(b) 
instrument approach operations in low-visibility conditions and 

(c) 
operations with operational credits, except for EFVS 200 operations, which are not subject to specific approval. 


Approach and Landing 
Specific Approval Criteria 
To obtain a specific approval the operator shall demonstrate that: 
¦ 
For low-visibility approach operations, LVTO operations in an RVR less than 125 m, and operations with operational credits, the aircraft has been certified for the intended operations. 

¦ 
The flight crew members are competent to conduct the intended operation and a training and checking programme for the flight crew members and relevant personnel involved in the flight preparation has been established. 

¦ 
Operating procedures for the intended operations have been established. 

¦ 
Any relevant changes to the minimum equipment list (MEL) have been made. 

¦ 
Any relevant changes to the maintenance programme have been made. 

¦ 
Procedures have been established to ensure the suitability of aerodromes, including instrument flight procedures, for the intended operations. 

¦ 
For the intended operations, a safety assessment has been carried out, and performance indicators have been established to monitor the level of safety. 




Aircraft Certification -Equipment Eligible for LVTO in an RVR Less Than 125 m 
Systems that are used to qualify for take-off in an RVR less than 125 m typically allow the pilot to use the external visual cues as well as instrumented guidance to track the runway centre line. The kind of systems in use today include para-visual display (PVD) and HUD. It is expected that EFVSs will be certified for take-off guidance in the future. Where the PVD or HUD uses an ILS localiser signal as reference, the ILS sensitive area must be protected by the LVPs at the aerodrome. 
Approach and Landing 
Low-Visibility Operations and Operations with Operational Credits 
The different types of approach and landing operations are classified according to the lowest DH (or MOH) and RVR applicable to the approach type. 
The classification of approach types does not depend on the technology used for the approach. The lowest minima specified do not take account of 'operational credits' that may allow for lower operating minima. 
The classification does not subdivide CAT Ill operations into CAT IIIA, 11IB, and IIIC. The actual minima applicable to any operation depends on the aircraft equipment and the specific LVO approval held by the air operator. 
The AFM for aircraft certified for CAT Ill operations will state the lowest usable DH, or no DH. Some AF Ms may refer to the previous ICAO classifications as follows: 
¦ 
CAT IIIA: a DH lower than 30 m (100 ft) or no DH and an RVR not less than 175 m 

¦ 
CAT 11IB: a DH lower than 15 m (50 ft) or no DH and an RVR less than 175 m but not less than 50 m and 

¦ 
CAT IIIC: no DH and no RVR limitations. (NOT used in Europe). 

¦ 
The minimum RVR in the EU regulations is 75 m. 




For each specific class of standard take-off or approach operations, a standard combination of airborne equipment, aerodrome infrastructure and equipment, and procedures (system components) needs to be available to ensure the required performance of the total system. 
In real-life operations, one or more system components may exceed the required standard performance this enhanced performance can be used to provide operational flexibility beyond the limits of standard operations. In certain circumstances it may be possible to achieve the required system performance without some standard items being available by using other enhanced equipment or procedures. 
Another application of operational credits is to use the enhanced performance of certain system components to allow operations to lower than the standard minima. 
For approach operations, an operational credit can be applied to the instrument or the visual segment or both. 
Where an operational credit allows operation to lower-than­standard minima, this is not considered a separate approach classification 
Approach and Landing 
Operating Procedures -General 
The Operator 
The operator shall establish procedures and instructions for all types of LVOs and operations with operational credits for which an operator is seeking approval. 
a. Operating procedures should be established for all types of LVOs and operations with operational credits for which an operator is seeking approval. The operating procedures should: 
1. 
Be consistent with the AFM. 

2. 
Be appropriate to the technology and equipment to be used. 

3. 
Specify the duties and responsibilities of each flight crew member in each relevant phase of flight. 

4. 
Ensure that flight crew workload is managed to facilitate effective decision-making and monitoring of the aircraft and: 

5. 
Minimise, as much as practical, the deviation from normal procedures used for routine operations (non­LVOs ). 




(b) Operating procedures should include: 
1. 
The required checks for the satisfactory functioning of the aircraft equipment, both before departure and in flight. 

2. 
The correct seating and eye position. 

3. 
Determination of aerodrome operating minima. 

4. 
The increment to be added to minima for use by pilots­in-command/commanders who are new to the aircraft type, if applicable. 

5. 
The effect on aerodrome operating minima of temporarily failed or downgraded ground equipment. 

6. 
The effect on aerodrome operating minima of the failure or change of the status of any aircraft systems. 

7. 
When the LVPs at the aerodrome are required. 


LVPs are required: 
(i) 
for low-visibility flight approach operations. 

(ii) 
for LVTOs with RVR less than 400 m.

This situation should be clearly noted in the operations manual or procedures manual, including guidance to the flight crew on how to determine that the suitable procedures are in effect at the time of an actual operation.

Approach and Landing 
Low-Visibility Take-Off (LVTO) Operations Aeroplanes in an RVR of Less Than 400 m 
The RVR required for multi-engined aeroplanes which, in the event of a critical engine failure at any point during take-off, can either stop or continue the take-off to a height of 1500 ft above the aerodrome while clearing obstacles by the required margins, the criteria in Table 1 should apply: 
Table 1: LVTO operations with aeroplanes -RVR versus facilities 
Minimum RVR  Facilities  
300m(day)  Centre line markings; and Runway edge lights.  
300m(night)  Centre line markings; and Runway edge lights; and Runway end lights or centre line lights.  
150 m  Centre line markings; and Runway end lights or centre line lights; and Runway edge lights; and Runway centre line lights.  
125m  Centre line markings; and Runway end lights or centre line lights; and Runway edge lights (spaced 60 mor less); and Runway centre line lights (spaced 15morless).  



For engined aeroplanes not complying with those conditions, there may be a need to land immediately and to see and avoid obstacles. Such aeroplanes may be operated to the take-off minima shown in Table 2 and the marking and lighting criteria shown in Table 1, provided that they are able to comply with the applicable obstacle clearance criteria, assuming engine failure at the height specified: 
Table 2: LVTO operations with aeroplanes -assumed engine failure height versus RVR 
Assumed engine failure height above the take-off runway (ft)  versus RVR (m)  
Less than 50  Not less than 200  
More than 50 but less than 100  Not less than 300  

The reported RVR value representative of the initial part of the take-off run can be replaced by pilot assessment. 
The minimum RVR value specified in Table 1 or 2 should be achieved for all reporting points representative of the parts of the runway from the point at which the aircraft commences the take­off until the calculated accelerate-stop distance from that point. 
Approach and Landing 
LVTO Operations with an RVR Less Than 125 m 
For LVTO operations with an RVR of less than 125 m, the following additional elements should apply: 
1. 
The runway has centre line lights spaced at intervals of 15 m or less. 

2. 
If an ILS signal is used for lateral guidance, the ILS localiser signal meets the requirements for category Ill operations, unless otherwise stated in the AFM. 

3. 
If an ILS signal is to be used, low-visibility procedures (LVPs) include protection of the runway and, where an ILS localiser signal is used, it should include protection of the ILS sensitive area unless otherwise stated in the 


AFM. 
4. If a GLS signal is used for lateral guidance, the GLS performance type meets the requirements for category 111 operations (GAST D and to GBAS point to which guidance is required), unless otherwise stated in the 
AFM. 
For LVTO operations with an RVR of less than 125 m, the reported RVR should be not less than the minimum specified in the AFM or, if no such minimum is specified, not less than 75 m. 


The minimum required RVR should be achieved for all reporting points representative of the parts of the runway from the point at which the aircraft commences the take-off until the greater of the calculated take-off distance or accelerate-stop distance from that point. 
The reported RVR value representative of the initial part of the take-off run can be replaced by pilot assessment. The value of 125 m RVR for take-off with 15 m centre line light spacing has been selected because flight deck geometry means that this will provide at least a 90 m visual segment for most aircraft types. In a 90 m visual segment the pilot is expected to be able to see six centre line light intervals (seven centre line lights) at 15 m spacing once lined up on the runway centre line.

Operations with Operational Credits 
SPECIAL AUTHORISATION CATEGORY I (SA CAT I) 
OPERATIONS SA CAT I is an operational credit that exploits a navigation solution with superior performance to that required for standard CAT I by extending the instrument segment of CAT I approach operations. 
This navigation solution may be an ILS installation with the necessary performance coupled to a suitably certified auto-land system or a HUD or equivalent display system or SVGS. The extended instrument segment means that the DH can be reduced from the standard minimum of 200 ft down to 150 ft. The lower DH allows a corresponding reduction in the RVR required for the approach. 
The approach should be flown using a certified system as identified in the AFM. 
The DH should be determined by the use of a radio altimeter or other device capable of providing equivalent performance, if so determined by the aircraft certification process.

Approach and Landing 

Operations with Operational Credits -EFVS Operations 
(a) 
EFVS operations, if approved, exploit the improved visibility provided by the EFVS to allow an operational credit applied to the visual segment of an instrument approach. An EFVS cannot be used to extend the instrument segment of an approach and thus the DH for operation with an EFVS is always the same as for the same approach conducted without an operational credit. 

(b) 
EFVS operations require specific approval from the competent authority in accordance with Part-SPA. However, other EFVS operations may be conducted by operators and without a specific approval if specifically covered in accordance with Part-CAT, Part-NCC or Part-SPO (e.g. 'EFVS 200'). 

(c) 
Equipment for EFVS operations: 


1. In order to conduct EFVS operations, a certified EFVS is used. An EFVS is an enhanced vision system (EVS) that also incorporates a flight guidance system and displays the image on a HUD or an equivalent display. The flight guidance system will incorporate aircraft flight information and flight symbology. 


Approach and Landing 
Crew Training and Competence 
(a) 
The operator shall ensure that the flight crew is competent to conduct the intended operations. 

(b) 
The operator shall ensure that each flight crew member successfully completes training and checking for all types of LVOs and operations with operational credits for which an approval has been granted. Such training and checking shall: 

1. 
Include initial and recurrent training and checking. 

2. 
Include normal, abnormal and emergency procedures. 

3. 
Be tailored to the type of technologies used in the intended operations and 

4. 
Take into account the human factor risks associated with the intended operations. 



(c) 
The operator shall keep records of the training and qualifications of the flight crew members. 

(d) 
The training and checking shall be conducted by appropriately qualified personnel. 




Experience in Type or Class or as Pilot-in-Command I Commander 
To ensure that the flight crew is competent to conduct the intended operations, the operator should assess the risks associated with the conduct of low-visibility approach operations by pilots new to the aircraft type or class and take the necessary mitigations. 
Where such mitigations include an increment to the visibility or RVR for LVOs, this should be stated in the operations manual 
Approach and Landing 
Recent Experience for SA CAT I, CAT II, SA CAT II and CAT Ill Approach Operation 
Pilots authorised to conduct low-visibility approach operations or operations with operational credits should complete at least two approaches using the operator's procedures for low-visibility approach operations or operations with operational credits, during the validity period of each operator proficiency check or periodic demonstration of competence, unless credits related to recent experience when operating more than one type are defined in the operational suitability data. 
If the operator is approved for more than one piece of aircraft equipment used (e.g. auto-land, HUD, auto-coupled approach with manual landing, SVGS, etc.), pilots should complete at least one additional approach in the lowest approved RVR (either to go­around or landing) for each piece of aircraft equipment used during the validity period of each operator proficiency check or periodic demonstration of competence (e.g. two approaches CAT II with auto-land and one CAT II with auto-coupled to below DH with manual landing, two CAT II auto-land and one CAT II HUD to below DH with manual landing or vice versa) unless credits related to recent experience when operating more than one type are defined in the operational suitability data. 


Pilots authorised to conduct low-visibility approach operations or operations with operational credits using HUD LS or equivalent display systems to touchdown should complete two approaches 
(e.g. an operator approved for CAT 11/111 HUDLS will do two CAT Ill HUDLS other examples would be two CAT Ill auto-land and two CAT Ill HUDLS to touchdown, two SA CAT II auto-land and two SA CAT II HUDLS, or when combining several LVOs and equipment, two CAT Ill auto-land and one CAT II auto-coupled to below DH with manual landing and two CAT Ill HUDLS to touchdown) using the operator's procedures for low-visibility approach operations or operations with operational credits using HUDLS, during the validity period of each operator proficiency check or periodic demonstration of competence unless credits related to recent experience when operating more than one type are defined in the operational suitability data. 
If a flight crew member is authorised to operate as pilot flying and pilot monitoring, the flight crew member should complete the required number of approaches in each operating capacity. 
Approach and Landing 
Recent Experience for EFVS Operations 
To be considered competent to conduct EFVS operations: 
(a) 
Pilots should complete a minimum of two approaches on each type of aircraft operated using the operator's procedures for EFVS operations during the validity period of each operator proficiency check or periodic demonstration of competence unless credits related to recent experience when operating more than one type are defined in the operational suitability data. When the operator is approved for both EFVS-L and EFVS-A (see definitions below), a minimum of one approach in each EFVS operation should be completed. 

(b) 
If a flight crew member is authorised to operate as pilot flying and pilot monitoring during EFVS operations, the flight crew member should complete the required number of approaches in each operating capacity. 




Definitions 
'Circling' means the visual phase of an instrument approach to bring an aircraft into position for landing on a runway/FATO that is not suitably located for a straight-in approach. 
EFVS An enhanced flight vision system (EFVS) uses an electronic means to provide a real-time display of the forward external scene topography using imaging sensors. 
EFVS-A systems may be used for approach operations down to a height of 100 ft above the runway. 
EFVS-L systems may be used all the way to landing EFVS-L systems will additionally provide flare cueing and height information from a radio altimeter. 
'Enhanced vision system (EVS)' means a system to display electronic real-time images of the external scene achieved using imaging sensors and displayed on a HUD (or equivalent). The system enhances the pilot's ability to detect and identify the required visual references for landing in order to gain an operational credit and descend below the decision altitude (DA) I decision height (DH) or minimum descent altitude (MDA). 
Approach and Landing 
Definitions continued 
'Fail-passive flight control system': a flight control system is fail­passive if, in the event of a failure, there is no significant out-of­trim condition or deviation of flight path or attitude, but the landing is not completed automatically. For a fail-passive automatic flight control system the pilot assumes control of the aeroplane after a failure. 
'Fail-operational flight control system' means a flight control system with which, in the event of a failure below alert height, the approach, flare and landing can be completed automatically. In the event of a failure, the automatic landing system will operate as a fail-passive system. 
'Fail-operational hybrid landing system' means a system that consists of a primary fail-passive automatic landing system and a secondary independent guidance system enabling the pilot to complete a landing manually after failure of the primary system. 
'Flight control system' in the context of low visibility operations means a system that includes an automatic landing system and/or a hybrid landing system. 


'Low visibility procedures (LVP)' means procedures applied at an aerodrome for the purpose of ensuring safe operations during lower than standard category I, other than standard category 11, category II and Ill approaches and low visibility take-offs. 
Low visibility take-off (LVTO)' means a take-off with an RVR lower than 400 m but not less than 75 m. 
'Visual approach' means an approach when either part or all of an instrument approach procedure is not completed and the approach is executed with visual reference to the terrain. 
Approach and Landing 
Single Pilot Operations 
For single-pilot operations, the minimum RVRNIS should be calculated in accordance with the following additional criteria: 
¦ 
an RVR of less than 800 m may be used for CAT I approaches provided any of the following is used at least down to the applicable DH: 

¦ 
a suitable autopilot, coupled to an ILS, MLS or GLS that is not published as restricted or 

¦ 
an approved HUDLS, including, where appropriate, enhanced vision system (EVS), or equivalent approved system 



¦ 
where RTZL and/or RCLL are not available, the minimum RVR/CMV should not be less than 600 m and 

¦ 
an RVR of less than 800 m may be used for APV operations to runways with FALS, RTZL and RCLL when using an approved HUDLS, or equivalent approved system, or when conducting a coupled approach to a DH equal to or greater than 250 ft. 




Performance Implications on Landing 
Before commencing an approach to land, the commander must satisfy himself that conditions (weather/aerodrome/runway) should not prevent a safe approach, landing or missed approach, having regard to the performance information contained in the Operations Manual. 
The in-flight determination of the landing distance should be based on the latest available report, preferably not more than 30 minutes before the expected landing time. 
We met the ICAO performance restrictions earlier, they basically required an aircraft to be able to land safely in the distance available and imposed an additional climb gradient limit. 
EASA regulations are more detailed and split aircraft into performance Classes A, B and C where Class A is the most capable and contains jets and turboprops, Class B contains light pistons and Class C heavy piston aircraft. 
The regulations are reproduced in full here because questions are likely to use the wording of the original. 
Approach and Landing 
Class A Aircraft 

Landing Climb Limit 
An operator has to ensure that the landing mass of the aeroplane does not exceed the maximum landing climb limit mass specified for the altitude and the ambient temperature expected for the estimated time of landing at the destination and alternate aerodrome. 
Missed Approach Requirement 
For instrument approaches with a missed approach climb gradient greater than 2.5%, the operator should verify that the expected landing mass of the aeroplane allows for a missed approach with 
a climb gradient equal to or greater than the applicable missed approach gradient in the one engine inoperative missed approach configuration and at the associated speed. 

For instrument approaches with DH below 200 ft, the operator should verify that the expected landing mass of the aeroplane allows a missed approach gradient of climb, with the critical The minimum gradients of the normal landing climb limit apply engine failed and with the speed and configuration used for a missed approach of at least 2.5%, or the published gradient, whichever is greater.

Lesson ll 
ETOPS and Long Range Flights 
ATPA OP8 ll 
ETOPS and Long Range Flights 
Introduction 
The same regulations that apply to shorter trips also apply to long range flights but there are additional factors to consider during planning. The large distances travelled require careful fuel and performance planning, as does the scarcity of diversion airfields in some areas of the world. The lack of navigation aids such as VOR and DME creates a requirement for reliable long range navigation systems. 
Some remote areas such as the North Atlantic have compounded problems as they are not only extremely busy sections of airspace but also outside of the range of ground based radar. Special navigational and ATC procedures have been introduced in these areas. 


ETOPS and Long Range Flights 
Operators' Responsibilities 
EASA regulations places the overall responsibility squarely on the operator who must ensure that operations are only conducted along such routes or within such areas, for which: 
¦ 
Ground facilities and services, (including meteorological services), are provided which are adequate for the planned operation 

¦ 
The performance of the aeroplane intended to be used is adequate to comply with minimum flight altitude requirements 

¦ 
The equipment of the aeroplane intended to be used meets the minimum requirements for the planned operation 

¦ 
Appropriate maps and charts are available 

¦ 
If two-engined aeroplanes are used, adequate aerodromes are available within the specified time and distance limitations for extended twin engined operations (ETOPS) 

¦ 
If single-engine aeroplanes are used, surfaces are available 


which permit a safe forced landing to be executed. 
An operator shall ensure that operations are conducted in accordance with any restriction on the routes or the areas of operation, imposed by the Authority of the state being flown over. 


Aircraft with more than 30 passenger seats that fail to meet the ditching standards detailed in the applicable airworthiness code must not be more than 120 minutes cruise or 400 NM, whichever is lesser, from 'land suitable for making an emergency landing'. 
There are some additional ICAO requirements relating to documents to be carried on flights over water although these are covered anyway in other requirements. 
An aeroplane shall carry: 
¦ 
The Operations Manual or those parts of it that pertain to flight operations 

¦ 
The Flight Manual for the aeroplane, or other documents containing required performance data and any other information necessary for the operation of the aeroplane within the terms of its certificate of airworthiness 

¦ 
Current and suitable charts to cover the route of the proposed flight and any route along which it is reasonable to expect that the flight may be diverted. 


ETOPS and Long Range Flights 
The Regulations 
The restriction on routing on long range flights comes from the Regulatory Authorities, in this case EASA. This is broadly done by restricting how far an aircraft may operate from a adequate alternate and by ensuring the aircraft is operated at weights that would allow it to hold an adequate height if an engine fails. 
Loss of an engine is more critical on a twin than on a three or four engined aircraft so the regulations tend to be more restrictive and complex for twins. 
Relatively recently, regulators have relaxed the rules for twin engined aircraft to allow commercial operations across the North Atlantic and Pacific. The relaxation carries a large number of caveats, the operator has to demonstrate high levels of reliability in service to be awarded extended twin operations (ETOPS) approval. 


Net and Gross Performance 
Several of the following regulations refer to net or gross performance standards. 
Gross performance is the average performance you might expect, there is a 50:50 chance that you might have better than gross performance and a 50:50 chance that your performance will be worse. 
The net performance is the gross performance factored down so that, if you plan to net standards, there is only a remote probability that you will fail to meet them. 
Net performance is worse than gross 
ETOPS and Long Range Flights 
Long Range Twin Ops Without ETOPS 
If operating without ETOPS approval a twin engined aeroplane must not be operated further from an adequate aerodrome than the distances listed below. 
¦ Performance Class A aeroplanes of either a maximum operational passenger seating configuration (MOPSC) of 20 or more or a MTOM greater than 45 360 kg a distance equivalent to 60 minutes flight time flown at the One Engine Inoperative (OEI) cruising speed. 
Non ETOPs pert Class A twins operate to a 60 minute rule time 
¦ 
Performance Class A with a MOPSC of less than 20 and an MTOM less than 45 360 kg, a distance flown in 120 minutes, or if approved by the Authority, up to 180 minutes for turbo-jet aeroplanes.) 

¦ 
Performance Class B or C aeroplanes the lower of 300 NM or 


a distance equivalent to 120 minutes flight time. 
The operator shall determine a speed for the calculation of the maximum distance to an adequate aerodrome for each two­engined aeroplane type operated, not exceeding VMo (maximum operating speed) based upon the true airspeed that the aeroplane can maintain with one engine inoperative. 


The operator shall include the following data, specific to each type or variant, in the operations manual: 
¦ 
the determined OEI cruising speed and 

¦ 
the determined maximum distance from an adequate aerodrome.

Prior to conducting an ETOPS flight, an operator shall ensure that an adequate ETOPS en-route alternate is available, within either the operator's approved diversion time, or a diversion time based on the MEL generated serviceability status of the aeroplane, whichever is shorter. 
An operator must ensure that the following data, specific to each type is included in the Operations Manual: 
¦ 
the one-engine-inoperative cruise speed and 

¦ 
the maximum distance from an adequate aerodrome determined in accordance with the rules 


The speeds specified above are only intended to be used for establishing the maximum distance from an adequate aerodrome. 


En-Route Performance Limitations 
The ICAO performance restrictions basically required an aircraft to be able to operate above MSA and land safely in the event of an engine failure. EASA regulations are, once again, more detailed. 
The en-route requirements refer to the ability to maintain at least MSA in the event of an engine failure. If an engine fails over the sea there is unlikely to be an issue with MSA. If it occurs over land the aircraft may use a drift-down to clear mountains. When working out the clearance it is an obvious advantage to assume the aircraft starts off very high. The regulations prevent this idea being abused by imposing a maximum height you can assume to be at, a performance ceiling, in Classes Baand C.

Lesson 72 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
ATPA OPB 72 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Introduction 
Much of the world's air traffic travels across the poles and the oceans. This chapter considers the general problems of long range and polar navigation and the specific procedures for the North Atlantic (NAT) region. 
Long Range and Polar Navigation 
Compass and DI 
Until the introduction of the first long range navigation equipment 
in the 1950s aircraft navigated primarily by magnetic compass and gyroscopes. Early magnetic compasses relied on a compass needle or card suspended in fluid, they can still be found on light aircraft and as standby compasses on larger aircraft. 
These compasses are unreliable in a turn and are therefore usually supplemented by a direction indicator, a DI. The DI is based on a gyroscope which keeps its heading reference over the short term and is therefore reliable in turns. 
Unfortunately the DI does not keep its heading reference over longer periods and needs to be intermittently caged and synchronised to compass heading when in level flight. 


Part of the reason for the longer term errors of the DI is that a gyroscope holds its position in space. Even though the aircraft may be stationary on the ground it is not holding a constant position in space because the Earth's surface is rotating. 
This error is called earth rate or astronomic precession and can be corrected for by a formula which is: 
Earth Rate = 15 x sine latitude 
... in degrees per hour. This correction can be built into a DI by means of a latitude nut which is adjusted to produce an equivalent opposite error in the DI and cancel the effect of the Earth rate. 
The second source of long term errors in the DI occurs if the aircraft is moved east or west. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Compass and DI continued 
The image opposite shows two gyroscopes, one at the equator and one further north, each with their spin axis initially aligned with true north on the left of the picture. Even if the Earth was completely stationary as the gyros are moved from left to right the one away from the equator loses its alignment. Depending on the direction of movement this transport wander will add to or act against earth rate. 
The magnitude of Transport Wander will equal the magnitude of Convergency. 
Therefore, an A/C flying along the Equator will experience ZERO Transport Wander. 
If flying exactly East or West on any other parallel: 
Transport Wandera= Groundspeed Kt x Tan of Latitude/ 60 deg/hr 
Any other track: 
Transport Wandera= d long X Sin mean Lata/ Flight Time 

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Calculating Transport Wander 
Example: 
An aircraft travels from 60°N 010°W to 60°N 040°W. Find the transport wander on a DI. 
Solution: 
Transport wander = degrees x sin lat 
= 30 x sin 60° 
26° 
= 
The final error source is the imperfection of the gyro itself, this will lead to real wander. The chances are that all the questions you get will assume that the real wander is zero, often by describing the DI as a 'perfect gyro'. 
We can now say that the total rate of wander is 
Real wandera+ Earth Ratea+ Latitude nuta+ Transport Wander 
The Earth rate will act in different directions in the northern and southern hemispheres, the transport wander will act in different directions depending on direction of travel and the latitude nut should oppose earth rate. 


Because the errors can be applied in different directions we must be careful with plus and minus signs when attempting any calculations. The error rates are applied according to the table below. 
Northern Hemisphere  Southern Hemisphere  
Earth's Rotation  - +  
Latitude Nut  +  - 
Transport W. East  - +  
Transport W. West  +  - 

You may be asked to calculate total gyro error. The example on the following page shows how it is done. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Calculating Total Gyro Wander 
Example: 
A perfect DI has its latitude nut set for 60N. The aircraft flies for 90 minutes on a track of 270a° T at Lat 50N. TAS is 300 KT, with a wind of 090/60 KT. At the end of the flight, what is the total DI error? 
Solution: 
This is a perfect DI, so RW = 0 
ER = -15 x sin LAT dg/hr 
LN = +15 x sin LAT NUT deg/hr TW = +[E/W GS (360) x tan lat] + 60 deg/hr 
Total error rate = RW + ER + LN + TW 
= 0 -(15Sin50] + (15sin60] + [6Tan50] = -(11.49] + [12.99] + [7.15] 
= +8.65 degrees per hour 
for 90 min flight the total error is: 
+8.65 x 1.5 = +12.975 ° or roughly 13 ° 


Notice that both transport wander and earth rate get greater nearer the poles. This makes the conventional direct reading compass and DI system unreliable in these areas. 
One final thought so far as exam questions are concerned, they always seem to assume a 'perfect gyro' (no real wander), and the latitude nut (or rate correction) is either set correctly (it compensates perfectly for Earth rate error), or the question will tell you there is no rate correction device, in which case you must calculate the earth rate error. 
An additional factor is the very rapid change in variation near the Earth's magnetic pole and, when very near to the magnetic pole, there is an area where the horizontal component of the Earth's magnetic field is so low that magnetic compasses cease to work reliably. This is called the 'six micro tesla zone'. 
The area near the north pole where compasses are unreliable is called the six micro tesla zone 

Near the magnetic pole, in Northern Canada, several airways 
have VORs aligned to true north rather than magnetic north as it is recognised that conventional compass systems cannot be used. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Gyro Compasses 

A part solution to this problem is to use a more modern compass system called a gyro compass which combines a gyroscope and a Latitude compass system to use the best bits of each. The compass nut detectors are less sensitive to turning errors and the gyro system 
is automatically synchronised to the compass system continuously removing the gyro errors we discussed above. 
Variation can be fed into the system using either a three dimensional cam or an electronic input. 
When compass information is unreliable near the poles this system will operate as a gyro in the directional gyro or DG mode. 
This is still only a part solution because a gyro compass in the DG mode is subject to the same errors as a normal DI. Thus any prolonged flight in areas of compass unreliability would still lead to navigational problems. 


Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Pure Gyro Navigation 
A second method for dealing with the problems of polar navigation is to discard the normal heading references of either the true or magnetic north and discard magnetic compasses in favour of pure gyroscopic or DI type heading references. 
We then overprint a grid of lines on a chart and then use the grid and gyro for heading reference. 
As the DI remains aligned in space it will keep its alignment as we move around, at least on a stationary earth, and the problem of transport wander is sidestepped. Notice that this does not remove any uncorrected earth rate or real wander. These residual errors mean that, even when using grid navigation, the gyro alignment must be repeatedly rechecked perhaps (going back a few years) with star shots from a sextant. 
The image opposite shows a grid aligned with the Greenwich meridian laid over a chart of the polar region. An aircraft track has been overlaid, in still air to avoid confusing the issue. You can see that, although the true track would change considerably the grid track, the aircraft's alignment to the grid, does not. 
Grid North Grid North Grid North Grid North 

True track changes, grid track does not 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Pure Gyro Navigation continuea 
If we were to state this in another way we could say that, apart from the line where grid north and true north coincide, the datum meridian, there will be a difference between the grid track and the true track. 
The difference between grid track and true track is called convergence and will alwaysbe the chart convergency between the datum meridian and where we are. 
If we know grid track we can find true track, if we know true track we can find grid track provided we know the chart convergency. Problems of this kind are usually presented on a polar stereographic chart where convergency is simply the change of longitude. 
If you are also studying Flight Planning and have the Jeppesen charts an example of such a chart is the 5AT(HI} polar high altitude en-route chart. 
Example: 
An aircraft is overhead 78a° N 095a° W flying a track of 040a° Grid on a polar chart with the grid aligned to the Greenwich meridian. Find the true track direction. 
Solution: 
Draw a sketch diagram. 

Grid Track 040° 
True Track 
? 

The grid track and true track must differ by the convergence, which is the change of long from the datum meridian, in this case 95a° . 
TRUE = GRIDa± CONVERGENCE 
= 040° ± 95° = 135°T or 305°T 

The diagram provides the answer, 305a° .

Example: 
An aircraft at 80a° S 130a° E is flying 030a° T. A grid is overlaid aligned with the Greenwich meridian, determine the grid track direction.

Draw a diagram

The diagram provides the final answer, 160a° G 
Although it is not a very elegant navigational solution it might be worth noting that, provided these diagrams are drawn correctly, an accurate drawing will allow the answer to be measured with a protractor. 

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Inertial Navigation 
Modern long range aircraft use inertial navigation systems (INS) 
or inertial reference systems (IRS) operating either independently or through a flight management system (FMS). 
Although an inertial system operates with gyros and accelerometers, it does not suffer from the gyro errors of transport wander and earth rotation. It can, however, give an incorrect position as a result of an initial input error, from real wander or from errors arising from a cyclical error called Schuler loop error. 
Schuler errors produce oscillating errors in position which are 
quite small and can be damped out. Input errors should be avoided and produce such large errors that they are usually easily detected. Real wander and, on some types, a 'quick' alignment procedure, can produce a position drift. Each system will have set limits of drift, usually betweena˝ NM to 2 NM per hour flown is acceptable. 
An inertial system uses three gyros and three accelerometers 
Inertial systems still work near the poles 
Unlike compass based systems an inertial system will work just as well in polar areas as anywhere else. 


Single INS 
A single INS must be backed up with another long range navigation system (the exact requirement would be determined by the airspace involved). The systems would operate independently with the pilots either monitoring the errors at waypoints by comparing positions or by observing cross track displacement on the HSI. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Twin INS/IRS 
Twin INS/IRS units might be used independently (older aircraft) or in more modern aircraft through a FMS. Errors in the two systems could be monitored by position checks at waypoints. With independent systems, the HSI will show distance and time to the next waypoint, track direction and cross track displacement to allow additional continuous monitoring. The diagram opposite shows two HS ls from independently operating INS, the one on the left is the Captain's and the one on the right the first officer's. The Captain's HSI is supplied by INS 1, as indicated by the 1 in the 9 o'clock position, which is steering the aircraft through the autopilot. The first officer's is supplied by INS 2 in the stand alone mode. 
The two HS ls show distance and time to the next waypoint at the top left and right of the display, there is a difference in distance of 
2.3 NM, and the FO's deviation bar shows a cross track error of just under one dot which would equate, on this system, to about 3 NM. Both course pointers indicate the same desired track.

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 

Twin INS/IRS continued 
The INS control unit (CDU) will also show distance and time to the next waypoint by selecting DIS/TIME on the rotary function selector. Cross track error (XTK) and track error angle (TKE) can also be displayed. The FO's CDU is shown next to it, with the two selections made, this mirrors the information on the HSI, except that a TKE of 0.1 ° has been detected which would be invisible on the other instrument. 
If an error arises the difficulty with twin INS is deciding when a system has failed and which IN is in error. If you are lucky the INS itself will detect an internal failure and show a 'fault' light, more likely it will be up to you to decide. This could be determined using another navigation system such as VOR or NOB, if available, a comparison of INS wind with forecast/reported wind values may assist, or possibly the weather radar may be of use in comparison of bearings to prominent land features. 


Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Triple INS/IRS 
It is highly unusual for triple INS/IRS to be used with anything other than an FMS. In this case, the position will be 'triple mixed', in other words the FMS position will be based on the average of the three IN positions. If one IN wanders off, the FMS will discard it and the pilots will be informed of a system degradation. In addition, most modern aircraft will be equipped with GPS navigation systems. 
For instance, a modern Airbus 'full up' navigation mode would be 'GPS primary', a problem with satellite coverage would lead to a system down-grade to 'triple mix'. These modern systems are both very reliable and extremely accurate. 
A total failure of a triple IRSa+ GPS system would be most 
unusual. However, one possible scenario would involve a computer system failure, which would deny steering guidance and the indication of position relative to cleared track, but the basic outputs of the IRS (LAT/LONG, drift and groundspeed) are left unimpaired. In this case crew will have to use the appropriate plotting charts to draw the cleared route and extract mean true tracks between waypoints. The basic IRS/GPS outputs can then be used to adjust heading to maintain mean track and to calculate ETAs. 


FMS Stored Routes 
FMS systems have stored waypoints and routes in databases that are updated every 28 days. The routes may be modified in flight by the crew but the changes only apply to that flight, the route that is stored in the FMS remains unchanged. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
North Atlantic High Level Airspace (NAT HLA) 
Certain Areas of the world are designated as only useable by aircraft meeting required navigation performance. The area of the North Atlantic stretching from just north of the Canary Islands (27a° N) up to the North Pole between FL285 and FL420 is one such area called, until 2016, the North Atlantic Minimum Navigation Performance Specification Airspace (MNPS) it is now called the North Atlantic High Level Airspace or NAT HLA (often shortened to just HLA). Note: Some Question Banks may still refer to MNPS. 
The governing documentation for the NAT airspace is ICAO Document 7030 (regional supplementary procedures) however, the detailed procedures and requirements for NAT HLA are found in NAT DOC 007 (North Atlantic Operations and Airspace Manual). 
NAT HLA lies between FL285 and FL420 
NAT HLA covers the Gander, Shanwick, New York, Santa Maria and Reykjavik Oceanic Control Areas (OCAs) 

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
North Atlantic High Level Airspace (NAT HLA) continued 
Aircraft flying in HLA have to meet standards of navigation, crew training and procedures. Specific approval to operate in the airspace would be given by the operator's authority and be detailed on an operator's AOC. Operators must ensure crews follow NAT HLA procedures. 
Certain levels within the HLA area are designated as reduced vertical separation minima (RSVM) levels. In the North Atlantic HLA area these are FL290 to FL410 inclusive (virtually all of HLA airspace). Aircraft, operators and crews must be authorised by the authority to operate at RVSM levels. 

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Pre Flight Considerations 
The pilot is ultimately responsible for ensuring that the aircraft has permission to enter the NAT HLA RVM airspace and that the aircraft is fitted with the required serviceable navigation and communication systems. The RNP approval status must be entered in Item 18 of the ATC flight plan and the pilot must verify the RNP value is entered in the Flight Management Computer. RNAV 10 retains RNP 10 and approval is given by the State of the Operator, or Registry, as appropriate. RNP 10 is the minimum specification for the application of 93 km (50 nm) lateral separation. 
The following procedures must be considered by the pilot: 
a.  Insertion of the Initial Latitude and Longitude. It is important  
the navigation system is properly aligned on the ground,  
including inputting the exact aircraft position, which should be  
recorded in the flight log or Master Document.  
b.  System alignment. The alignment of the inertial systems  
must be completed and equipment put into NAV mode prior  
to parking brake release. Some systems will align in about  
10 minutes but will take longer in extreme cold or higher  
latitudes or when the aircraft is buffeted by winds or rocked  
by cargo loading.  



c. GNSS (GPS) Systems. When both LRNS required for unrestricted NAT HLA operations are GPS. A Pre-Departure Satellite Navigation Prediction Programme which allows the manual designation of satellites that have been NOTAMed to be unavailable (offa-line or affected by solar flares) must be carried out. This prediction programme must be able to predict, prior to departure on a "specified Route" (timings on the route to be calculated for a range of expected ground speeds) the following: 
i. Any loss of navigation coverage (less than 3 satellites visible to the receiver). 
ii. Any loss of the RAIM / FOE function and its duration. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Pre Flight Considerations continued 
d. 
Satellite Availability. The following numbers of satellites are required but for RAIM and FDE only that number can be reduced by one if barometric aiding is used. 

i. 4 satellites are required for a 3-D position. 
ii. For Receiver Autonomous Integrity Monitoring (RAIM), 5 satellites are required to detect the presence of a single faulty satellite. 
iii. For Fault Detection and Exclusion (FDE) purposes, 6 satellites are required to identify a faulty satellite and exclude it from navigation solution calculations. 

e. 
Operational Control Restrictions. If loss of coverage is predicted then the flight must be delayed, re-routed or cancelled. If no loss of coverage is predicted then a RAIM/FDE outage prediction programme is run and if a predicted outage is greater than 51 minutes (or 25 minutes for RLatSM tracks) then again the flight should be re-routed, cancelled or delayed. 




f.  Loading of Initial Waypoints. Manual entry ofawaypoints must be carried out by 2 persons one should key in and insert data, the other should recall it and confirm it against source information. The ramp position, plus at least 2 additional waypoints, or, if possible all the waypoints relevant to the flight must be loaded whilst at the ramp.  
g.  Flight Plan Check. This compares the data in the Master Document and the calculated output from the navigation systems. Actions could include.  
I.  Checking distance from the ramp position to the first waypoint.  
II.  Selecting track Waypoint 1 to Waypoint 2 and checking the track and distance against the Master Document.  
Ill.  Checking each leg in a similar manner.  
IV.  When using integral navigation databases, the recency of the database must be verified, and it is still good practice to check the route as for manual waypoint entry.  
h.  Prior to leaving the Ramp. The Zero Ground Speed indications on the LRNS should be confirmed.  

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Master Document 
A Master Working Document is used on the flight deck based 
upon the flight plan, navigation log or other suitable document which lists sequentially, the waypoints defining the route, the track and distance between each waypoint, and other information relevant to navigation along the cleared track. 
Use of the Master Document 
a.  Only one Master Document is to be used on the flight deck, although crew members can maintain a separate flight log  
b.  On INS equipped aircraft the waypoint numbers should be entered on the Master Document and the identical  
numbering sequence used for storing waypoints in the navigation computers.  
c.  On aircraft equipped with FMS data bases, FMS generated, or inserted waypoints should be compared to the Master Document waypoints and cross checked by both flight crew members. In which case one crewman reads out the FMS  
waypoint and the other crewman compares that to the Master Document.  
d.  An appropriate symbology should be adopted to indicate the status each waypoint listed in the Master Document.  



e. 
Waypoints in the Master Document can be numbered to match the number and sequence in the navigation computers. Once checked against the waypoints entered in the navigation computers the waypoint can be circled on the Master Document. Other annotations include ticking the circled waypoint number to signify the track and distance information has been double-checked or crossing out the circled waypoint, once overflown. 

f. 
ATC clearances or re-clearances should be monitored by two qualified flight crew one of them recording the clearance on the Master Document as it is received, the other cross­checking the receipt and read back. 


Although the operator must obtain the approvals the final responsibility rests with the Captain to ensure the approvals are in place and the aircraft is equipped for the route. 
Over the high seas, the NAT region is primarily Class A airspace (at and above FLSS), in which instrument flight rules (IFR) apply at all times. 
RVSM levels are FL290 to FL410 inclusive 
NAT HLA airspace is IFR 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Longitudinal Separation 
Longitudinal separation between following and crossing aircraft is established by time at waypoints and controlled by regulating the aircraft speed as a Mach number. 
No matter how accurate the navigation system the separation is degraded if the aircraft clock is in error. Because of this, and because many master clocks can only be reset on the ground, the pre-flight checks for an aircraft intending to operate in HLA must include synchronising the clock to UTC from an acceptable source such as GPS or the BBC time signal. 
The minimum longitudinal separation between jet aircraft in HLA is 
10 minutes for aircraft reporting over a common point and travelling at the same speed. If the aircraft in front is going faster by at least M0.02 and it is possible to ensure that the time interval exists using radar or another approved means the separation can progressively reduced down. 
Normal longitudinal separation is 10 minutes 


If the aircraft do not report over a common point and are out of radar range the separation is 15 minutes. 
Following trials some higher level tracks (Data Link Mandated Airspace) have reduced longitudinal separation (5 min) and lateral separation (15nm) using Controller Pilot Data Link Communications (CPDLC) and Automatic Dependant Surveillance -Contract (ADS-C) but that is not covered by the current (2018) EASA LOs. 
Timing is important and so the pre-flight procedures for any NAT HLA flight must include a Universal Coordinated (UTC) time check. 
Vertical Separation 
Below FL410, minimum vertical separation in HLA is 1000 ft. Therefore, aeroplanes and crew operating in HLA require RVSM approval. This approval would once again be detailed on an operator's AOC. 
Vertical separation within RVSM airspace is 1000 ft 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
RVSM Minimum Equipment 
The minimum equipment standard is embodied in the minimum aircraft systems performance specification (MASPS) for RVSM operations. The MASPS require: 
¦ two fully serviceable independent primary altitude measurement systems 
¦ one automatic altitude-control system and 
¦ one altitude-alerting device. 
A functioning Mode C SSR Transponder is also required for flight through radar controlled RVSM transition airspace. 
Airworthiness approval for RVSM operations may be granted by the appropriate State Authority to individual aircraft, or to a group of aircraft which are nominally identical in aerodynamic design and in items of equipment contributing to height keeping accuracy. 
When checking altimeters (pre-flight or in-flight), confirmation is necessary that all altitude indications are within the tolerances specified in the aircraft operating manual. At least two primary altimeters must at all times agree within plus or minus 200 ft. 
RVSM approved aircraft must have 2 primary altimeters that stay within 200 ft 


HLA approved aircraft that do not meet RVSM standards may climb and descend through RVSM levels, with clearance, and can in certain circumstances such as humanitarian flights be allowed to operate at RVSM levels. 
At RVSM levels, moderate and severe turbulence may also increase the level of system risk and crews should report ALL vertical deviations of 300 ft or more. 
Crews not using CPDLC must report to ATC immediately on leaving current cruising levels and upon reaching the new cruising level. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Lateral Separation 
ATC clearances ensure a lateral separation of 60 NM. (The defining waypoints of OTS tracks are specified by whole degrees of latitude and, using an effective 60 NM lateral separation standard). Within certain parts of the NAT, this minimum separation can be reduced, although there are equipment and navigation performance conditions. 
60 NM lateral separation 
The NAT HLA defines a requirement for the standard deviation of lateral track errors to be less than 6.3 NM. Since two standard deviations provide for about 95% containment, the HLA statement is effectively equivalent to an RNP value of 12.6 in a lateral sense. It is also interesting to note that actual measurements of the achieved navigation performance by the entire fleet of NAT aircraft, even before GPS came into use by a large proportion, indicated an achieved standard deviation of approximately 2 NM. 


The State of Registry or the State of the Operator, as appropriate, should verify that the lateral navigation capability of approved aircraft meets the above specified requirements. It should, however, be noted that aircraft that have been approved by the State of Registry or the State of the Operator, as appropriate, for RNP 10 (PBN application of RNAV 10) or RNP 4 are considered to meet the requirements. Although when granting consequent approval for operations in NAT HLA airspace, States should take account of the RNP 10 time limits for aircraft equipped with dual INS or inertial reference unit (IRU) systems. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Performance Monitoring 
The horizontal (i.e. latitudinal and longitudinal) and vertical navigation performance of operators within NAT HLA is monitored on a continual basis. If a deviation is identified, follow-up action after flight is taken, both with the operator and the State of Registry of the aircraft involved, to establish the cause of the deviation and to confirm the approval of the flight to operate in 
NAT HLA and/or RVSM. The overall navigation performance of all aircraft in the HLA is compared to the standards established for the Region, to ensure that the relevant Target Level of Safety (TLS) are being maintained. 


Nav Equipment Requirements For Unrestricted HLA Operations 
For unrestricted operation in the HLA an aircraft must be equipped with two fully serviceable long range navigation systems (LRNSs) which may be INS, IRS/FMS or GPS. So far as GPS is 
concerned, the rules are quite specific on which systems are acceptable. 
Two long range navigation systems are required 
Each LRNS must be capable of providing to the flight crew a continuous indication of the aircraft position relative to desired track. Although not mandatory, it is desirable that the steering navigation system can be coupled to the autopilot. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Routes Usable by Aircraft Without Two LRN Systems 
A number of special routes, called blue spruce routes, have been developed for aircraft equipped with only one LRNS and carrying normal short-range navigation equipment (VOR, DME, ADF). Additional special routes designated G3 and G11 can be used by aircraft that only have VOR, DME and ADF. 
If you only have one LRNS use a blue spruce route 
These routes are within HLA airspace and still require approval from the National Authority. It is the responsibility of pilots with limited certification to reject clearances which would otherwise divert them from officially permitted routes. 

Centreline or Lateral Offset to the Right of Course only, in increments of 0.1 NM (0.1, 0.2, 0.3etc ) out to 2 NM.

These routes are used to avoid French Airspace when flying North / South or vice versa and to reach the Canary Islands. Some require less equipment than normal for NAT HLA operations, but all require HLA approval if cruising above FL 285.

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 

Aircraft not Approved for NAT HLA 
Aircraft not approved for operation in HLA airspace may be cleared by ATC to climb or descend through HLA airspace provided that the climb or descent can be completed within the coverage of selected VOR/DMEs or NDBs or within the radar coverage of the ATC unit. The aircraft must be able to maintain direct controller/pilot communications (DCPC) on VHF and HLA must not be adversely affected. 
Non-HLA approved aircraft may also be cleared to climb or descend through HLA to depart or land at an airport underneath the HLA airspace even if it does not have serviceable short range navaids, radar or DCPC.

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
As a result of passenger demand, time zone differences and airport noise restrictions, much of the NAT air traffic concentrates on two major alternating flows: a westbound flow departing Europe in the morning, and an eastbound flow departing North America in the evening. The effect of these flows is to concentrate most of the traffic unidirectionally 
Because of large horizontal separation criteria and the economical height band (FL310-400) the airspace is congested at peak hours. The NAT organized track system (OTS) caters for this congestion. The OTS is constructed to accommodate as many flights as possible within the major flows on or close to their minimum time tracks and altitude profiles. Due to weather patterns, including the presence of jet streams, consecutive eastbound and westbound minimum time tracks are seldom identical. The creation of a different organised track system is therefore necessary for each of the major flows. Separate organised track structures are published each day for eastbound and westbound flows. 
The westbound tracks begin with the letter 'fl:. as the most northerly track and continue vertically down with 'B', 'C', 'D', and so on depending on how many tracks are needed to accommodate the forecast traffic. 
The diagram opposite illustrates a typical distribution of westbound tracks. 


DATE 1110211seTh41 .
11 FROM . UTCTO . UTC 
I)\"\ 
-. 
Westbound NAT tracks are lettered A to Z top to bottom. They never get much beyond G 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
The Organised Track System (OTS) continued 
This diagram shows typical eastbound tracks. The eastbound tracks begin with 'Z' as the most southerly track and continue vertically upward with 'Y', 'X', 'W' etc. Note the one degree or 60 NM lateral separation. 
When considering the allocation of flight levels and routes on the North Atlantic, the fact that the eastbound flow is predominantly a night time phenomena and the westbound the opposite gives rise to two distinct and separate traffic flows. 
The NAT OTS operates twice during each 24 hour period with a westbound system operational from 1130 hours to 1900 hours UTC and an eastbound system operational between 0100 and 0800 UTC. 
These published times refer to when the flight is going to cross the 030°W meridian of longitude. This is the boundary point between the Shanwick and Gander Oceanic Control Areas (OCAs), the areas through which the majority of flights transit. Within these areas there are some 300 individual operations at any one point in time during peak hours. 
NAT track messages are issued twice daily once to describe the eastbound tracks and once for the westbound. They originate with the Gander and Shanwick OCAeATS.

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Eastbound NAT OTS Track Message 
Issued by Gander Oceanic Control Area Centre (OAC) at around 1400 UTC and valid for flights flying between North America and Europe at 030a° W during the hours of 0100 to 0800 UTC the next day. 
Westbound NAT OTS Track Message 
Issued by Shanwick Oceanic Control Area Centre around 2200 UTC and valid for flights operating between Europe and North America at 030a° W during the hours of 1130 to 1900 UTC the next day.
