Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Preferred Route Message (PRM) 
Operators should submit in advance the number of flights and 
their company preferred route for these flights through the appropriate oceanic controlled areas. This helps the authorities in the compilation of the NAT OTS for the particular time period in the following 24 hours. The PRM will normally take into account the "minimum time route" (MTR) which we looked at earlier. 
¦ 
For eastbound flights the PRM should reach the Gander OAC by 1000 UTC 

¦ 
For the westbound operations it should reach the Shanwick 


OAC by 1900 
In HLA waypoints should be on whole degrees of latitude and longitude, every 10° of longitude when flying predominantly east/west and, when predominantly north/south, every 5° of latitude. Tracks between waypoints should be great circles. 


OTS Changeover Periods 
There is always a gap of several hours between the de-activation of one set of NAT tracks and the activation of the next between 0801 and 1129 UTC and between 1901 to 0059 UTC to avoid confusion on the changeover. 
During the changeover periods some restrictions to flight planned routes and levels are imposed. Eastbound and westbound aircraft operating during these periods should file flight level requests in accordance with the Flight Level Allocation Scheme (FLAS) as published in the UK and Canada AIPs. Operators should recognise that they might not be allocated the level requested. 
Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Random Routings During the Operating Times of the NAT Organised Track System 
It is recommended that the OTS is used whenever possible. Operators who plan flights on 'random routes' will not be refused clearance but, in the majority of cases, will be assigned an uneconomic flight level below NAT tracks or will be re-routed to avoid the OTS system altogether. Either of these will incur an economic penalty. 
Aircraft may fly on random routes which remain clear of the OTS or may fly on any route that joins or leaves an outer track of the OTS. There is also nothing to prevent an operator from planning a route which crosses the OTS, if this occurs in the early stages of the flight it is likely the aircraft will be held down below the NAT tracks until clear, if it happens near to destination the aircraft might be descended early or re-routed. The maximum flight time recommended between significant points on random routes is approximately one hour. 

Polar and Oceanic Procedures 
Random Routings Outside the Operating Times of the NAT OTS 
Outside of the OTS periods operators may flight plan any random routeing, except that during the two hours prior to each OTS period the following restrictions apply: 
(1) 
Eastbound flights that cross 30°W less than one hour before the incoming westbound NAT tracks (after 1029 UTC), or westbound flights that cross 30°W less than one hour before the incoming eastbound NAT tracks (after 2359 UTC) should plan to remain clear of the incoming OTS structure. 

(2) 
Any such opposite direction flights crossing 30°W between one and two hours before the incoming OTS where the route beyond 30°W would coincide with the incoming OTS structure at any point should plan to join an outer track at any point, or backtrack the length of one of the incoming tracks. 




The time restriction effectively means this applies to aircraft crossing 30°W eastbound between 0930 and 1029 UTC or westbound between 2300 and 2359 UTC. 
For flights whose flight paths at or south of 80a° N are predominantly oriented in a north-south direction, the planned tracks shall normally be defined by significant points formed by the intersection of whole degrees of longitude with specified parallels of latitude which are spaced at intervals of 5 degrees. 


Lesson 73 
NAT HLA 
ATPA OPB 13 
NAT HLA 
Route Structures Adjacent to NAT HLA 
North American Routes (NARs) 
The North American Routes (NARs) consist of a numbered series of predetermined routes which provide an interface between NAT oceanic and North American domestic airspace. The NAR System is designed to accommodate major airports in North America. The names of these track systems are self-explanatory, and details would be found in State AIPs. 
US East Coast Transitions 
Canadian Domestic Track Systems 
Routes between North America and the Caribbean area 
There are also Transition Areas. HLA requirements are still applicable from FL285 to FL420, except that flights routing such that they are not subject to an Oceanic Clearance, are not required to be HLAApproved. Again, details published in State AIPs. 
Shannon Oceanic Transition Area (SOTA) Northern Oceanic Transition Area (NOTA) Brest Oceanic Transition Area (BOTA) Gander Oceanic Transition Area (GOTA) 


Strategic Lateral Offset Procedure (SLOP) 
Separation between aircraft in HLA is achieved by a chain of clearance definition, delivery and execution which involves a series of technical system processes and human actions. However, the accuracy of modern navigation and altimeter systems introduces a new area of 'risk' a simple error in any one of these events (for example a one degree error in waypoint data ­inserting 59N instead of 58N) could mean that a serious risk of collision is created. To reduce this risk pilots can select to fly a lateral offset. This procedure is known as "strategic lateral offset procedure (SLOP)". A pilot may select an offset within the following guidelines: 
¦ 
An aircraft may fly offsets right of the centreline, in 

increments of a tenth of a nautical mile, up to a maximum of 2 NM. 


¦ 
Offsets to the left of the centreline are NOT permitted. 

¦ 
Aircraft without automatic offset programming capability must fly the centreline. 

¦ 
Aircraft with an automatic offset programming capability should randomly select flying the centreline or an offset. 


NAT HLA 
Strategic Lateral Offset Procedure {SLOP) continued 
¦ 
Crews may apply the offset outbound at the oceanic entry point and must return to the centreline prior to the oceanic exit point, unless otherwise authorised by the appropriate ATS authority or directed by the appropriate ATC unit. 

¦ 
No ATC clearance is required for the procedure, nor is it necessary to advise ATC. 

¦ 
For wake turbulence purposes, flight crews should fly one of the offset positions and may contact other crews on the air­to-air channel (123.45 MHz) to co-ordinate the best option. 

¦ 
Aircraft overtaking other aircraft should offset within the confines of the procedure to avoid wake turbulence. 

¦ 
SLOP is NOT applied in the Oceanic Transition areas (SOTA, NOTAaetc.) apart from the Gander Oceanic Transition Area (GOTA) where it can be applied. 




NAT HLA 
The Oceanic ATC Flight Plan 
Flights in HLA require an ATS flight plan. 
ITEM 10 EQUIPMENT. 
Include the following: 
HLA authorised X 
RVSM authorised W 
DATA LINK both in ITEM 10 and ITEM 18, -you need to tell 
ATC what you have, and any relevant codes and contacts: 
RNP insert R, then indicate performance in ITEM 18 
ITEM 18. Other information 
For CPDLC Data link REG/ ... [FANS] or CODE/ ... [ATN]. PBN/A1 (foraRNP 10aApproval) PBN/L 1 (for RNP 4 Approval) 
ITEM 10 of the ATC flight plan is annotated X for NAT HLA authorised aircraft 


Item 15 Route 
The NAT track can only be used in ITEM 15, the route, if the whole track is flown. The normal routing is shown with the speed as a TAS up to the entry point, then the speed is expressed as a Mach number for the route and after the exit point the speed reverts to TAS. 
N0465F310 WAL UB3 BEL DCT 59N01 0W/M080F350 NAT C CARPE REDBY/N0470F370 NA210 
Random Routing 
For flights operating on random routes, or routes not following the full length of a published NAT track, the route entry in ITEM 15 on the ATS flight plan cannot refer to the NAT track. In this case the route description must be completed using all the latitude and longitudinal co-ordinates. 
For example: 
N0465F310 WAL UB3 BEL DCT 58N01 0W/M080F350 DCT 59N020W 59N030W 58N040W 57N050W CARPE REDBY/N0470F370 NA210 
NAT HLA 
NAT HLA Communications 
Oceanic Clearance 
The Captain must obtain Oceanic ATC clearance before entering the NAT area, ideally 40 minutes before. The OACs recognise 40 minutes notice is not always possible. 
The call westbound is usually made to Shanwick on VHF, eastbound will be to Gander on VHF. Oceanic clearances can also be obtained on HF or sent to the operator and on to the aircraft via the VHF data link AGARS. Lastly, modern aircraft are increasingly equipped with SATCOM. SATCOM Voice, can be used as a supplement to HF communications throughout the NAT Region for any routine, non-routine or emergency ATS air/ground communications. NAT ATS StateAIPs contain the necessary telephone numbers and/or short-codes for air-initiated call access to aeradio stations and/or direct to OACs. 
A typical request for clearance would indicate Mach number, level, the highest level the aircraft can cope with and a second choice of NAT track. 
"Speedbird 464A request Oceanic clearance. Estimating 56N010W at 1131. Request Mach decimal eight zero, Flight level three five zero, able flight level three six zero, second choice track Charlie". 


The Oceanic clearance will be based on time estimates for the major reporting points filed in the ATC flight plan and, as the flight progresses, any differences in these ETAs of three minutes or more must be reported to ATC. 
The clearance is received: 
"Speedbird 464A is cleared to Toronto via track Bravo 283, from 56N01 OW maintain flight level three five zero, Mach decimal eight zero". 
The clearance is read back verbatim except the TMI number (assuming TMI 283): 
"Speedbird 464A is cleared to Toronto via track Bravo, from 
56N01 OW maintain flight level three five zero, Mach decimal eight zero". 
Procedures exist for an abbreviated read back of an Oceanic Clearance. The flight crew will confirm that they are in possession of the current NAT Track message by using the TMI number (including any suffix) in the read-back of the Oceanic Clearance, as follows: 
''.4CA865 is cleared to Toronto via Track Bravo 283A, from PIKIL maintain Flight Level three five zero, Mach decimal eight zero". 
NAT HLA 
Oceanic Clearance continued 
If the TMI number is included in the read-back there is no requirement for the pilot to read back the NAT Track co-ordinates even if the cleared NAT Track is not the one which was originally requested. If any doubt exists as to the TMI or the NAT Track co­ordinates, the pilot should request the complete track co-ordinates from the OAC. Similarly, if the pilot cannot correctly state the TMI, the OAC will read the cleared NAT Track co-ordinates in full and request a full read back of those co-ordinates. 
It should also be noted that some airports are located close to oceanic airspace boundaries, in this situation, it may be necessary to obtain oceanic clearance before departure. Clearance errors that are not picked up by either ATC or pilots are called ATC loop errors. 


Delayed Oceanic Clearance 
If pilots have not received their Oceanic Clearance prior to reaching the Shanwick OCA boundary, they must contact Domestic ATC and request instructions to enable them to remain clear of Oceanic Airspace whilst awaiting such Clearance. This is not the case for other NAT OCAs into any of which flights may enter whilst pilots are awaiting receipt of a delayed Oceanic Clearance. Pilots should always endeavour to obtain Oceanic Clearance prior to entering these other NAT OCAs however if any difficulty is encountered the pilot should not hold while awaiting Clearance unless so directed by ATC. In such circumstances, pending receipt of the Oceanic Clearance, the aircraft should continue to maintain the flight level cleared by the current control authority. 
NAT HLA 
HF Comms 
Most NAT air/ground communications are conducted on single sideband HF frequencies 24 HF frequencies have been allocated, in bands ranging from 2.8 to 18 MHz. Pilots communicate with OACs through radio operators. The ground radio operators are not controllers, they relay messages to the relevant OAC for action. 
Frequencies from the lower HF bands tend to be used for communications during the night and higher frequency bands during daytime. Generally, in NAT HLA, frequencies of less than 7 MHz are used at night and more than 8 MHz during the day. 
Primary Communication in the NAT HLA is via HF and even when aircraft are equipped with, and use CPD LC for communications and receive an acknowledgement of handover from one OAC to another, the crew must establish communications with the new OAC on HF. 
North Atlantic comms is largely through HF 


Aeradio stations and OACs are not necessarily co-located. For example in the case of Shanwick operations, the OAC is located at Prestwick in Scotland whilst the associated aeradio station is at Ballygirreen in the Republic of Ireland. 
VHF Comms 
The carriage of HF communications equipment is mandatory for flight in the Shanwick OCA. Aircraft with only functioning VHF communications equipment should plan their route outside the Shanwick OCA and ensure that they remain within VHF coverage of appropriate ground stations throughout the flight. The VHF air­to-air frequency is 123.45 MHz. 
NAT HLA 
Data Link Communications 
Although HF is still required, Data Link communications are progressively being introduced and allowing higher densities of traffic in the HLA March 2019 saw the introduction of closer horizontal and vertical spacing between aircraft but this has yet to become part of the syllabus. Over 90% of aircraft operating in the NAT HLA are equipped with FANS 1.A or equivalent ADS-C and CPDLC. Although outside of the current syllabus, some definitions follow: 
FANS 1/A Future Air Navigation System 
Increases reliability and ease of communication between the pilot and the controllers by providing a common language and a reliable automatic or text communication format. The 1/A represents Boeing/Airbus. 
There are two parts to FANS ADS-C and CPDLC 


ADS-C Automatic Dependant Surveillance -Contract 
There are three types of contracts that can be set up by ATC. (ATC tell your FANS/ADS-C what information they want) 
¦ 
Periodic contracts are time-based and can be varied by air traffic controllers depending on their needs. 

¦ 
Event contract, Air Traffic Control (ATC) sets it up so that if your aircraft deviates in altitude, vertical speed, or several other different parameters that they predesignate and they're notified of it immediately. 

¦ 
Demand contracts are typically used when ATC wants to know where everybody is right now, and they'll initiate a contract and everyone will reply. 

¦ 
There is one more type of contract, but this contract unlike the previous three is not set up by ATC, it's controlled by the pilot. This is initiated with a mayday message. 


With FANS and the ADS-C aspects of automatic position reporting, air traffic control can set up under a periodic contract how often they want a report on an aircraft so they can monitor it at all times. These reports can also include weather 
(wind/temperature) information. 
NAT HLA 
ADS-B Automatic Dependant Surveillance -Broadcast 
The aircraft determines its position via satellite navigation and periodically broadcasts it, enabling it to be tracked. The information can be received by air traffic control ground stations as a replacement for secondary radar. It can also be received by other aircraft to provide situational awareness and allow self­separation. 


CPDLC Controller Pilot Data Link Communication 
This is a text message facility between air traffic control and the pilot. 
On first contact with the initial aeradio stations crews of participating aircraft should expect to receive the instruction "VOICE POSITION REPORTS NOT REQUIRED". 
Pilots electing to use Data link communications for regular ATS communications remain responsible for operating SELCAL (including completion of a SELCAL Check), or maintaining a listening watch on the assigned HF frequency. 
Flights equipped with FANS CPDLC and /or ADS-C should ensure that the data link system is logged on to the appropriate control area. CPDLC provides communication redundancy. ADS-C furthermore enables ATC to perform route conformance monitoring for downstream waypoints. 
ADS-C messages contain Met information (wind and temperature) thus meteorological voice reports are not required. But these automated reports do not report unusual phenomena (moderatea/ severe turbulence or icing, thunderstorms, volcanic ash etc.). These reports should be made via voice messaging or, if appropriate, via a CPDLC free text download message. 
NAT HLA 
SELCAL 
Large aircraft have equipment fitted that allows the pilots to be contacted on the radios without continuously monitoring the frequency. This is particularly needed for long oceanic legs using 
HF frequencies. SELCAL, short for selective calling, operates on 
HF or VHF radios. 
Each airframe has a SELCAL code allocated to it made up of four letters, for example RHKM, which is notified to the ATC agency on the flight plan and once more on first R/T contact. 
The ATC agency can transmit coded tones corresponding to the letter code that activates a flashing light and an audio tone in the cockpit. Once called the crew can put their headsets on and respond on the appropriate radio. 
The diagram shows an A320 comms box with the SELCAL 'CALL' light illuminated on the number 1 HF radio. 
The SELCAL must be checked with the ATC agency before the crew remove their headsets and go from a listening watch on the selected frequency to a SELCAL watch. In practice this SELCAL check is carried out on first contact with each new agency, even if the SELCAL has already been checked on the same frequency with the previous agency and the first check must be completed at or prior to entry into the OCA. 



The SELCAL code goes in item 18 of the flight plan 
The SELCAL must be checked at or prior to entry into oceanic airspace and thereafter on first contact with each new agency 
NAT HLA 
Position Reports 
If not operating on FANS 1 /A or ADS-C CPDLC, then voice position reports should be made at designated reporting points and at the significant points listed in the flight plan. If on an NAT track report at the points listed in the track message and at designated reporting points. 
The general guide is that ATC should have reports at roughly hourly intervals, as a rule, on an East-West track, north of 70°N every 20° of longitude, South of 70° every 10° of longitude. Any changes to ETAs of three minutes or more should be advised to ATC as soon as possible. 
ETA changes of 3 minutes or more must be advised 

Centreline or Lateral Offset to the Right of Course only, in increments of 0.1 NM (0.1, 0.2, 0.3etc ) out to 2 NM. 


NAT HLA 
"When Able Higher" (WAH) Reports 
Prior advice to ATC of the time or position that a flight will be able to accept the next higher level can assist ATC in ensuring optimal usage of available altitudes. A WAH Report must be provided by all flights entering the MNPS Airspace portion of the New York OCA and entering the Santa Maria OCA. Due to the higher number of step climb requests on the generally longer NAT route segments that transit New York and Santa Maria OCAs and also because of the greater frequency of crossing traffic situations. The provision of WAH Reports in these circumstances allows the controllers to more effectively utilise their airspace and provide aircraft more fuel efficient profiles. Provision of WAH Reports on entering other NAT OCAs is optional or they may be requested by anyaOAC. 
When required or when otherwise provided, upon entering an oceanic FIR, pilots should include in the initial position report the time or location that the flight will be able to accept the next higher altitude. The report may include more than one altitude if that information is available. 
Pilots must always report to ATC as soon as possible on reaching any new cruising level. 


For flights outside the domestic ATS route network, position 
should be expressed in terms of latitude and longitude except when flying over named reporting points. For flights whose tracks are predominantly east or west, latitude should be expressed in degrees and minutes, longitude in degrees only. For flights whose tracks are predominantly north or south, latitude should be expressed in degrees only, longitude in degrees and minutes. All times should be expressed in four digits giving both the hour and the minutes UTC. 
NAT HLA 

HF Comms Failure When Out of VHF Range 
¦ 
Two way HF communications failure can be caused either by on board equipment failure (effecting single aircraft) or through poor HF propagation conditions as a result of ionospheric disturbances (effecting many aircraft possibly over a wide area). General rules for either of these scenarios are as follows: 

¦ 
With two way ATS communications failure operate the SSR transponder on identity Mode A Code 7600 and Mode C. 

¦ 
If equipped, use SATCOM to contact the responsible radio station. However, it must be appreciated the capability varies between different NAT OACs. 

¦ 
If not equipped with SATCOM then attempt to use VHF to contact any (other) ATC facility or use VHF air-to-air 123.45 MHz so another aircraft can relay information to ATC. 


The North Atlantic air-to-air frequency is 123.45 MHz 
¦ In view of traffic density in the NAT region, broadcast regular position reports on the inter-pilot frequency. 


NAT HLA 
HF Comms Failure Before and After Entering the NAT Region 
Communications Failure General Provisions 
The pilot of an aircraft experiencing a two-way ATS communications failure should operate the SSR Transponder on identity Mode A Code 7600 and Mode C. 
1. 
When so equipped, an aircraft should use Satellite Voice Communications to contact the responsible aeradio station. 

2. 
If the aircraft is not equipped with SATCOM Voice then the pilot should attempt to use VHF to contact any (other) ATC facility or another aircraft. 

3. 
The inter-pilot air-to-air VHF frequency, 123.45 MHz, may be used to relay position reports via another aircraft. 

4. 
In view of the traffic density in the NAT Region, pilots of aircraft experiencing a two-way ATS communications failure should broadcast regular position reports on the inter-pilot frequency (123.45 MHz) until such time as communications are re-established. 


The communications failure could be due to Poor HF Propagation Condition in this scenario, there could be a large number of NAT aircraft affected (HF blackout). 


The communication failure could be due to an equipment failure ­in this case, it is just that one aircraft that is not talking to ATC. The guidance in NAT DOC 007 (North Atlantic Airspace and Operating Manual) summarizes the actions required quite nicely: 
¦ Equipment Failure before receiving an Oceanic Clearance:­
Divert or fly the Flight Plan route, speed and initial planned oceanic level to landfall. 
¦ Blackout encountered (in an HF com ms Domestic ATC environment) before receiving an Oceanic Clearance:­
Continue at Domestic cleared level and follow flight planned route and speed to landfall. 
¦ Equipment Failure or Blackout after receiving an Oceanic Clearance:­
Fly that clearance to landfall. 
In all cases, after landfall rejoin, or continue on, the flight planned route, using appropriate State AIP specified procedures for the domestic airspace entered.

NAT HLA 
In Flight Contingencies 
If there is an emergency or a situation on board the aircraft that results in the aircraft being unable to comply with the ATC clearance then, ideally, a revised clearance should be obtained before any deviation is made using the prefix 'PAN PAN' or 'MAYDAY' as appropriate. 
If the nature of the emergency is such that prior clearance cannot be obtained the aircraft should broadcast its position at frequent intervals on 121.5 MHz with 123.45 MHz as a back-up. 
Until a revised clearance is obtained the NAT inflight contingency procedure should be followed. 
An aircraft that is able to maintain its assigned flight level, should turn at least 30a° to the left or right of its cleared track. 


After deviating 5 NM from its original cleared track centreline (laterally clear of any potentially conflicting traffic following the same track), it should parallel track: 
¦ 
Descend below FL290 and establish a 150 m (500 ft) vertical offset from the flight levels normally in use and proceed as cleared by ATC or as the operational situation requires or 

¦ 
If not descending below FL290, 

¦ 
Climb or descend 300 m (1000 ft) if above FL410 

¦ 
Climb or descend 150 m (500 ft) if below FL410 

¦ 
Climb 1000 ft or descend 500 ft if at FL410 


An aircraft that is unable to maintain its assigned flight level should, whenever possible, minimise its initial rate of descent when leaving its original track centreline and then when expected to be clear of any possible traffic following the same track at lower levels, expedite descent to an operationally feasible flight level which differs from those normally used by 500 ft below FL410 (or by 1000 ft if above FL410) whilst obtaining and maintaining a 5 NM offset from its original cleared track centreline. 
NAT HLA 
In Flight Contingencies continued 
During the procedure the following actions should be taken: 
¦ 
Turn on all aircraft external lights 

¦ 
Watch for conflicting aircraft both visually and by reference to ACAS (if equipped) leaving ACAS in RA mode at all times, unless aircraft operating limitations dictate otherwise. 

¦ 
As soon as practicable, advise air traffic control of any deviation from assigned clearance. 

¦ 
Use whatever means is appropriate (voice and/or CPD LC) to communicate during a contingency or emergency. 

¦ 
When emergency situations are notified using CPDLC, the controller may respond via CPDLC or attempt voice communication with the aircraft. 

¦ 
Keep the SSR transponder on at all times and squawk 7700, as appropriate, when able. 


If unable to maintain clearance, offset by 5 NM and climb/descent 500 ft 


Factors which may affect the direction of turn when carrying out a contingency procedure include: 
¦ 
Direction to an alternate aerodrome 

¦ 
Terrain clearance 

¦ 
Levels allocated on adjacent routes or tracks and any known SLOP offsets adopted by nearby traffic. 


NAT HLA 
Deviation Around Severe Weather 
When weather deviation is required, the pilot request a revised clearance from ATC via voice or CPDLC. A rapid response may be obtained by either: 
1. 
Stating "Weather Deviation Required" to indicate that priority is required on the frequency and for an ATC response: or 

2. 
Request a weather deviation via CPDLC. 


If a revised clearance cannot be obtained the pilot shall take the following actions: 
¦ 
If possible, deviate from the track or route maintaining the assigned level until offset by 5.0 NM 

¦ 
Establish communications with and alert nearby aircraft by broadcasting, at suitable intervals, on 121.5 MHz or the inter­pilot air to air frequency 123.45 MHz and where appropriate on the frequency in use: aircraft identification, flight level, intentions, position (including ATS route designator or track code) 

¦ 
Watch for conflicting traffic both visually and by reference to ACAS (if equipped) 

¦ 
Turn on all external lights (commensurate with operating limitations) 

¦ 
Once 5.0 NM or more from track descend or climb by 90 m (300 ft) as indicted in the table opposite 




If a clearance has been obtained to deviate from track a specific distance and that distance must be exceeded but further clearance cannot be obtained. The pilot must descend or climb as per the table before deviating further. 
Route centre line track  Deviations> 19 km (10 NM)  Level change  
EAST (000° to179° magnetic)  LEFT of Track RIGHT of Track  DESCEND 300 ft (90 m) CLIMB 300 ft  
WEST (180° to359° magnetic)  LEFT of Track RIGHT of Track  CLIMB 300 ft DESCEND 300 ft  


¦ 
When returning to track the aircraft must regain the original assigned flight level by the 5.0 NM off set point before returning to the original track or route 

¦ 
Inform ATC when weather deviation is no longer required, or when the aircraft has returned to the centreline (or previously adopted SLOP offset) of its cleared route 


NAT HLA 
Indications of Navigation System Degradation 
Normally, navigation systems include comparator and warning devices such as RAIM or Fault Detection Alerts (FOE) but it is still necessary to make frequent checks. In an aircraft fitted with three independent systems the identification of a defective unit compared to the other two should be straightforward. 
In an aircraft with only 2 systems the following actions should be considered: 
¦ 
Check malfunction codes 

¦ 
Obtain a fix (radar, ADF, VOR) 

¦ 
Contact nearby aircraft on VHF and compare information on spot wind, ground speed and drift. 

¦ 
Compare Flight Plan wind speed and direction for the current DR position against outputs from the navigation systems. 





NAT HLA 
Partial or Complete Loss of Navigation/FMS Capability 
Some aircraft carry triple ( or more) LRNSs and hence if one system fails, even before take-off, the (2 fully serviceable LRNS) basic requirements for HLA operations may still be met and the flight can proceed normally. The following guidance is offered for aircraft equipped with only two operational LRNSs: 
One System Fails Before Take-Off: 
The pilot must consider: 
¦ 
Delaying departure until repair is possible 

¦ 
Obtaining a clearance above or below HLA airspace 

¦ 
Planning on 'blue spruce' or 'GOLF' routes as appropriate. 


One System Fails before the OCA Boundary is Reached 
The pilot must consider: 
¦ 
Landing at a suitable aerodrome before the boundary or returning to the aerodrome of departure 

¦ 
Diverting via one of the special routes described previously 

¦ 
Obtaining a re-clearance above or below NAT HLAaairspace.

NAT HLA 
Once the aircraft has entered Oceanic airspace, the pilot should normally continue to operate the aircraft in accordance with the Oceanic clearance already received, appreciating that the reliability of the total navigation system has been significantly reduced. 
The pilot should, however, assess the prevailing circumstances, consult with ATC with respect to the prevailing circumstances (e.g. request clearance above or below HLA, turn-back, obtain 
clearance to fly along one of the special routes, etc.), obtain appropriate re-clearance prior to any deviation from the last 
acknowledged Oceanic clearance. 
When operating in areas of compass unreliability, as a 
contingency against INS failure the route may be drawn on a plotting chart and the latitude and longitude plotted every 15 
minutes and compared to the flight plan track. 
The Remaining System Fails after Entering the NAT HLA 
The pilot should notify ATC immediately, attempt visual sightings and establish contact on VHF (121.5 MHz) with adjacent aircraft 
for useful information, keep a special look-out for possible conflicting aircraft, and make maximum use of exterior lights if no 
contact with ATC is possible, consider climbing or descending 500 ft. 


Before commencing any diversion across the flow of adjacent traffic, aircraft should, whilst maintaining the 5 NM offset track, expedite climb above or descent below the vast majority of NAT traffic (i.e. to a level above FL410 or below FL285), and then maintain a flight level which differs from those normally usede(+/­500 ft). However, if the pilot is unable or unwilling to carry out a major climb or descent, then any diversion should be carried out at a level 500 ft different from those in use within the HLA, until a new ATC clearance is obtained. 
If these contingency procedures are employed by a twin engine aircraft as a result of the shutdown of a power unit or the failure of a primary aircraft system the pilot should advise ATC as soon as practicable of the situation, reminding ATC of the type of aircraft involved and requesting expeditious handling. 


Lesson 74 
Turbulence and Windshear 
ATPA OP8 74 
Turbulence and Windshear 
Introduction 
This chapter considers the responses required to in-flight hazards and emergencies caused through wind shear, thunderstorms, microbursts and wake turbulence. 


Wind Shear 
Wind shear means a marked change in the wind vector -either direction, speed or both -over a short distance or time. This can cause the aircraft speed to unpredictably increase or decrease. 
Wind shear can be horizontal -where the wind vector changes rapidly at the same altitude -or vertical where the vector changes rapidly as you climb and descend. Most significantly, downdrafts and updrafts -changes in vertical wind speed -can be associated with large thunderstorms. 
Wind shear is most likely close to the ground, which makes it particularly dangerous. 
Wind shear is a marked change in the wind vector over a short time or distance 
Turbulence and Windshear 
Wind Shear at Fronts 
You will recall that wind direction changes as the front passes or, indeed, as an aircraft flies through the front itself. The animation shows the effect on an aircraft crossing a frontal surface on the final approach. 
In the diagram it is assumed that there is a 90° shift of wind direction through the front. A 15 KT headwind suddenly becomes a 25 KT crosswind and a zero headwind. The sudden loss in headwind causes the IAS to decrease and the equilibrium of the aircraft is disturbed. The momentum of the aircraft initially remains unchanged, but the decrease in IAS causes a decrease in lift. 
Without pilot input, the aircraft will again seek equilibrium, and with this decrease in lift, it will begin to go low on the original flight path. If the aircraft was on approach to land, this means it will go below the glideslope. This is a transient change the groundspeed of the aircraft initially doesn't change: i.e. the momentum of the aircraft does not suddenly change: it will change as a result of the change in forces, but that will take a finite time. The change in airspeed causes that change in forces: pilot action is required to return to the original state. 
We have discussed a decrease in headwind equally, an increase in tailwind will have the same effect. 


Rapid loss of headwind leads to sink 
Turbulence and Windshear 
Wind Shear near Thunderstorms 
Cold air descending from a thunderstorm can run ahead of the storm, producing a mini cold front. These gust fronts, sometimes called the "first gust", can give considerable wind shear and turbulence, effects similar to those of frontal wind shear discussed above. Wind shear has been measured at up to 80 KT and 90° in direction through shallow layers. 
Gust fronts can extend at worst to 24 to 32 km (15 to 20 NM) ahead of the thunderstorm and up to 6000 ft. When there is a line or group of thunderstorms gust fronts can extend to twice this distance. Their presence is sometimes marked by a roll of cloud at the gust front with rain and squally conditions. 


Turbulence and Windshear 
Wind Shear at Inversions and in The Boundary Layer 
Because of the friction at the surface the surface wind will be slower than the 2000 ft wind and will be blowing from a different direction. Climbing from the surface to the free-stream wind, or descending on the approach, will therefore always take you through vertical wind shear. This is not normally significant, unless the free-stream wind is very strong or there is a temperature inversion present, which produces a very sharp boundary between the airflow above and below the inversion. 


The critical conditions are: 
¦ A free-stream wind of over 40 KT. This implies turbulence in the boundary layer, and, over land, at least a 20 KT speed and 40° heading difference between the surface wind and the free-stream. 
¦ A vector difference between the surface and the free­stream wind of 40 KT or more. This is saying approximately the same thing as above. 
¦ 
A temperature inversion of 10°C or more in the first 1000 ft AGL. This will completely isolate the slow surface wind from the fast free-stream wind above the inversion, giving marked wind shear in the climb or descent and clear air turbulence near the top of the inversion. 

¦ 
The presence of a turbulence inversion. Here, too, the inversion will produce a sharp wind shear boundary between the turbulent airflow below the inversion, and the smooth 


free-stream airflow above. 

It is not a coincidence that these conditions are used to generate mandatory warnings of wind shear or turbulence. 

Turbulence and Windshear 
Standing Waves 
Standing waves in the troposphere occur when a stable air mass blows at moderate speed over a range of hills or mountains.

downwind carrying their turbulence with them. Note that rotors and rotor streaming can occur in the lee of ridges even when the upper air conditions are not right for standing wave formation. 
Flying with the wind over the ridge itself produces no serious problems, but flying into wind means that you may be trying to climb to clear the ridge when the downdraft is forcing you down. This is not a hazard only for light aircraft. There have been several near disasters to civil airliners caught in these conditions, and one case of an aircraft broken up in flight that may have been due to wave turbulence.

The turbulence from standing waves can be felt right up into the

low stratosphere, and as the start conditions call for a moderate low level wind that steadily increases with height, standing waves are often, but not always, associated with jetstreams aloft. The wave effects can persist for up to 100 NM downwind, depending on the size of the hills and the windspeed. 
5 10 15 20 
Turbulence and Windshear 
Microbursts 
The microburst is an extreme form of wind shear generated by the slug of descending air that comes from a thunderstorm cell. 
These downdrafts average around 3-4000 ft/min but have been measured at 6000 ft/min, 60 KT, and when they hit the ground 
they can flow out at 50 KT in one direction and 50 KT in the opposite direction. This gives a vector change in the surface wind of 100 KT over a relatively short distance -1 to 3 km. 
Microbursts only last for a few minutes, but the combination of downdraft and rapid wind reversal can be fatal. 
Consider the diagram opposite, which shows an aircraft making 
an approach in a microburst. The aircraft at position A above has reached the first sector of the microburst. The headwind is increasing rapidly to 50 KT, IAS has gone up, and the aircraft will go high on the glidepath. The pilot reduces power to compensate, and just as this is taking effect, the aircraft is hit by the downdraft and the headwind changes to a tailwind. IAS falls and the aircraft sinks below the glidepath at B. 
At C the pilot has started his wind shear recovery procedure, but the IAS is still falling as the tailwind is still increasing. The ability to recover the situation will depend on the power, height and speed reserves available. 


Microburst are characterised by downdrafts of 3000 to 4000 ft/min Microbursts can be 1 to 3 km across and last only a few minutes 
Turbulence and Windshear 
Microbursts continued 
The downdraft is initiated by precipitation, and is often marked by a column of rain. If the air below the cloud base is relatively dry, and the precipitation re-evaporates, giving a "dry" microburst, the wind shear will be worse. The evaporation will cool the slug of descending air, making it more dense and increasing the downdraft speed. 


Turbulence and Windshear 

There are two principal systems in use to give warning of microbursts and wind shear at airfields. One is a field of anemometers surrounding the airfield set some 5 to 10 NM out. These are linked to the control tower and display the pattern of surface winds on the approach. This is called the low level wind shear alert system (LLWAS). The second is low frequency doppler radar, used to directly measure wind vectors around the airfield. 
The best way to deal with wind shear is to avoid it, particularly microburst wind shear, and the LLWAS and pilot reports are invaluable in this respect. 
Given that avoidance has proved impossible the next problem is one of detection. In situations where wind shear is likely the pilot should be alert for rapid changes in airspeed that might be caused by wind shear. Older aircraft equipped with INS as opposed to 
IRS can obtain wind shear indications by selecting 'WIND" and "HOLD" simultaneously. A flashing display of 88888 signifies wind shear is shown opposite. 
More modern aircraft have a wind shear warning incorporated into the GPWS. When wind shear is detected below 1500 ft on the radio altimeter a visual Wind shear Warning message appears on each EADI with the audio warning 'WIND SHEAR, WIND SHEAR, WIND SHEAR'. Wind shear warnings take priority over other GPWS modes. 


Turbulence and Windshear 
Responding to Wind Shear 
Both detection systems are sensitive and wind shear warnings therefore only require a 'suitable response', which may be no more than extra vigilance. 
The only exception is when microburst wind shear is suspected, this is so extreme that the only appropriate reaction is a 'wind shear go-around'. This differs from a normal go-around in that full thrust is selected, often disregarding TGT limits, and the aircraft is pitched to maximum angle of attack rather than a fixed attitude. 
MaxaAoA is the pitch that corresponds to the 'pitch limit eyebrows' on an EFIS aircraft and on a pre-EFIS aircraft means flying on the light buffet or, more usually, stick-shaker. If speed brakes or spoilers are out they should be selected in. Depending on type, flaps may be raised from full to the take-off position. The gear is often left down because the retraction sequence may open undercarriage doors which temporarily add to the drag. 
Out of interest, an A330 windshear recovery checklist is shown below -this is produced by an operator as part of the operations manual. The terminology may seem a little strange, but the basic actions are consistent with our discussion.

The recommended recovery action is usually to select full thrust and pitch to max alpha, leaving the aircraft configuration unchanged 
Turbulence and Windshear 

Wake Turbulence 
Large aircraft at high angles of attack generate severe wake turbulence in the form of wingtip rotors or vortices that start as flow moves from the high pressure lower surface of the wing to the low pressure upper surface. Once formed the vortices sink slowly towards the ground after the aircraft has passed and then move outwards in still air at 1 to 5 KT. 
A separate argument in an ICAO document simply suggests that the area affected by wake turbulence behind an aircraft extends laterally to twice the wingspan, and vertically one wingspan. A simplistic view to this suggests the larger the wingspan, the greater area is affected to a following aircraft. 
Wake turbulence starts on take-off as the aircraft rotates, and on finals does not end until the aircraft nose goes down on the landing roll. The worst vortices come from heavy, clean and slow aircraft -which maximises the pressure difference lower surface/upper surface. 
Turbulence behind aircraft is most dangerous in light crosswinds which cause the vortices to drift onto the runway centre line. Stronger crosswinds will move the rotors off the flightpath and the associated turbulence will destroy the rotors. 
The picture shows a vortex visible in smoke as a B747 lands at Kai Tak Airport. 


Wake turbulence is present from take-off (rotation) to touchdown 
Turbulence and Windshear 



Wake Turbulence continued 
Wake turbulence categories are defined by maximum take-off mass with the exception of the B757 which, although it fits in the medium category by MTOM, is assessed as heavy because it generates unusually powerful vortices. 
The basic categories are as follows: 
On your flight plan form

M Medium -MTOM 7000 kg and 136 000 kg 
L Light -MTOM 7000 kg or less 
These are ICAO categories, which we require for the exam. Do not get confused with individual state rules and definitions -if you look at UKaAIPs, you will find 6 wake turbulence categories, which include SMALL, LIGHT, 2 x MEDIUM, HEAVY and A380 (superheavy). 

There are minimum spacings laid down both for aircraft on the Heavy aircraft are more than 136 000 kg approach and for departing aircraft. The tables include the recommendations for separation behind an A380.

Note that the distance based separation distances apply to both the departure and approach phases of flight. 
In the exam, there is a possibility you will bump into 'old' terminology. The separation criteria used to talk about radar and non-radar separation. These terms are no longer in use: we now talk about distance based (radar), and time based separation (non-radar). 
A separation of 2 minutes is applied in other situations not described above such as a light or medium aircraft landing after a heavy aircraft has departed or overshot. Similarly a light aircraft landing after a medium aircraft has departed or overshot requires 2 minutes and aircraft one category or more down departing after a heavier aircraft has landed also need 2 minutes. 


Lesson 75 
Icing Hazards 
ATPA OPB 75 
Icing Hazards 
Introduction 
This section considers the procedures for removing ice and contaminants from an aircraft on the ground, a process called de­icing, and how ice protection is achieved both on the ground and in the air, called anti-icing. Procedures for ground de-icing and anti-icing are set out in the Operations Manual, Part A, as are the in-flight procedures relating to icing. 


Icing Hazards 
Definitions 
Before we start, some definitions from ICAO Doc 9640, the Manual of Aircraft Ground De-icing/Anti-icing Operations, which form part of the Operational Procedures learning objectives: 
Drizzle. Fairly uniform precipitation composed exclusively of fine drops (diameter less than 0.5 mm (0.02 in)) very close together. Drizzle appears to float while following air currents although, unlike fog droplets, drizzle falls to the ground. 
Fog. A visible aggregate of minute water particles (droplets) in the air reducing the horizontal visibility at the Earth's surface to less than 1 kilometre. 
Freezing Fog: A fog formed of supercooled water droplets which freeze upon contact with exposed objects and form a coating of rime/clear ice. 
Freezing Rain or Drizzle. Rain or drizzle in the form of supercooled water drops which freeze upon impact with any surface. 
Frost. Referred to as "hoar frost". A deposit of ice having a crystalline appearance, generally assuming the form of scales, needles or fans. Frost is formed by sublimation, i.e. when water vapour is deposited upon a surface whose temperature is at or below freezing. 


Mist. Mist is caused by water droplets or ice crystals, and is defined as visibility 1000 m or more. Relative humidity may be assumed to be around 95%. 
Rain. Precipitation of liquid water particles, either in the form of drops of more than 0.5 mm in diameter or smaller drops which, in contrast to drizzle, are widely separated. 
Rime. A deposit of ice, produced by freezing of supercooled fog 
or cloud droplets on objects at temperatures below or slightly above freezing. It is composed of grains separated by air, sometimes adorned with crystalline branches, also known as clear ice. 
Snow. Precipitation of ice crystals, mostly branched in the form of six-pointed stars. The crystals are isolated or agglomerated to form snowflakes. Dry snow occurs when the ambient temperature is below freezing, wet snow when the ambient temperature is near or above freezing. 
Slush. Water-saturated snow which with a heel and toe slap down motion against the ground will be displaced with a splatter specific gravity 0.5 up to 0.8. 
Icing Hazards 

Definitions continued 
Anti-icing. Anti-icing is a precautionary procedure by which clean aeroplane surfaces are protected against the formation of ice and frost and the accumulation of snow and slush for a limited period 
of time. 
De-icing. The process which removes ice, snow, slush or frost from aeroplane surfaces. This may be accomplished by mechanical methods, or by pneumatic methods or through the use of heated fluids. Mechanical methods may be preferred under extremely cold conditions or when it has been determined that the frozen contaminant is not adhering to the aeroplane surfaces. When using heated fluids and optimum heat transfer is desired, fluids should be applied at a distance from the aeroplane surfaces in accordance with the approved operator procedure and fluid manufacturer recommendations. 
De-icing/anti-icing. A procedure combining both the deicing process and the anti-icing process and which can be performed in one or two steps. 
Holdover time. Holdover time (HOT) is the estimated time the anti-icing fluid will prevent the formation of ice and frost and the accumulation of snow on the protected (treated) surfaces of an aeroplane. 



Icing Hazards 
The Effects of Ice in Flight 
When supercooled water droplets hit the aircraft surface (which is below 0a° C), a fraction of the droplet freezes instantly forming rime ice on the leading edge the remaining portion of the droplet rolls 
Clear ice 
back over the surface, freezing and forming clear (or roll back) ice. 
Ice on the airframe adds weight, increases drag and disrupts the airflow, in some cases leading to dangerous handling characteristics. In icing conditions, for a given speed and angle of attack, wing lift could be reduced by as much as 30% and drag increased by up to 40%. The stall speed could increase significantly, controllability could reduce and flight characteristics might change. 
Ice forming on pitot probes and static ports or angle of attack vanes may give false altitude, airspeed, angle of attack and engine power information for air data systems. 
Ice in the engine intakes causes loss of thrust as it disturbs the airflow and can cause engine failure if ingested. For piston engined aircraft without fuel injection carburettor icing can cause loss of power and, in extreme cases, engine failure. 


Icing Hazards 
Responsibilities 
Safety has to come before commercial pressures (schedules, turn around times) and the time, and money "saved" by not de-icing or anti-icing the aircraft will directly increase running costs by affecting performance but the direct and indirect cost of an 
incident or accident would be far more costly. 
An operator must establish procedures to be followed when ground de-icing and anti-icing and related inspections of the aeroplane(s) are necessary. (Ops Manual Part A). 
A commander must not commence a flight under known or expected icing conditions unless the aeroplane is certificated and equipped to cope with such conditions. 
The Operations Manual Part B will specify if the aircraft is certificated for flight in icing conditions and if any restrictions apply. 
The Clean Aircraft Concept 
ICAO Doc 9640 states that take-off shall not be attempted when ice, snow, slush or frost is present or adhering to the wings, propellers, control surfaces, engine inlets or other critical surfaces. This is known as the "Clean Aircraft Concept" 


Icing Hazards 
Ground De-Icing/Anti-Icing 
Ground de-icing/anti-icing can be carried out in one or two stages. When it is completed in one stage heated anti-icing fluid is used to de-ice the aeroplane and then the fluid remains on the aeroplane surface to provide an anti-icing capability. When ground de­icing/anti-icing is carried out in two stages the first step, de-icing, is followed by the second step, anti-icing, as a separate application of anti-icing fluid applied to protect the aeroplane's critical surfaces. 
The holdover time in a single stage de-icing/anti-icing starts from the begining of the application. In a two stage process it starts from the beginning of the second (anti-icing) application. 
The greater the holdover times the greater the chance the aircraft can become airborne before the anti-icing becomes ineffective. If the holdover time expires the aircraft must taxy back to the ramp and start the de-icing process again. One way round this is to site taxy-through de-icing gantries at the holding point. These are not often seen in Europe but can be found at several North American airports. 
Before any ground de-icing operation is started intake blanks must be fitted where possible to prevent snow, ice and fluids from entering the aircraft systems. Engines and APU can be running but the bleed air valve that supplies cabin conditioning must be 


The holdover time starts at the beginning of the second (anti-ice) application 
Icing Hazards 



Ground De-Icing/Anti-Icing continued 
Any de-icing/anti-icing fluid must be symmetrically applied to the aeroplane. 
Deposits of dry snow can be blown or brushed off the aircraft. Care must be taken to prevent snow from entering the gap between flying control surfaces, flaps, wings and so on as this could freeze later in flight 
For wet snow or ice, brushing and blowing would be ineffectual. If the ambient temperature is -7a° C or above hot water can be used to remove the contaminant. The water is heated to 95a° C and sprayed at a maximum pressure of 100 psi. 
The aircraft must then either be quickly dried or, within 3 minutes, have a chemical anti-icing fluid put on it. 

The hot water method is rarely used because of the risk of it re­freezing. Most usually chemical de-icing fluids are used either neat or mixed with water. When fluids are diluted with water they are always heated, when used neat they are sometimes heated. Intake blanks must be fitted before de-icing is commenced 
There are four types of anti-icing/de-icing fluid but the two most common are ISO types I and II. 
Icing Hazards 
Type I Fluid 
Type I fluid contains a minimum of 80% glycol in a water base, it melts ice very quickly but has a low viscosity and the fluid will run off the aeroplane surfaces rather easily. The low viscosity combined with the diluting effect of melting contaminant and the evaporation of the glycol once it is applied limits its holdover time. 
It is a clear liquid dyed red-orange. Holdover times are shown here.

Type I fluid de-ices well but has a short hold over time 
Icing Hazards 

Type II Fluids 
Type II fluids have a minimum of 50% glycol and a thickening agent in the mix to make the fluid more viscous so it does not run off the airframe too quickly. The thickening agent also reduces the evaporation of the glycol. Type II fluids have a longer hold over time than type I. It is a clear or straw coloured liquid for ease of identification. 
Type II fluid gives a longer hold over time 
Type 11 Fluids can be diluted with water to give a mixture of fluid to water 75/25, 60/40, and 50/50 and may be heated to 70a° C then sprayed against the aircraft at a maximum pressure of 100 psi. 
The heat and pressure will de-ice the aircraft and a layer of glycol will be left on the skin. Ambient conditions at the airport will decide the fluid to water ratio required see table on next page. 


Icing Hazards 
Type II Fluids continued 
Type II fluid can also be used undiluted. This is referred to as "100% cold spray". This gives the longest hold over time of all. In extreme conditions the fluid becomes so viscous that it has be heated up to a maximum of 60° C to allow it to be sprayed. If this temperature is exceeded then the thickening agent forms a gel, which will do more harm than good. Type 
II undiluted fluid will function down toe-25°C. Holdover times are also shown in the table opposite.

Icing Hazards 
Information Given to the Flight Crew 
Once de-icing then anti-icing is complete the de-icing crew should give the flight crew four crucial bits of information, the type and temperature of fluid used, the concentration and the time the application began, usually as a local time. 
Shear Value 
The thicker anti-ice fluids (II and IV) are formulated with a 'shear' value in other words, at a certain airspeed the fluid will shear and roll off the surfaces of the aircraft. The shear value must be lower than rotation speed so that at rotation the surfaces of the wing are clear of fluid. Because of this, Type II and IV fluids may not be suitable for smaller aeroplanes, or for aeroplanes with a low rotation speed. 
Other Fluids 
The other fluids include Type 111, which is a 'one step' treatment relevant to smaller commuter type aeroplanes, and Type IV, which is similar to Type 11. Type IV fluid has an advanced thickening agent so has a longer hold over time. For identification Type Ill is yellow/green, Type IV is emerald green. 


Wheelbay De-Icing 
De-icing of wheel bays must be done either using large volumes of hot air or by hand. Fluids can not be used as they could refreeze in flight and leave the gear locked up. 
After take-off from a contaminated runway, it is a good idea to raise and lower the gear several times to remove slush and snow, but only if the climb profile permits it. 
An aircraft with mudguards or spats must have them removed and cleaned of any ice or snow after landing as this could freeze. An engineer must refit the spats before flight. 

Icing Hazards 
Jet Aircraft Anti-Icing Equipment 
A jet aircraft is usually well protected against icing. The engines have hot air anti-icing from engine bleeds supplied to the intake to prevent ice build-up, some engines also anti-ice the inlet guide vanes. Engine anti-ice can be used on the ground as well as when airborne. 
Nose cowl 
Nose cone 
Intake guide vanes

Air intake manifold

Icing Hazards 

Jet Aircraft Airframe Anti-Icing 

Typical thermal anti-ice system 
The airframe is protected by hot air ducted through the leading edges of the wings and tail. The airframe anti-icing may not be selected on the ground because it requires the cooling airflow over the wings to keep the temperatures down and avoid 'duct overheat' warnings. Some aircraft employ 'squat switches' to avoid the airframe anti-icing being activated on the ground, some manufacturers merely prohibit its use until airborne.

Icing Hazards 

Turbo-Prop Anti-Ice and De-Icing Equipment 
Turbo-prop and piston aircraft are at greater risk from icing in flight than jet aircraft as they operate continuously in the icing levels. 
Engines 
Turbo prop engine intakes can be de-iced with electrical mats that cycle the current to intermittently break off ice deposits. The 

Airflow 
stagnation zone at the lip of the intake is anti-iced with continuous current. 

Air stagnation on intake lip ¦ Continuously heated elements 
¦ Intermittently heated elements 


Icing Hazards 

Airframes 
Airframe de-icing is provided with pneumatic boots that intermittently inflate to break off accumulations of ice. 
Aircraft with super-critical wings can experience aerodynamic problems when rain ice forms a ridge of ice behind and out of reach of the de-icing boots. 


Rain ice can form in a ridge behind the boots 
Icing Hazards 
Propellers 
Propeller de-icing is usually electrical, using intermittently heated mats to break off accumulated ice. 
When the ice comes off the prop it is often flung against the side of the fuselage and the noise can distress the passengers. 


Icing Hazards 
Piston Engined Aircraft Anti-Icing and De-Icing Equipment 
Piston engined aircraft employ substantially the same airframe and propeller de-icing systems as turboprops with a few minor variations discussed in the Aircraft General Knowledge section. 
Piston engines with carburettors are at risk from carburettor icing. Carburettor icing can be reduced or avoided by supplying hot air to the air-intake, this reduces engine power. 


Windscreen Anti-Icing 
Windscreen anti-icing is provided by transparent foil heaters in between the perspex layers of a laminated windscreen. Windscreen heating is thermostatically controlled and should be on all the time, whether in icing conditions or not, as it makes the screen more flexible and resistant to birdstrike damage. 
Night Flying and Icing 
Aeroplanes operated in expected or actual icing conditions at night shall be equipped with a means to illuminate or detect the formation of ice. 
The means to illuminate the formation of ice shall not cause glare or reflection that would handicap crew members in the performance of their duties. 


Lesson 76 
Contaminated Runway Operations 
ATPA OP8 l6 

Contaminated Runway Operations 
Introduction 

Contaminated runway operations present significant problems to operators and pilots attempting to keep a service operating. When they are attempted, safety margins are smaller than in normal operations and pilots rely heavily on accurate reporting of the conditions. 


Contaminated Runway Operations 

Definitions 
As we discovered earlier, a contaminated runway is a runway where more than 25% of the surface area is covered in either: 
¦ 
More than 3 mm of water or its equivalent in wet snow or slush 

¦ 
Compacted snow which will resist further compression and either hold together or break into lumps when picked up or 

¦ 
Ice 


A wet runway has water, slush or snow less than 3 mm or the equivalent depth, or there is enough moisture present to make the surface reflective but without significant areas of standing water. 
Wet runways have less than 3 mm of water, a reflective surface and no significant standing water 
A damp runway has moisture on it but it does not have a reflective surface. 
A dry runway is neither wet nor contaminated and might include special grooved or porous surfaces which maintain dry braking action even when moisture is present. 

Regulations 
EASA regulations do not preclude contaminated runway operations although some National Authorities advise that they should be avoided whenever possible and prohibit take-offs with more than 15 mm of water, wet snow or slush or more than 80 mm of powder snow. 
Contaminated runway operations are allowed under the regulations 
From an operating point of view consideration should be given to the possibility of ingesting contaminant into the engines in such conditions and continuous ignition should be selected to reduce the risk of flame-out. This also applies in heavy rain. 
Contaminated Runway Operations 

The Effect on Aircraft Performance 
All contaminants make the braking action less effective and all, except ice, act to resist acceleration on the take-off run. 
Assessing how much the contaminant affects the aircraft performance is difficult even when the precise depth, type and coverage of the contaminant is known, which it rarely is. 
Many Flight Manuals contain contaminated runway data extrapolated from tests carried out in the USA in the 1960s using a Caravelle aircraft. Some, more modern, aircraft have contaminated runway data that is more appropriate to their type. 

The Take-Off 
The American tests on the Caravelle revealed that, in the early stages of the take-off run, slush and water builds up as a bow wave in front of the wheel adding significantly to the drag. 
Once a certain speed was reached the bow wave disappeared, wheel rotation slowed or stopped and the tyre slithered or planed on a layer of contaminant. From that point on the resistance to acceleration was minimal. 
Contaminated Runway Operations 

The point at which the aircraft starts to plane is called the hydroplaning speed. This can be approximated from one of two formulae: 
For a rotating tyre (take-off): 
Hydroplaning Speed (KT) 

For a non-rotating tyre (landing): 
Hydroplaning Speed (KT) =7.7.(Pe+p) 
Where Pis the tyre pressure in psi and p is the specific gravity (SG) of the contaminant. 
If the tyre pressure is given in bars multiply it by 14.5 to get psi 
If the contaminant is water where the specific gravity has a value of 1 then the speed might be described as the aquaplaning speed and the formula given just as 9. P or 7.7. P without mention of density. 
An obvious difficulty here is finding the specific gravity or slush density of the contaminant, this is not included in information given to pilots. Some authorities publish tables allowing the conversion of the pilots' assessment of the type of contaminant to an equivalent SG. 

Some sources state that for dynamic hydroplaning to occur the contaminant must be deeper than the tyre tread. 
There are two other forms of hydroplaning that should be considered: 
Viscous Hydroplaning 
A thin film of fluid on a smooth runway surface prevents the tyre making contact with the runway. Viscous hydroplaning can occur at much lower speeds that dynamic hydroplaning, and is most likely on a smooth, dirty surface. Runway 'touchdown' areas are likely to provide these conditions -the back rubber deposit offering a 'smooth dirty surface'. 
Reverted Rubber Hydroplaning 
Reverted rubber hydroplaning occurs after a skid, often on the nosewheel which has neither braking nor anti-skid protection. The high temperatures created by friction boil a thin layer of water and vaporised rubber to steam to support the tyre. This is likely if nosewheel steering is used indiscriminately on a slippery runway. 
If the take-off is rejected the contaminant which resisted acceleration now becomes as slippery as sin and braking action is drastically reduced. 
Contaminated Runway Operations 

Braking Action 
Braking action is usually described as a coefficient of friction, where perfect friction has a coefficient of one and less than perfect is less than one. Braking action can also be verbally described as good, medium or poor or described numerically as part of the RCR / SNOWTAM code. It is possible to relate these three reporting systems, see table below. 
Runways that are wet or contaminated assume braking action poor with a coefficient of 0.25 or less, SNOWTAM code 1. 
Braking action POOR is SNOWTAM code 1, a friction reading of 0.25 or less 
Braking Coefficient  Braking Action  SNOWTAM Code  
0.40 and Higher  Good  5  
0.39-0.36  Medium to Good  4  
0.35-0.30  Medium  3  
0.29-0.26  Medium to Poor  2  
0.25 and below  Poor  


Landing 
The principle effect on landing is reduced braking performance. OPS requires an additional 15% factor to be imposed on the landing distance required when the runway is wet or contaminated unless Flight Manual information allows a reduction below this. 
EU-OPS requires an additional 15% added to the LDR on wet or contaminated runways 

Contaminated Runway Operations 

State of Runway Reports 
Prior to November 2021 the state of the runway could be reported in METAR / SPECI by using the MOTNE code or a SNOWTAM. The MOTNE code is copied on the opposite page for those National Authorities that still examine using the FCL 2016 syllabus. The MOTNE code has been replaced by the Global Reporting Format (GRF). 
SNOWTAMs were only issued in winter in a specific format. That format has changed and SNOWTAMS or Runway Condition Reports (RCR) are issued all year round to report contamination, be it water from Tropical Revolving Storms or Snow storms. 

The MOTNE Code 
The FCL Learning objectives 2020 still require identification of hazards from METARS and SNOWTAMS and actions required to mitigate them. Before November 2021 Aerodrome condition information could be obtained from METARS and SPECI which included MOTNE Runway State Groups in the form of an 8 figure number. It is not practical or advisable to memorise the table, a copy will be provided in the FCL exams if required. Some National authorities may still provide questions on these but in reality the information is provided by NOTAM or SNOWTAM. 
As can be seen in the table on the next page, a code starting 88 covers all runways try decoding 88290095. Individual runway states can be seen in the Stockholm example as follows:

Contaminated Runway Operations 

Global Reporting Format (GRF) 
The GRF enables runway surface conditions to be reported in a standardized manner, so that flight crew can accurately determine aircraft take-off and landing performance. The GRF also incorporates the potential to communicate actual runway surface conditions to flight crew in real time and in terms that directly 
relate to aircraft performance data. 
The GRF can be used in all climates and provides a means for aerodrome operators to correctly assess runway surface conditions including rapidly changing conditions such as those experienced during winter or in tropical climates. 
The GRF comprises an assessment by airport operations staff using a Runway Condition Assessment Matrix (RCAM) and the consequent assignment of a Runway Condition Code (RWY CC) ranging from 6 to 0. This code is complemented by a description of the surface contaminant based on type, depth and % coverage for each third of the runway. The code is based on the effect of the runway conditions on aircraft braking. 
The outcome of the assessment and associated RWY CC are transmitted using a Runway Condition Report (RCR) forwarded to air traffic services and the aeronautical information services for dissemination to pilots. 

The pilots will use the RWYCC to determine their aircraft's performance by correlating the code with performance data provided by their aircraft's manufacturer. 
This will help pilots to correctly carry out their landing and take-off performance calculations for wet or contaminated runways. 
The GRF consists of five elements. They are: 
¦ 
Runway Condition Assessment Matrix (RCAM). 

¦ 
Standardized definitions of runway surface conditions. 

¦ 
Standardized definitions of runway surface descriptors. 

¦ 
Runway Condition Code (RWYCC). 

¦ 
Runway Condition Report (RCR). 


Contaminated Runway Operations 

Runway Condition Assessment Matrix (RCAM) 
The RCAM is a means by which airport authorities can assess runway surface conditions. It can be used to identify factors that would affect an aircraft's takeoff and landing performance. Most importantly, it helps evaluate the braking performance of an airplane while on the runway surface. 
Whenever requested by ATC, or If the braking action encountered during the landing roll is not as the RCR the pilot should provide a braking action report to ATS, using an AIREP as soon as possible, this is especially important where the braking action is worse than reported. A braking action AIREP of "Less than Poor" leads to a runway closure until the aerodrome operator can improve runway conditions. 
The terminology in the AIREP is the same as the runway condition codes and downgrade assessment criteria columns in the RCAM table the pilot will report braking action in an AIREP as GOOD, GOOD TO MEDIUM etc the RWYCC numbers are not used.

Contaminated Runway Operations 

The runway surface description aims to show the different effects contaminants have on braking performance based on their depth and OAT. 
For example, wet snow in the RCAM has a different RWYCC based on the depth of the snow. When the depth is at or below 3 mm, the RWYCC is 5 when the depth is more than 3 mm, the RWYCC falls to 3, indicating noticeably reduced braking action and directional control. 
The prevailing temperature or OAT has a similar effect. Compacted snow, when the OAT is-15 degrees or lower, has an RWYCC of 4, but when the OAT is above -15 degrees, the RWYCC falls to 3. 
The RCAM method is the most effective and accurate way to report the runway condition to the pilots as it gives both the contaminant type and depth, significantly affecting an aircraft's braking capability. 
Airports may use friction measuring equipment. These devices are attached to a vehicle, and then as the truck moves over the runway, the equipment records the friction value. This value is known as the Measured Surface Friction and can be reported in 
ITEM S of the RCR. 
Pilots must report to ATS as soon as practicable using an AIREP if braking and directional control conditions are different from those Pilots report braking action using the terminology: Good I Good To Medium I Medium I Medium To Poor I Poor. 

When using the RCAM system to measure runway contaminant, the runway is divided into three segments. 
Runway conditions are conveyed to flight crews using a Runway Condition Report (RCR), also called a SNOWTAM. 
¦ 
Airport: London Heathrow (EGLL), UK, Runway 09L/27R. 

¦ 
Date: January 22, 2024, at 1110 UTC. 

¦ 
First third of the runway: 60% covered by 5 mm of DRY SNOW. 

¦ 
The second third of the runway: 70% covered by 7 mm of DRY SNOW. 

¦ 
Third runway third: 100% covered by 8 mm of DRY SNOW. 


A SNOWTAM for the above data may be written as: 
EGLL 01221110 09L 3/3/3 60/70/100 05/07/08 DRY SNOW/DRY SNOW/DRY SNOW 
The 3 in the SNOWTAM is the RWYCC derived from the RCAM. In our case, it is DRY SNOW with a depth of more than 3 mm which has a code of 3. 
A RWYCC can also be transmitted through the airport's Automatic Terminal Information Service (ATIS). 
Contaminated Runway Operations 

RCR / SNOWTAM 
A special series NOTAM given in a standard format providing a surface condition report notifying the presence or cessation of hazardous conditions due to snow, ice, slush, frost, standing water or water associated with snow, slush, ice or frost on the movement area. 
The maximum validity of an RCR / SNOWTAM is 8 hours when no SNOWTAM is issued after 8 hours of a previous SNOWTAM for 
an aerodrome, the old SNOWTAM is expired, and it is assumed that there is no more significant runway surface condition to be reported. 
When a new SNOWTAM is issued for a specific aerodrome that has another valid SNOWTAM, the new one automatically replaces the older SNOWTAM (there is no need to reference the older SNOWTAM in the new SNOWTAM, as what we do for NOTAM). 
Metric units shall be used in SNOWTAM and the unit of measurement (e.g. mm, cm, m, etc.) should not be reported. 
The standard RCR / SNOWTAM format can be seen on the next page, but note that the letters used to indicate (A to T) are only used for reference and are not included in the messages. 

The letters used in the second column of the template indicate each sections usage, M (mandatory), C (conditional),O (optional) hence the mandatory (minimum) elements of a SNOWTAM are: 
A) Aerodrome. 
B) Date and Time of observation. 
C) Lower runway designator. 
D) Runway condition in thirds using the Runway Condition Assessment Matrix (RCAM). 
G) Condition description over total runway length. 
Contaminated Runway Operations 
RCR / SNOWTAM continued 
The RCR / SNOWTAM can give the minimum mandatory elements above, but section D, using the Runway Contamination Codes (1-6) (shown above) gives important information on the type of contaminant and its affect on aeroplane deceleration or directional control in conjunction with pilot's reports of braking action. 
Further information regarding the percentage of contaminant coverage in each third of the runway is covered, the depth and type of contaminant are listed in items E-G. 
A final "Situational Awareness" which is given in "plain text" gives additional information in items H to T, such as width of runway cleared, the position and heights of snow banks, reduced runway length, anti-icing used and state of taxiways and apron. 
Item T is an optional item and consists of plan language remarks for ease of clarity it is often added to the mandatory items in a short SNOWTAM to aid comprehension.

Lesson 77 
Decompression and Oxygen Systems 
ATPA OPB 77 

Decompression and Oxygen Systems 

Introduction 
Pressure is provided in the aircraft cabin by supplying engine bleed air. The required pressure differential is maintained by a controlled release to the atmosphere. If the release becomes uncontrolled, perhaps because a large hole suddenly appears in the pressure hull as a door or hatch is blown off, or if there are smaller leaks that allow more air out than the engine bleeds can supply then cabin pressurisation will fail. 
If there is a rapid or explosive decompression there will be a loud noise followed by a rushing of air. The water vapour in the cabin may well condense into a fog which will quickly clear, the cabin pressure warning light will be illuminated in the cabin with an audio alert, usually a horn, as the cabin height rises through 10 000 ft and the passenger oxygen masks will be deployed as the cabin altitude rises through 14 000 ft. A slower decompression may well not be noticed by the crew until the cabin altitude warning horn goes off. Masks must be deployed at the latest by 
15 000 ft cabin altitude. 


Slow decompressions are caused by small leaks and outflow valve malfunctions 
Decompression and Oxygen Systems 

Immediate Actions 
The actions in all cases are the same. First go on to oxygen then initiate an emergency descent to a cabin altitude of less than 10 000 ft selecting seat belt and no smoking signs on as soon as possible. 
A commander must ensure that flight crew members engaged in performing duties essential to the safe operation of an aeroplane in flight, use supplemental oxygen continuously whenever cabin altitude exceeds 10 000 ft for a period in excess of 30 minutes and whenever the cabin altitude exceeds 13 000 ft. Supplemental oxygen means the additional oxygen required to protect each occupant against the adverse effects of excessive cabin altitude and to maintain acceptable physiological conditions. 


First go on to oxygen, then start an emergency descent 

Decompression and Oxygen Systems 
Equipment 
Crew 
Masks for flight crew must be available at each crew station, and if operating above 25 000 ft, need to be 'quick donning'. 
Flight crew masks usually have a 'diluter demand' oxygen supply which provides an oxygen and air mix up to 32 000 ft cabin altitude and then 100% oxygen thereafter. The system can be selected to 100% oxygen at any altitude and an 'emergency' selection provides 100% oxygen at an increased pressure. Above 35 000 ft oxygen is automatically supplied under pressure. 
Oxygen is supplied from a single cylinder to all flight crew and the supply is independent of the passenger oxygen system. Crew oxygen supply pressure is indicated on the lower EICAS display when STATUS is selected. 
Masks for cabin crew and portable oxygen units need not be quick donning but must be evenly distributed through the cabin. 


Diluter demand systems provide 100% oxygen at 32 000 ft cabin alt 
Decompression and Oxygen Systems 

Passengers 
Passenger oxygen is supplied by chemical generators or through a cylinder system independent of the flight crew supply. The system can be activated manually from the cockpit or automatically as the cabin altitude rises to between 13 200 and 14 000 ft but no higher than 15 000 ft. 
The masks drop to the 'half hung' position and are only activated when the passenger pulls the mask down to use it. The oxygen supply is continuous and does not fill the plastic 'reservoir bag' just above the mask when used normally. 


Passenger oxygen is activated by a cabin height of 14 000 ft, the masks drop. The oxygen is supplied when they are pulled down further 

Decompression and Oxygen Systems 

Oxygen Requirements 
Pressurised Aircraft 
The EASA regulation requirements for carrying supplemental oxygen in pressurised aeroplanes slightly modify the ICAO requirements we met earlier, see the table. 
The minimum flight crew supply is 30 minutes for aircraft with a certificated ceiling up to 25 000 ft and 2 hours for aircraft certificated to fly above that. 
The minimum supply times given assume a descent from the aircraft's maximum certificated altitude followed by stabilised flight at the altitudes given.
