Lesson 78 
Fire and Smoke 
ATPA OPB 78 
Fire and Smoke 
Introduction 
Fire and smoke is a major hazard on board all aircraft from the smallest Cessna right up to the A380. There are three methods of detecting a fire, visually, by detecting smoke and by detecting high temperatures. 
People are excellent smoke detectors, especially a plane full of worried passengers. Places that are permanently staffed, such as flight decks and passenger cabins, do not require smoke or fire detecting units. These are called as Class A compartments, other areas need smoke detectors. 


Automatic Systems 
There is a requirement for duplication of automatic smoke and fire detection systems. Each detector is able to operate 
independently but they are normally operated in parallel to prevent spurious warnings. Both detectors have to register the smoke or fire before the warning is given. This makes use of an "and" logic gate. 
To achieve this each pair of smoke detectors share a common air supply and two firewires or capillary tubes run side by side to form two loops for each fire detector. 
Fire and Smoke 
Smoke Detection 
Smoke detection is required in: 
¦ 
Unmanned compartments that the crew can access during flight, such as avionics bays. These are called Class B compartments. 

¦ 
Cargo bays and holds that are not accessible have smoke detection and remotely operated fire extinguishers and are called Class C compartments. 

¦ 
Class D compartments, cargo bays with increased fireproofing. If a fire does occur it will not affect the structure of the aircraft, so is left to burn. 

¦ 
Galleys. 

¦ 
Toilets. It is mandatory to have a smoke detector unit in each toilet compartment. The warning can be given on the flight deck or a cabin crew station. 




Fire Detection 
Areas that have fire detection systems are: 
¦ 
Jet engine bays 

¦ 
APU bays 

¦ 
Main gear bays 


Fire detection is required in engine bays, APU bays and wheel bays 
Fire and Smoke 



Jet Engine Zones 

Jet engine bays, nacelles or pods are divided into three zones as Zone 1 shown here. Zone2 
Zone 1 is the area surrounding the hydraulic pumps, ancillary gearbox, engine lubrication oil reservoir, fuel control unit and so on. This is the most likely place for a fire to start and is the only zone that has fire detection and remotely operated fire extinguishers. To reduce the chance of fire spreading the zone is kept at a lower temperature than the other bays by a ventilation system. 
Zone 2 is the area where the compressor blades could touch the engine case and create a metal fire. This would be indicated via the engine vibration monitoring system. This zone is hotter than zone 1. 
Zone 3 is the hottest zone, as it is the area surrounding the engine and jet pipe aft of the combustion chamber. Zone 3 is separated from zone 1 by a fire bulkhead. 
Fire and Smoke 
Pipes in Hot Zones 
If a combustible fluid or gas has to pass through a hot zone then it must be routed through a double skinned pipe (a pipe inside a pipe). 
Auxiliary Power Units 
Auxiliary power units (APUs) are small gas turbine units that provide electricity and compressed air for ground or backup operations. They can be divided into zones in the same way as the main engines, but in most cases the whole bay in which the APU is located is classed as zone 1. APUs have there own fire detection and protection systems, and as they can be left running unattended and during the refuelling operation the protection system can be selected to automatic. APUs are also fitted with an automatic shut down system. A drop in oil pressure and/or increase in vibration will trigger a shutdown. 


Wheel Bays 
Main gear bays are given overheat detection systems in case an overheated brake unit causes a fire. If there is a fire warning there is no extinguishing system available, the checklist or quick reference handbook (QRH) will call for speed to be reduced below the gear operating speed, VLO, and the gear to be lowered to blow the fire out. 
Overheated brakes on the ground are prone to exploding if normal water or foam extinguishant is applied to them, because of this 
risk they should never be approached from the side. Sand or dry powder extinguishers used to be used on brake fires, nowadays a special water mist is applied. 
Fire and Smoke 

Smoke Detection Systems 
Smoke detection systems can be either based on optical principles, where smoke in the air scatters light, or on ion detection. 

Ion Detection Systems 
Ion detection systems contain a small quantity of radioactive material that bombards the oxygen atoms in the air with alpha particles to create ions, charged gas molecules, in a small chamber. The ions that have been created allow a small current to flow through the air from one side of the chamber to the other. 
When smoke particles are present they absorb the alpha particles without ionising and cause the current to fall. The fall in current is sensed and a warning light or audio alarm is set off. 
Fire and Smoke 

Optical Systems 
The second, and probably most common, system relies on the idea that smoke particles scatter light, this is the labyrinth smoke detector. 
The unit is located in an air extraction duct where a fan draws a constant flow of air through it. A beacon lamp is located upstream of the 'labyrinth' with is a photoelectric cell at the other end. 
Under normal circumstances the labyrinth prevents the light from the beacon lamp reaching the photoelectric cell. In the event of a fire starting in the protected area smoke and fumes are given off. As these pass through the labyrinth they allow the light to reflect and refract on to the photoelectric cell creating a current. Detection of this current sends a fire warning signal to the flight deck. 
To test the smoke detector the pilot closes the test switch. This operates a relay switch that routes the power to a test lamp located behind the photoelectric cell. With test selected the lamps are wired in series so the complete circuit is tested. 
The electrical supply for the system is 28 volts DC from the vital services or battery busbar. 

Pulsed infra-red LED Photo-electric 
beacon lamp 

Alarm 
Light proof chamber 


Fire and Smoke 
Immediate Actions 
Often the immediate actions are type-specific, such as the non operating pilot dons his smoke mask and then takes control whilst the other pilot dons his smoke mask the pilot should know the procedures relevant to his aircraft and type. 
If smoke is detected in the cabin in flight the crew should don smoke masks immediately and attempt to locate the source. If the source is a galley fire the cabin crew should attempt to extinguish it using BCF fire extinguishers. 
If the source is in the cabin furnishing the crew should extinguish the fire first with BCF then water to damp the fire down. If it is electrical use BCF to douse the flames while the flight crew attempt to isolate the electrical supply. 
If smoke is coming through the air conditioning system, perhaps from the engines, the first action is to turn off the ACS packs or close the pack flow control valve. 
If smoke is entering the cabin through the air conditioning system turn it off 


If smoke is detected in the cabin on the ground the best course of action is to evacuate the aircraft. The effects of toxic smoke must not be under estimated, it disables in seconds and kills in minutes. No airlines supply smoke hoods for passengers. Passenger oxygen masks are useless in combating smoke as they draw in toxic cabin air, a damp handkerchief is better protection. 
Cabin crew do have some smoke protection. Crew portable oxygen cylinders are fitted with full face smoke masks and provide a supply of 100% oxygen on demand or continuous pressure breathing. More modern aircraft supply smoke hoods for crew, sometimes called personal breathing equipment (PBE). 
Fire and Smoke 
Crew Smoke Protection 

Cabin Crew 
Cabin crew are provided with smoke hoods (PBE) which cover the entire head. 
Some smoke hoods use a filtration system to remove toxic smoke from the air. The more common type illustrated here have a continuous flow of oxygen provided from a small bottle of gas. 
The oxygen is activated by pulling a toggle and provides about 15 minutes supply. 

Fire and Smoke 

Flight Crew 
If smoke is detected in the cockpit the flight crew will don oxygen masks, select 100% oxygen and the put on smoke goggles. The goggles are designed to fit over the oxygen mask and are ventilated by the oxygen supply to the mask. The ventilation switch is a sliding switch over the nose which is opened by sliding it down as the mask is fitted. 


Immediate actions -put on oxygen mask and goggles, select 100% oxygen 
Fire and Smoke 
Fire Detection Systems 

There are four different designs for fire detection units these are designed to react to general heating, temperatures between 175 ­800 °F, over a wide area and hot spot heating, temperatures between 800 -1200 °F, over a small area. 
The systems are: 
¦ 
Resistive continuous firewire system -Kidde and Fenwal 

¦ 
Capacitive continuous firewire system -Kidde and Fenwal 

¦ 
Gas pressure system -Systron Donner 

¦ 
Thermocouple system 





Fire and Smoke 
Resistive Fire Detection 
A fire detection system is made up of two independent loops of firewire supported by a central structure, either a piece of thicker wire or a more rigid tube. 
The two loops are usually referred to on control panels as loop A and loop B, both have to be activated for a fire to be reported. 
A cross section of each resistive firewire shows a fine central electrode surrounded by filler material inside a capillary tube. The two ends of the wire are brought together at a junction which is linked to an arrangement of diodes called a diode bridge or a "Wheatstone bridge". The capillary tube is earthed to the aircraft's structure. 
When the system is switched on a 28 volt DC electrical potential will be present in the central core but as this an endless loop no current will flow. In normal situations when the area to be protected is within its normal operating temperature range this is how the system will remain.

Fire and Smoke 
Resistive Fire Detection continued 
As the temperature increases in a fire the resistance of the filler material drops, allowing the electricity to track to earth via the capillary tube. This creates a flow of electricity that unbalances the bridge, which allows a signal to be sent to the appropriate fire warning panel and triggers the warning. 
As any earthing out of the central electrode will activate this system, should the firewire be crushed in any way it can cause a false fire warning, as the central core and the capillary tube touch. 
When system test is selected the detection loop is earthed out and a continuous red light illuminates on the appropriate panel the aural warning is muted. The test is for the integrity of the whole system.

To fire warning 
¦

the resistance system. In this system, however, the central 
electrode is connected to a charging and measuring unit. 
The charging unit charges the central electrode for a set length of time then discharges it into the measuring unit, where its capacitance is compared with a reference value. 
In a fire the capacitance of the system increases as the temperature increases. The measuring unit senses this and sends a signal to the fire warning panel. 
Once again, if the firewire is crushed it will earth out but now the loss of capacitance will prevent a fire warning from being given. This is called a no fault fire detection system. 
The capacitive system is generally preferred to the resistance system on the basis that no fire warning is better than a spurious one, a fire will probably show itself in other ways. The system test, when operated, alters the capacitance and a continuous red light illuminates on the appropriate panel. The aural warning is muted.

Fire and Smoke 

Thermocouple System 
Thermocouples are loops of wire made of two different metals joined end to end. One join is placed where the temperature is high, the hot junction, and one in the cockpit where the temperature is low, the cold junction. The difference in temperature causes a current to flow through the loop of wire. 
If there is a fire the current flow will increase for those thermocouples with their hot junction in the fire. The difference in current between these and a reference thermocouple is enough to operate a relay that, in turn, activates the warning system. 
The weakness with this system is that should the fire burn through the interconnecting cable, the system will fail completely. Testing is carried out by heating one of the thermocouples. 


Fire and Smoke 
Overheat Sensors 
Many aircraft have both fire and overheat warnings originating from the same engine fire detection system, the distinction between warnings being only one of temperature. 
Separate overheat sensors are used in ducts carrying hot bleed air, typically for airframe anti-icing, and occasionally in engine zone 3, around the jet pipe. 
Overheat sensors work on the principle of differential expansion as heat is applied. 
A tube which expands easily has a pair of low expansion rate spring strips inside, each with a contact. These remain open until the outer tube gets hot. As it expands it stretches and pulls the springs together to close the contacts and make the warning circuit. 
Overheat detectors are wired in parallel. 


Toilet Fire Extinguishers 
Fitted in the toilet compartment under the sink unit is a small fire bottle which has two nozzles, one directed into the waste bin and one under the sink. The nozzle is sealed with a low melting point metal which when heated melts and automatically discharges freon or BCF. The preferred fire-extinguishing agent is BCF (bromo chloro difluoro methane), this is also known as halon 1211 or freon. 
A placard mounted inside the waste bin door has silver spots, these turn black as the temperature rises to indicate how hot the fire was. 
The fire extinguisher system is not connected to the smoke warning system in the toilets. 
Fire and Smoke 

Fixed Fire Extinguishers with Squibs 

Fixed fire extinguishers consist of bottles made of copper or steel

A single operating head will only deliver the fire-extinguishing agent to one area. To save weight by reducing the number of fire

Fire and Smoke 
Fire Warnings 

When the detecting units sense a fire, a signal is sent to the flight deck. The warning given will be one or more of these: 
¦ 
Red flashing attention-getters 

¦ 
An aural horn or chime 

¦ 
A continuous red light on the central warning panel or, on an EFIS aircraft, a master fire warning light in front of the pilots 

¦ 
A continuous red light in the appropriate panel or fire handle 

¦ 
ECAM and EICAS warning messages 


Attention getters and aural warnings can be cancelled but the continuous red lights will remain on until the fire is out. 


Fire and Smoke 
Master Fire Warnings 
Most aircraft have a master fire warning light in clear view of the pilots. Shown in the diagram opposite are the lights on the glareshield panel of a 8737. 
When any fire warning is set off the master fire warning light will illuminate and a bell will sound. Pressing the light silences the bell. 
Once the bell is silenced the checklist for the appropriate fire warning and the specific fire warning lights will also go out. 
Delayed Fire Warnings 
Certain fire and smoke warnings may be suppressed at critical stages of flight such as just after rotation and just before landing and announced only when the aircraft is in a safer situation. This prevents the pilot being distracted from the primary task of flying the aeroplane. 


Fire and Smoke 

Cargo Bay Fire Protection 
Cargo bays use optical smoke detectors, several to each cargo bay. The system below is from a B737NG. There are two fire bottles, each with nozzles and squibs to discharge into either bay, the green lights under the DET FAIL lights indicate the integrity of the squibs. 
• 

The cargo fire warning consists of a bell, the master fire warning light, a continuous red FIRE light on the panel above and amber DET lights indicating which sensors have gone off. 
Pulling and rotating the arming switch will arm the squibs of all the extinguisher nozzles that discharge into the affected bay the green ARMED lights will illuminate under the guarded discharge switches. 
The first extinguisher is discharged by lifting the guard on the firing switch and pressing the button operation of this switch fires the squib and an amber bottle DSCH light will illuminate in the switch. 
If the second bottle is required then pressing its firing button will also discharge it into the affected bay, no extra arming is required. When TEST is selected a fault in a smoke detector illuminates the amber FAIL lights.

Fire and Smoke 
Engine Fire Extinguisher Operation 
The actions taken in the event of an engine fire may be type­specific and should be known by the pilot but a general example follows. 
The engine fire warnings are usually located on the overhead panel. An engine fire is detected by the firewire loops sensing an overheat. It is indicated by the master fire warning light and bell and by a continuous red warning light in the fire handle of the appropriate engine. 
The first of the immediate actions is to close the throttle, then turn the fuel and ignition off, then pull the fire handle. 
Pulling the fire handle out isolates the engine from the fuel, hydraulics, electrics, and engine bleeds and simultaneously arms the firing circuit, in some aircraft the audible warning is also silenced. 
Referring to the diagram opposite, if the fire was in no.a1 engine the no.a1 handle would be pulled and then turned to the left to fire squib A. 
There is normally sufficient discharge from one bottle to put the fire out and prevent re-ignition as the area cools. When the fire is out the light in the fire handle and the master fire warning light will also go out. 


Sql;libs 
............................... , 

Pulling the fire handle cuts off the fuel, turning it fires the extinguisher 

Fire and Smoke 
Engine Fire Extinguisher Operation continued 
If one bottle fails to extinguish the fire then, normally after 30 seconds, the second bottle can be fired. In this case turning the handle to the right would fire squib C. Even if one bottle has put out the fire the pilot may discharge the second bottle into the engine bay as a precaution just before landing. 
Test functions are available for each engine fire warning system. When selected the fire handle will be illuminated and the audible warning will sound. 
The overheat warnings can also be tested. Three green lights at the bottom right hand corner of the panel show the integrity of the squibs and firing circuits. 
The OVHT DET switch in the top left corner of the panel can be selected to A to make loop A the active fire detection loop or to B to make loop B active. 
The NORMAL selection requires both loops to be activated for a fire warning to be given. 
With the switch in NORMAL if a fault is sensed in one loop that loop is automatically de-selected and the remaining loop operates on its own. If both loops fail, or if a single selected loop fails, the FAULT light illuminates. 


Sq1:1ibs 
B 


In NORMAL operation both loops A and B must be activated to give a fire warning 
Fire and Smoke 
Jet Pipe Fires 
Fires in this area are rare nowadays as most high bypass engines only have very short jet pipes. If there is a jet pipe fire on start up there are normally no indications other than from the ground crew jumping up and down and from excessive TGT. The actions vary from type to type but most involve cutting the fuel supply by turning the fuel and ignition off and then re-engaging the starter motor and dry cranking the engine to blow the fire out. 
If there is a jet pipe fire when the engine is stopped, fit the intake and jet pipe blanks. 


If there is a jet pipe fire on start turn the fuel and ignition off and dry crank the engine 
Fire and Smoke 
APU Fire Protection 
The APU fire warning is on the same panel as the main engine fire warnings and works in a similar way except that there is only one extinguisher bottle that can be fired by turning the fire handle either way. 
When a fire is detected the APU has an automatically shuts down. The APU fire warning light and the master fire warning light illuminate and the fire warning bell sounds. 
On the ground the APU fire warnings are repeated with a warning horn in the main wheel well and a flashing APU fire warning light. The aural warnings can also be silenced and the fire extinguisher activated from a panel in the wheel well. 
In the B737 both main engine and APU fire detection systems use systron donner gas pressure activated detectors. 


Piston Engine Carburettor Fires 
Carburettor fires can occur in piston engined aircraft as a result of either over enthusiastic priming or component failure. Actions taken in the event of a carburettor fire may be type specific and should be known by the pilot but the following is an example. 
There is no fire extinguisher in the engine bay so if the engine has started, keep it running, if not move the mixture control to ICO (idle cut-off), open the throttle fully and continue to operate the starter motor. 
If the fire goes out run the motor for a while to make sure it really is out then shut it down and evacuate the aircraft. The engine bay will need to be inspected before flight. 
If the fire does not go out carry out the drills for engine fire on the ground which will include fuel off, electrics off, brakes off and evacuate the aircraft. 
Fire and Smoke 
Brake fires can occur after an heavy-weight landing, a rejected take-off, or as a result of bad handling. 
Large aircraft brake fires are fought by the airport fire section. With modern aircraft and modern brake materials ( carbon or composite materials) the approach to aircraft brake fires has changed. 
On reaching an aircraft with hot brakes, for instance after a rejected take-off, the fire crew will assess the situation. If the brakes are just hot, the first aim will be to cool them. Possible methods include fan units, or a water mist or fog so which will cool the brakes without introducing a massive thermal shock. If the fire crew arrive and the tyre fuse plugs have already blown, or if there is already a fire, the need for caution has passed, the fire crew will now just hose the wheels and brakes with water, preferably from the turret of the fire engine, or certainly using specialist protective equipment. If the brakes are burning, then it is a bit late to worry about thermal shock! 
Historically, the wording of exam questions concerning brake fires has been misleading. For example, 'how would you fight an aircraft brake fire' has been answered 'use a dry powder extinguisher'. Possibly, in a small single engine aircraft this could help: dry powder will certainly knock down any flames. However, on a large passenger aircraft this 


Fire and Smoke 
Steel and Carbon Brakes 
Steel brake wear is directly proportional to the kinetic energy absorbed by the brakes. Maximum steel brake life can be achieved during taxi by using a large number of small, light brake applications, allowing some time for brake cooling between applications. High airplane gross weights and high brake application speeds tend to reduce steel brake life because they require the brakes to absorb a large amount of kinetic energy. 
Carbon brake wear is primarily dependent on the total number of brake applications -one firm brake application causes less wear than several light applications. Maximum carbon brake life can be achieved during taxi by using a small number of long, moderately firm brake applications instead of numerous light brake applications. 


This can be achieved by allowing taxi speed to increase from below target speed to above target speed, then using a single firm brake application to reduce speed below the target and repeating if required, rather than maintaining a constant taxi speed using numerous brake applications. Carbon brake wear is much less sensitive to airplane weight and speed than steel brake wear. 
Fire and Smoke 
Classification of Fires 
Fires are classed by type, there is no common European classification, the following conforms to ISO 3941: 
¦ 
Class A Fires involving solid materials, usually of an organic nature, in which combustion normally takes place with the formation of glowing 

¦ 
Class B Fires involving liquids or liquefiable solids 

¦ 
Class C Fires involving gases 

¦ 
Class D Fires involving metals 




Fire and Smoke 
Hand Held Fire Extinguishers 
Hand held fire extinguishers are coloured red with a label that indicates their application. There may also be a coloured panel corresponding to their type, see opposite. 
Water, or H2O, extinguishers are found in the aircraft cabin together with BCF/halon (or approved equivalent) units. Water extinguishers are used on Class A fires and no others. 
BCF/halon 1211 is an agent which leaves no deposit and extinguishes flames but does not remove any heat from the fire. Halon can be used on electrical fires and could also be used on Class A, B and C fires but there is a danger a hot fire may re­ignite. The use of halon in new extinguishers has been banned and it will gradually be replaced in aircraft systems by new 'clean agents' like halotron 1 and FE-36TM. Under EASA Regulations, HALON is not approved, and the rules simply say the type of extinguisher needs to be appropriate to the type of fire likely to be encountered. However, as with any change, it takes time to take place, so from January 2015 no new aircraft will be allowed HALON, then from January 2018, HALON will not be permitted in any aircraft. 

. 
Only in the One in the passenger cockpit, the rest cabin in the cabin 

Fire and Smoke 
Hand Held Fire Extinguishers continued 
Carbon dioxide or CO2 extinguishers are not found on aircraft. CO2 leaves no residue, it may be used on Class B, C or electrical fires. CO2 reacts with burning metals and may not be used on Class D fires. 
Dry powder, or dry chemical extinguishers are also not found on aircraft. They are more effective than halon and CO2 in that they extinguish the flames and then leave a blanket of powder residue on the fire to prevent re-ignition. The residue is corrosive, and for this reason dry powder is not recommended for use on aircraft fires with the exception of brake and wheel fires when a special dry powder formula is applied by the fire service alternating with water mist. 

. 
Only in the One in the passenger cockpit, the rest cabin in the cabin 

Fire and Smoke 
Fighting Cockpit and Cabin Fires 
Cabin Soft Furnishing Fires 
Use an appropriate extinguisher to put out the flames, then use a water extinguisher to damp down the heat source. 
Cabin Hard Furnishing Fires 
The source of the fire is likely to be electrical and may well be hidden behind trim panels and overhead lockers. When it is identified use an appropriate extinguisher. 


Cockpit Fires 
Cockpit fires are fought with BCF/halon (or approved equivalent) extinguishers which leave no residue. On the ground CO2 extinguishers may also be used. 
Toilet Fires 
To fight a toilet fire, use a BCF/halon (or approved equivalent) extinguisher. Open the door just enough to get the nozzle in, fully discharge the extinguisher then close the door. After a few minutes check the fire is out. Check the bin. 


Lesson 79 
Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 
ATPA OP8 l9 

Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 
Introduction 

This section discusses the outline requirements for emergency landings and ditchings, including the requirement for most large aircraft to have a fuel jettison system. In reality the emergency landing and ditching drills vary by aircraft type and, to some extent, by operator. Should you ever find yourself in such a situation you will be operating from an operator approved checklist. 
Fuel Jettison 
A fuel jettison system is required where, minutes following a take­off at MCTOM, it can jettison sufficient fuel within 15 minutes to reduce the aircraft mass in an emergency such that it is capable of meeting the go-around climb requirement: 2.7% in approach configuration with one engine inoperative and 3.2% in landing configuration with all engines operative. 
Additional certification requirements are that the system does not cause a fire hazard, that the discharged fuel stays clear of the aircraft, that fumes do not enter the cabin and that there are no adverse control effects. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 



Fuel Jettison procedure 
Unless it is shown that using any means (including flaps, slots and slats) for changing the airflow across or around the wings does not adversely affect fuel jettisoning, there must be a placard, adjacent to the jettisoning control, to warn flight-crew members against jettisoning fuel while the means that change the airflow are being used. 
When an aircraft operating within controlled airspace needs to dump fuel, the flight crew shall advise ATC. The ATC unit should then coordinate with the flight crew the following: 
¦ 
the route to be flown, which, if possible, should be clear of cities and towns, preferably over water and away from areas where thunderstorms have been reported or are expected 

¦ 
the level to be used, which should be not less than 1 800 m (6 000 ft) and 

¦ 
the duration of the fuel dumping. 


Jettison stops automatically with enough fuel remaining to climb to 10 000 ft followed by 45 minutes cruise 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 
Fuel Jettison procedure continued 
During the jettison HF radio calls must be avoided and VHF calls kept to a minimum. Electrical switching should likewise be kept to a minimum and, if it is possible, there should be visual confirmation that jettison has started and, more importantly, stopped. Automatic switches should ensure that jettison ceases when the fuel in tanks is just enough for a take-off and climb to 10 000 ft with 45 minutes cruise. 
Fuel jettison procedures are outlined in the checklist which forms part of the Flight Manual. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 
Precautionary and Emergency Landings 

Precautionary Landings 
A precautionary landing should be carried out when it is wise to terminate the flight, but there is no immediate danger to the aircraft. Precautionary landings may be urgent enough to warrant an urgency call (PAN PAN, PAN PAN, PAN PAN), but by definition not a MAYDAY. An example might be a landing following a contained engine failure with no fire indications. 
A precautionary landing would seem like a normal landing to the passengers, except that they might see the emergency services. ATC would have been informed and it is likely that the fire crew would have been called to follow the aircraft down the runway on landing and attend it when clear. The fire chief will normally contact the flight crew by external intercom to be briefed by the Captain, and would inspect the aircraft for signs of damage or fire. Assuming all is clear, the aircraft would taxy to the gate for a normal disembarkation. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 

Emergency Landings 
An emergency landing is required where there is risk to the aircraft and its contents, perhaps a fire, and would require a MAYDAY call. Precautionary landings can deteriorate into emergency landings. 
As the definition of MAYDAY includes the contents of the aircraft, the passengers and crew, a medical emergency such as a heart attack on board might necessitate an emergency landing that 
need not be dramatic for either the crew or the remainder of the passengers. 
Other emergency landings will result from a systems failure that increases the risk to the majority of the passengers. This would ideally require a full passenger briefing on the PA. 
The Captain should call the number 1 or purser to the flight deck and brief him or her on the situation and his intentions. The number 1 will then brief the cabin crew. The Captain may make an initial PA informing the passengers of the general situation and the full passenger briefing will then be left to the number 1. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 

Emergency Landings continued 
The full emergency landing briefing will remind the passengers of the location and use of emergency exits and escape slides. If a landing on water is contemplated use of the life vests will be described and it will be emphasised that they should not be inflated inside the cabin. The cabin crew will brief on the 'brace' position and when it should be adopted and ensure seat backs are upright, seat belts fastened, loose objects stowed and high heeled shoes are removed. 
Just before touchdown the flight crew will make the PA call "BRACE, BRACE". When the aircraft comes to a halt and the engines shut down the flight crew will initiate the evacuation with either a PA or a signal from the flight deck. The cabin crew will open the doors in 'automatic' deploying the slide rafts and assist the passenger evacuation. 
After evacuation the crew should ensure the passengers move clear of the aircraft. 
Air transport aircraft of 44 seats or more must be able to be evacuated in 90 seconds through 50% of the available exits. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 

Emergency Lighting 
Internal emergency lighting consists of floor lights no more than 
40 inches apart along the aisle, floor proximity escape path marking, a light at each exit point and a light at each aisle junction. 
Externally there is a light illuminating the area around the slide to assist in evacuation. 
This diagram shows the emergency internal and external lighting of a typical aircraft with the slides deployed. 
These lights are operated from the battery or vital services busbar and have two switches. The flight crew have an ON -ARMED ­OFF switch, the cabin crew have ON -OFF. The cabin crew's switch can override the flight crew's switch. 
When in flight, the flight deck switch is put in the armed position, and a loss of AC power will then automatically switch on the emergency lights. Once activated the lights will operate for 10 minutes from their own power source. 


Fuel Jettison and Emergency Landings 

Ditching 
Ditching characteristics are allegedly established during the certification procedure although this tends to be done with models rather than the real aircraft. 
It is recommended that a landing is attempted along the line of the swell unless the wind is over 35 to 40 KT in which case one should land into wind. 
After ditching the aircraft may not stay afloat for long so the evacuation into the slide rafts should be quick and orderly. If the slide rafts do not inflate automatically the bottles of compressed air can be triggered by pulling a manual inflation handle. 
Once the crew have confirmed the aircraft is empty the life rafts are separated from the aircraft. Each life raft should have at least one crew member on board. 
Evacuation procedures are detailed in the Operations Manual. 

CS 25 Evacuation Procedures 
Each crew and passenger area must have emergency means to allow rapid evacuation in crash landings, with the landing gear extended as well as with the landing gear retracted, considering the possibility of the aeroplane being on fire. 
For aeroplanes having a seating capacity of more than 44 passengers, it must be shown that the maximum seating capacity, including the number of crew members required by the operating rules for which certification is requested, can be evacuated from the aeroplane to the ground under simulated emergency conditions within 90 seconds. Compliance with this requirement must be shown by actual demonstration. 
Evacuation procedures must be detailed in the Operations Manual 


Lesson 20 
Dangerous Goods 
ATPA OP8 20 
Dangerous Goods 
Introduction 
Dangerous goods are defined as "articles or substances which are capable of posing a significant risk to health, safety or property when transported by air". Many items routinely found on aircraft would come into this category and they are excluded from the regulations, we shall list some of them later on. An operator requires approval from the State of Registration in order to carry dangerous goods by air, this approval may be temporary or permanent. 


Definitions 
As ever, before we start the discussion, here are a list of definitions these definitions are from Annex 18 and EASA OPS. 
Dangerous goods. Articles or substances which are capable of posing a risk to health, safety, property or the environment and which are shown in the list of dangerous goods in the Technical Instructions or which are classified according to those 
Instructions. 
Dangerous goods accident. An occurrence associated with and related to the transport of dangerous goods by air which results in fatal or serious injury to a person or major property damage. 
Dangerous goods incident. An occurrence, other than a dangerous goods accident, associated with and related to the transport of dangerous goods by air, not necessarily occurring on board an aircraft, which results in injury to a person, property damage, fire, breakage, spillage, leakage of fluid or radiation or other evidence that the integrity of the packaging has not been maintained. Any occurrence relating to the transport of dangerous goods which seriously jeopardizes the aircraft or its occupants is also deemed to constitute a dangerous goods incident. 
Dangerous Goods 
Exemption. An authorization issued by an appropriate national authority providing relief from the provisions of this Annex (Annex 18). 
Incompatible. Describing dangerous goods which, if mixed, would be liable to cause a dangerous evolution of heat or gas or produce a corrosive substance. 
Packaging. Receptacles and any other components or materials necessary for the receptacle to perform its containment function. 
Note.-For radioactive material, see Technical Instructions Part 2. 
UN number. The four-digit number assigned by the United Nations Committee of Experts on the Transport of Dangerous Goods to identify a substance or a particular group of substances. 
Acceptance checklist means a document used to assist in carrying out a check on the external appearance of packages of dangerous goods and their associated documents to determine that all appropriate requirements have been met with 
Technical instructions (Tl) means the latest effective edition of the ICAO DOC 9284 'Technical instructions for the safe transport of dangerous goods by air', including the supplement and any addenda, approved and published by the International Civil Aviation Organisation Overpack, for the purpose of transporting dangerous goods, means an enclosure used by a single shipper to contain one or more packages and to form one handling unit for convenience of handling and stowage. 


Package, for the purpose of transporting dangerous goods, means the complete product of the packing operation consisting of the packaging and its contents prepared for transport. 
Packaging, for the purpose of transporting dangerous goods, means receptacles and any other components or materials necessary for the receptacle to perform its containment function. 
Dangerous Goods 
Legislation 

The general regulations for handling and transporting dangerous goods by air are laid down in ICAO Annex 18. ICAO also produce Doc 9284, Technical Instructions for the Safe Transport of Dangerous Goods by Air, which contains all the detailed handling and packaging information. This title is abbreviated to Technical 
Instructions (Tl). 
Individual States, in our case EASA, lay down operating procedures which are found in EASA OPS (Annex V Part SPA, Subpart G, Transport of Dangerous Goods). Lastly, the operating procedures that we need as flight crew will be found in Part A of the operations manual -these procedures are written by the operator, approved by the competent authority, and are based on the documents we have already mentioned.

Dangerous Goods 
Operator Approval 
Annex 18 states that each Contracting State shall take the necessary measures to achieve compliance with the detailed provisions contained in the Technical Instructions for the Safe Transport of Dangerous Goods by Air (Doc 9284 ). 
In turn, EASA OPS CAT.GEN.MPA.200 Transport of Dangerous Goods, states: 
Dangerous goods shall only be transported by an operator approved in accordance with Annex V (Part-SPA), Subpart G, except when: 
¦ 
they are not subject to the technical instructions in accordance with Part 1 of those instructions or 

¦ 
they are carried by passengers or crew members, or are in baggage, in accordance with Part 8 of the technical instructions. 


An operator shall establish procedures to ensure that all reasonable measures are taken to prevent dangerous goods from being carried on board inadvertently. 
The operator shall provide personnel with the necessary information enabling them to carry out their responsibilities, as required by the technical instructions. 


The operator shall, in accordance with the technical instructions, report without delay to the competent authority and the appropriate authority of the State of occurrence in the event of: 
¦ 
any dangerous goods accidents or incidents 

¦ 
the discovery of undeclared or misdeclared dangerous goods in cargo or mail or 

¦ 
the finding of dangerous goods carried by passengers or crew members, or in their baggage, when not in accordance with Part 8 of the technical instructions. 


The operator shall ensure that passengers are provided with information about dangerous goods in accordance with the technical instructions. 
The operator shall ensure that notices giving information about the transport of dangerous goods are provided at acceptance points for cargo as required by the technical instructions 
Dangerous Goods 
Classification 
In general, dangerous goods are divided into various classes or divisions according to the hazard they present. A detailed list of individual commodities is shown in Tl Part 3 Dangerous Goods List which indicates the class or division into which each commodity falls as well as its acceptability for transport by air and under what conditions. 
Since such a list cannot be exhaustive, it also includes various generic or "not otherwise specified" entries to assist in the transport of those commodities not specifically listed by name. 

Dangerous Goods Classes and Divisions 
Class 1: Explosives 
Division 1.1: Substances and articles which have a mass explosion hazard 
Division 1.2: Substances and articles which have a projection hazard but not a mass explosion hazard 
Division 1.3: Substances and articles which have a fire hazard and either a minor blast hazard or a minor projection hazard or both, but not a mass explosion hazard 
Division 1.5: Veryinsensitive substances which have a mass explosion hazard 
Division 1.6: Extremely insensitive articles which do not have a mass explosion hazard 
Class 2: Gases 
Division 2.1: Flammable gases 
Division 2.2: Non-flammable, non-toxic gases Division 2.3: Toxic gases 
Class 3: Flammable liquids 
Class 4: Flammable solids; substances liable to spontaneous combustion; substances which, on contact with water, emit flammable gases Division 4.1: Flammable solids, self-reactive and related substances and desensitized explosives Division 4.2: Substances liable to spontaneous combust on
i 
Division 4.3: Substances which, in contact with water, emit flammable gases 
Class 5: Oxidizing substances and organic peroxides 
Division 5.1: Oxidizing substances 
Division 5.2: Organic peroxides 
Class 6: Toxic and infectious substances Division 6.1: Toxic substances Division 6.2: Infectious substances 
Class 7: Radioactive material 
Class 8: Corrosive substances 
Class 9: Miscellaneous dangerous substances and articles, including environmentally hazardous substances 
Dangerous Goods 
Classification continued 
It should be noted that the numerical order of the classes and divisions is not that of the degree of danger this is specified by a separate category, the packing group. Packing group 1 represents great danger, group 2 medium danger and group 3 minor danger. 
Each class or division has a specific label that will be on the package to warn handlers of the danger.

Many of the column headings are self-explanatory, but some explanatory notes are below: 
Column 2 -this column contains the serial number assigned to the article or substance under the United Nations classification system. 
Column 3 -"Class or division" -this column contains the class or division and in the case of Class 1, the compatibility group assigned to the article or substance according to the classification system described in Tl Part 2. 
Column 4 -"Subsidiary risk" -this column contains the class or division number of any important subsidiary risks which have been identified by applying the classification found in Tl Part 2. 
Column 5 -"Labels" -this column specifies the class hazard label followed by the subsidiary risk label(s) (aftera"&") to be applied to each outside packaging and overpack. 
Column 6 -this column shows State variations from these Instructions (detailed in Tl). 
Column 7 -this column shows special provisions which are listed in Tl Part 3.

described in Tl Part 4 which indicates the maximum quantity per inner and outer packaging for transporting dangerous goods as excepted quantities. 
Column 10 -"Passenger and cargo aircrafta-Packing instruction" ­this column refers to the relevant packing instructions listed in Part 4 for transport of the article or substance on a passenger aircraft. For some entries an alternative packing instruction is shown prefixed with the letter "Y". Such packing instructions are for limited quantities of dangerous goods. 
Column 11 -"Passenger and cargo aircrafta-Maximum net quantity per package" -this column shows the maximum net quantity (mass or volume) of the article or substance allowed in each package for transport on a passenger aircraft. Where a maximum net quantity appears beside a packing instruction prefixed by the letter "Y", this indicates it is the maximum net quantity permitted in a packaging containing limited quantities of dangerous goods unless indicated by a letter "G" where the mass quoted is the total mass of the package.

Dangerous Goods 
Limited Quantities 
The dangerous goods rules contain provisions for limited quantities of dangerous goods. These recognize that many dangerous goods when in reasonably limited quantities present a reduced hazard during transport and can safely be carried in good quality packaging of the types specified in the recommendations but which have not been tested and marked accordingly. 
The net quantity per package must not exceed the quantity specified in column 11 of the dangerous goods list against the packing instruction number identified by the prefix letter "Y" in column 10. 


Excepted Quantities 
Small quantities of dangerous goods, as defined in Tl Part 3, are excepted from certain provisions of Tis subject to certain conditions. 
If it is desired to transport the substance or article under the provisions for excepted quantities, all the requirements of Tl Part 3 Chapter 5 must be met. The substance or article will then not be subject to any of the other requirements of the Tl other than those listed in this chapter. 
Dangerous Goods 
In-flight Emergency 
If an in-flight emergency occurs, the pilot-in-command shall, as soon as the situation permits, inform the appropriate air traffic services unit, for the information of aerodrome authorities, of any dangerous goods on board the aircraft, as provided for in the Technical Instructions. This will enable the appropriate handing procedures to be adopted by the aerodrome emergency services. 


Packing Instructions 
Information regarding packing instructions can be found in the Dangerous Goods List Column 10 (Passenger and Cargo Aircraft) and Column 12 (Cargo Aircraft only). The detail on each instruction (for Hydrogen peroxide, aqueous solution -Y541) can then be located in Tl Part 4. This detail tells us that Y (limited quantities) 541 allows a maximum quantity per inner package of 30 ml, with a maximum quantity per outer package of I litre. Acceptable packing devices will also be detailed. 
Dangerous Goods 
Incompatibility and Segregation 
Packages containing dangerous goods which might react dangerously one with another must not be stowed on an aircraft next to each other or in a position that would allow interaction between them in the event of leakage. As a minimum, Tl Part 7 shows the segregation required in a table. The separation scheme shown must be followed in order to maintain acceptable segregation between packages containing dangerous goods having different hazards. The scheme applies irrespective of whether the hazard is the primary or subsidiary risk. Some explosive classes/divisions must be loaded into different, non­adjacent loading positions and separated by other cargo with a minimum separation distance of 2 m.

the articles or substances are not forbidden for transport by air (see Tl Part 1)

¦

use an acceptance checklist 

(c) 
ensure that dangerous goods are accompanied by the required dangerous goods transport document(s), as completed by the person offering dangerous goods for air transport, except when the information applicable to the dangerous goods is provided in electronic form 

(d) 
ensure that where a dangerous goods transport document is provided in written form, a copy of the document is retained on the ground where it will be possible to obtain access to it within a reasonable period until the goods have reached their final destination 




(
e) ensure that a copy of the information to the pilot-in­command/commander is retained on the ground and that this copy, or the information contained in it, is readily accessible to the aerodromes of last departure and next scheduled arrival, until after the flight to which the information refers 

(f) 
retain the acceptance checklist, transport document and information to the pilot-in-command/commander for at least three months after completion of the flight 

(g) 
retain the training records of all personnel for at least three 


years. 

If the volume of information provided to the pilot-in­command/commander by the operator is such that it would be impracticable to transmit it in the event of an in-flight emergency, an additional summary of the information should also be provided, containing at least the quantities and class or division of the dangerous goods in each cargo compartment. 
DangerousGoods 

Acceptance of Dangerous Goods 
(a) The operator should not accept dangerous goods unless: 
(1) 
the package, overpack or freight container has been inspected in accordance with the acceptance procedures in the Technical Instructions 

(2) 
they are accompanied by two copies of a dangerous goods transport document or the information applicable to the consignment is provided in electronic form, except when otherwise specified in the Technical Instructions and 

(3) 
the English language is used for: (i)package marking and labelling and 


(ii)the dangerous goods transport document, in addition to any other language provision. 
(b) The operator or his/her handling agent should use an acceptance checklist which allows for: 
(1) 
all relevant details to be checked and 

(2) 
the recording of the results of the acceptance check by manual, mechanical or computerised means.

Dangerous Goods 
Acceptance Checklist 
Before a consignment consisting of a package or overpack containing dangerous goods is first accepted for carriage by air, the operator must, by use of a checklist, verify the following: 
a) 
the documentation or, when provided, the electronic data, complies with the detailed requirements specified in Tl 

b) 
the quantity of dangerous goods stated on the dangerous goods transport document is within the limits per package on a passenger or cargo aircraft as appropriate 

c) 
the marking of the package, overpack or freight container accords with the details stated on the accompanying dangerous goods transport document and are clearly visible 

d) 
where required, the letter in the packaging specification marking designating the packing group for which the design type has been successfully tested is appropriate for the dangerous goods contained within. This does not apply to overpacks where the specification marking is not visible 

e) 
proper shipping names, UN numbers, labels, and special handling instructions appearing on the interior package(s)are clearly visible or reproduced on the outside of an overpack 




f) 
the labelling of the package, overpack or freight container is as required by Tl 

g) 
the outer packaging of a combination packaging or the single packaging is permitted by the applicable packing instruction, and, when visible, is of the type stated on the accompanying dangerous goods transport document 

h) 
the package or overpack does not contain different dangerous goods which require segregation from each other according to Tl and 

i) 
the package, overpack, freight container or unit load device is not leaking and there is no indication that its integrity has 


been compromised. 
Dangerous Goods 
Damaged Packages of Dangerous Goods 
Where any package of dangerous goods loaded on an aircraft appears to be damaged or leaking, the operator must remove such package from the aircraft, or arrange for its removal by an 
appropriate (qualified) authority or organization, and thereafter 
arrange for its safe disposal. In the case of a package which appears to be leaking, the operator must ensure the remainder of the consignment is in a proper condition for transport by air and that no other package, baggage or cargo has been contaminated. 


Visibility of Markings and Labels 
During the course of air transport, including storage, markings and labels required by Tl must not be covered or obscured by any part of or attachment to the packaging or any other label or marking. 
Replacement of Labels 
When an operator discovers that labels for packages of dangerous goods have become lost, detached or illegible the operator must replace them with appropriate labels in accordance with the information provided on the dangerous goods transport document. 
Dangerous Goods 
Carriage of Radioactive Material 
Tis establish standards of safety which provide an acceptable level of control of the radiation (criticality) and thermal hazards to persons, property and the environment that are associated with the transport of radioactive material. The prime responsibility for safety must rest with the person or organization responsible for facilities and activities that give rise to radiation risk. 
The objective of Tis is to establish requirements to ensure safety and to protect persons, property and the environment from the effects of radiation in the transport of radioactive material. This protection is achieved by requiring: 
¦ 
containment of the radioactive contents 

¦ 
control of external radiation levels 

¦ 
prevention of criticality and 

¦ 
prevention of damage caused by heat. 




These requirements are satisfied by: 
¦ 
Applying a graded approach to the limits of the contents for packages and aircraft and to the performance standards. 

¦ 
Imposing conditions on the design and operation of packages and on the maintenance of the packagings, including consideration of the nature of the radioactive contents. 

¦ 
Requiring administrative controls including, where appropriate, approval by competent authorities. 


Dangerous Goods 
Limitation of Exposure of Persons to Radiation 
The radiation exposure of transport and storage personnel must be so controlled that none of them are likely to receive a radiation dose in excess of that permitted for members of the public. In special cases, arrangements may be made with the competent authority for radiological control to have such personnel classified as radiation workers and to comply with the necessary provisions. 
All relevant transport and storage personnel must receive such instructions as are necessary concerning the hazards involved and the precautions to be observed. 
The practice should be followed of keeping exposure to radiation as low as reasonably achievable. The separation distances shown in Tl are minimum values, and greater distances should be used where feasible. As far as possible, packages of radioactive materials stowed in underfloor cargo compartments of passenger aircraft should be placed on the compartment floor. 
Where exposure to radiation exceeds certain limits (detailed in Tl) the operator must have procedures in place to measure and record that exposure. These records must be retained.

Dangerous Goods 
Other Operator Responsibilities 
Dangerous goods shall only be transported by an operator approved in accordance with Annex V (Part-SPA), Subpart G, except when: 
¦ 
they are not subject to the technical instructions in accordance with Part 1 of those instructions or 

¦ 
they are carried by passengers or crew members, or are in 


baggage, in accordance with Part 8 of Tl. 
An operator shall establish procedures to ensure that all reasonable measures are taken to prevent dangerous goods from being carried on board inadvertently. 
The operator shall provide personnel with the necessary information enabling them to carry out their responsibilities, as required by the technical instructions. 


The operator shall, in accordance with the Tl, report without delay to the competent authority and the appropriate authority of the State of occurrence in the event of: 
(1) 
any dangerous goods accidents or incidents 

(2) 
the discovery of undeclared or mis-declared dangerous goods in cargo or mail or 

(3) 
the finding of dangerous goods carried by passengers or crew members, or in their baggage, when not in accordance with Part 8 of Tl. 


The operator shall ensure that passengers are provided with information about dangerous goods in accordance with the technical instructions. 
The operator shall ensure that notices giving information about the transport of dangerous goods are provided at acceptance points for cargo as required by the technical instructions. 
Dangerous Goods 
(a) 
Any type of dangerous goods accident or incident, or the finding of undeclared or mis-declared dangerous goods should be reported, irrespective of whether the dangerous goods are contained in cargo, mail, passengers' baggage or crew baggage. For the purposes of the reporting of undeclared and mis-declared dangerous goods found in cargo, the Tl considers this to include items of operators' stores that are classified as dangerous goods. 

(b) 
The first report should be dispatched within 72 hours of the event. It may be sent by any means, including e-mail, telephone or fax. This report should include the details that are known at that time, under the headings identified in (c) below. If necessary, a subsequent report should be made as soon as possible giving all the details that were not known at the time the first report was sent. If a report has been made verbally, written confirmation should be sent as soon as possible. 

(c) 
The first and any subsequent report should be as precise as possible and should contain the following data, where relevant: 

(1) 
date of the incident or accident or the finding of undeclared or mis-declared dangerous goods 

(2) 
location, the flight number and flight date 





(3) 
description of the goods and the reference number of the air waybill, pouch, baggage tag, ticket, etc. 

(4) 
proper shipping name (including the technical name, if appropriate)and UN/ID number, when known 

(5) 
class or division and any subsidiary risk 

(6) 
type of packaging, and the packaging specification 

marking on it 

(7) 
quantity 

(8) 
name and address of the shipper, passenger, etc. 

(9) 
any other relevant details 

(10) 
suspected cause of the incident or accident 

(
11) action taken 

(12) 
any other reporting action taken and 

(13) 
name, title, address and telephone number of the person making the report. 



(d) Copies of relevant documents and any photographs taken should be attached to the report. 
Dangerous Goods 

Dangerous Goods Accident and Incident Reporting continued 

( e)  A dangerous goods accident or incident may also constitute an aircraft accident, serious incident or incident. Reports should be made for both types of occurrences when the criteria for each are met.  The first dangerous goods incident report should be dispatched within how many hours of the event?  
(f)  The dangerous goods reporting form provided in EASA OPS should be used, but other forms, including electronic transfer of data, may be used provided that at least the minimum information is supplied:  SUaBMaIT ANSWER  

Dangerous Goods 
Provisions Concerning Passengers and Crew 
Dangerous Goods Carried by Passengers or Crew 
Except as otherwise provided in Tl Part 8, dangerous goods, including excepted packages of radioactive material, must not be carried by passengers or crew members, either as or in carry-on baggage or checked baggage or on their person. 
¦ 
Security type equipment such as attache cases, cash boxes, cash bags, etc. incorporating dangerous goods, for example lithium batteries or pyrotechnic material, are totally forbidden. 

¦ 
Personal medical oxygen devices that utilize liquid oxygen are forbidden either as or in carry-on baggage or checked baggage or on the person. 

¦ 
Electroshock weapons (e.g. tasers) containing dangerous goods such as explosives, compressed gases, lithium batteries, etc., are forbidden in carry-on baggage or checked baggage or on the person. 


Notwithstanding any additional restrictions which may be implemented by States in the interests of aviation security, the normal regulations regarding dangerous goods do not apply to the items listed in Tl Part 8 when carried by passengers or crew members or in baggage that has been separated from its owner during transit (e.g. lost baggage or improperly routed baggage) or in excess baggage carried as cargo. 


The table in Tl Part 8 is several pages long, but a summary of some relevant points is as follows: 
Articles Used in Dressing or Grooming 
¦ 
Toiletry articles (including aerosols). The total net quantity of each single article must not exceed 0.5 kg or 0.5 L. 

The term "toiletry articles (including aerosols)" is intended to include such items as hair sprays, perfumes and colognes. 


¦ 
Hair curlers containing hydrocarbon gas, no more than one per person, provided that the safety cover is securely fitted over the heating element. Gas refills for such curlers must not be carried 


Dangerous Goods 
Consumer Articles 
¦ 
Alcoholic beverages (when in retail packaging), containing more than 24 per cent but not more than 70 per cent alcohol by volume, in receptacles not exceeding 5 L, with a total net quantity per person of 5 L for such beverages. 

¦ 
One small packet of safety matches or a cigarette lighter that does not contain unabsorbed liquid fuel (other than liquefied gas), intended for use by an individual when carried on the person. Matches and lighters are not permitted in checked or carry-on baggage. Lighter fuel and lighter refills are not permitted on one's person, in carry-on or checked baggage Note.-"Strike anywhere" matches are forbidden for air transport. 

¦ 
Portable electronic devices (watches, calculating machines, cameras, cellular phones, laptop computers, camcorders, etc.) containing lithium or lithium ion cells or batteries when carried by passengers or crew for personal use, which should be carried as carry-on baggage. Spare batteries must be individually protected. 

¦ 
Fuel cells used to power portable electronic devices (for example cameras, cellular phones, laptop computers and camcorders)and spare fuel cell cartridges, (there are conditions). 

¦ 
Dry ice, with the approval of the operator, up to 2.5 kg per person in either checked or carry-on baggage. 




¦ 
A mercurial barometer or mercurial thermometera-in carry­on baggage only with the approval of the operator. 

¦ 
Spare lithium ion batteries -carry-on baggage or on the person. 

¦ 
Avalanche rescue backpack -in either checked or carry-on baggage, provided it is made safe, and with the approval of the operator. 


Any organization or enterprise other than an operator (such as a travel agent), involved in the air transport of passengers, should provide passengers with information about the types of dangerous goods which they are forbidden to transport aboard an aircraft. This information should consist of, as a minimum, notices at those locations where there is an interface with the passengers. 
Where provision is made for the purchase of tickets via the Internet, information on the types of dangerous goods which a passenger is forbidden to transport aboard an aircraft should be provided in either text or pictorial form and should be such that ticket purchase cannot be completed until the passenger, or a person acting on their behalf, has indicated that they have understood the restrictions on dangerous goods in baggage.
